# The Essential Writings of Sabina Spielrein: Pioneer of Psychoanalysis

**Author**: Sabina Spielrein; Ruth I. Cape (editor); Raymond Burt (translator)

The Essential Writings of Sabina Spielrein Sabina Spielrein’s writings explore the burning topics in the early days of psychoanalysis while providing insight into the culture of the time and her own personal struggles.

After a comprehensive historical and biographical introduction to Spielrein by John Launer, The Essential Writings of Sabina Spielrein: Pioneer of Psychoanalysis presents full-length English translations of her first three and most essential writings, offering deep insight into her brilliant and pioneering mind: •• The first unabridged English rendition of her medical dissertation of 1911, entitled “On the Psychological Content of a Case of Schizophrenia (Dementia Praecox),” with an afterword by Adrienne Harris •• A new, improved English translation of Spielrein’s seminal essay of 1912, “Destruction as the Cause of Becoming” •• A faithful English rendition of her 1913 essay “Contributions to Understanding a Child’s Mind” The Essential Writings of Sabina Spielrein: Pioneer of Psychoanalysis presents a rich source of materials and inspiration to psychoanalysts, psychoanalytic psychotherapists and analytical psychologists, as well as scholars in the humanities and the behavioral sciences.

Ruth I. Cape is Associate Professor of German at Austin College in Sherman, Texas.

After doctoral studies in history, she received a doctoral degree in German from the University of California, Los Angeles.

She has published several bilingual and annotated editions of historical documents.

Raymond Burt is Professor of German at the University of North Carolina Wilmington.

He completed his doctoral work in German at the University of California, Los Angeles.

His research and publications focus on fin-de-Siècle Vienna, in particular the writings of Friedrich S.

Krauss, a controversial folklorist in the Freudian circle.

Acknowledgments Translating Spielrein’s medical dissertation into readable English was a daunting task due to the denseness and complexity of the text.

Thanks go to Ann Belford-Ulanov from Union Theological Seminary in NYC for her enthusiastic encouragement and support; to the helpful staff of the Kristine Mann Library in Manhattan, particularly to Robin S.

Brown, for granting access to important documents relating to Sabina Spielrein, and for his willingness to explore this translation project in inspiring conversations.

Thanks also go to Austin College for readily funding a weeklong research stay at the Kristine Mann Library and providing a summer travel grant to work extensively on editions of not only the dissertation, but also Spielrein’s 1913 essay on child psychology, and a new English rendition of her famous 1912 “Destruction” essay.

Last but not least, deep thanks go to family members, friends, and colleagues for their support, encouragement, and patience.

Preface This volume includes the unabridged translations of Sabina Spielrein’s first three scholarly writings: the first full-length English rendition of her medical dissertation of 1911, entitled “On the Psychological Content of a Case of Schizophrenia (Dementia Praecox)” [“Über den psychologischen Inhalt eines Falles von Schizophrenie (Dementia praecox)”],1 as well as a new translation of her seminal essay “Destruction as the Cause of Becoming” [“Die Destruktion als Ursache des Werdens”] (1912);2 furthermore, a faithful English rendition of her 1913 essay “Contributions to Understanding a Child’s Mind” [“Beiträge zur Kenntnis der kindlichen Seele”]. 3  These three works comprise about half of all her writings, whereas her subsequent publications were, for the most part, only several pages long.

Sabina Spielrein (1885–1942) was a Russian physician and the first psychiatric patient to become a psychoanalyst herself.

She had a profound impact on those around her throughout her career, yet she remained unacknowledged during her lifetime and was virtually forgotten after her death. 4  In the written history of psychoanalysis, her name could not be found until in more recent decades,5 when she became the subject of scholarly books and articles as well as various plays and films that focus on aspects of her writings and her life.

To date, three plays about Sabina Spielrein have been produced in English.

Sabina by Willy Holtzman appeared off-Broadway in New York in 1996.

There are also two British plays that were produced in London: In 1998, Snoo Wilson’s Sabina, and in 2003, Christopher Hampton’s The Talking Cure.

Equally, to date, three films have been released in which Spielrein figures prominently: In 2002, the Italian filmmaker Roberto Faenza produced Prendimi l’anima [The Soul Keeper] based on a script by Aldo Carotenuto.

In the same

Preface  xi year, the Swedish filmmaker Elisabeth Márton directed the documentary My Name Was Sabina Spielrein, which was released in the United States in 2005.

It was the first film about Sabina Spielrein’s life that creatively adapted excerpts from hospital records and materials Jung published on her case, as well as parts of letters and her diary addressing the triangular relationship between Spielrein, Freud, and Jung.

More recently, Christopher Hampton adapted his own play The Talking Cure for the feature film A Dangerous Method, directed by David Cronenberg and released in 2011.

This movie centers on the relationship between Carl Jung, Sigmund Freud, and Sabina Spielrein, and its impact on the “birth of psychoanalysis.” Finally, in 2012, Sabina, a libretto for chamber opera, was produced by David Pountney. 6  Several novels have been written about Spielrein as well, including Wolfgang Martynkewicz’s Sabina Spielrein und Carl Gustav Jung: Eine Fallgeschichte [Sabina Spielrein and Carl Gustav Jung: a case history] (Berlin: Rowohlt, 1999), and Karsten Alnaes’ novel Sabina which appeared in Norway in 1994.

His book was published in German in 2007, with the title Als sie mit Jung tanzte [When she danced with Jung].

Parallel to these artistic treatments, the renewed interest in Sabina Spielrein’s life and work sparked a new wave of scholarly books and articles.

Among them is Sabine Richebächer’s full-length biography of Spielrein in German (2005). 7  Most recently, John Launer in his remarkable book Sex Versus Survival: The Life and Ideas of Sabina Spielrein (2015), the first full-length biography of Spielrein in English, counts Spielrein “among the most original thinkers of the twentieth century,” and “a bridge-builder and a peacemaker: between people, schools of thought, professional disciplines and ways of looking at the world.”8 Thus he considers Spielrein a major figure in the intellectual history of the twentieth century.

He also carefully reassesses her relationship with both Jung and Freud and the relationship between the two men.

This volume aims at giving Spielrein’s own voice center stage.

Spielrein’s collected works Sabina Spielrein: Sämtliche Schriften were first published in German in 1987, without a listed editor. 9  Italian translations of her works were published in 1986 in the volume Sabina Spielrein: Comprensione della schizofrenia e altri scritti. 10  Unfortunately, the English-reading world has limited access to Spielrein’s publications.

Only some of her writings, most of which were written in German, a few

xii  Preface in French and Russian, have been translated into English, among them the above-mentioned “Destruction” essay of 1912. 11  It is surprising that Spielrein’s 1911 dissertation for her medical doctorate at the University of Zurich – a landmark achievement initiating her successful career as a physician and psychoanalyst – has previously never been published in English in full length, this in spite of the fact that there are numerous references to this publication in her famous “Destruction” essay.

Although portions of English translations of her medical dissertation have been widely quoted in various articles and books, they unfortunately contain significant inaccuracies and often lack a broader context.

Brian R.

Skea, in his 2006 article “Sabina Spielrein: Out From the Shadow of Jung and Freud,”12 points out that, in actual fact, McCormick had privately published his translation of Spielrein’s dissertation in 1992, before his English translation of the “Destruction” essay.

McCormick’s “Destruction as the Cause of Coming into Being” with his introduction and postscript appeared in the 1994 Journal of Analytical Psychology.

Skea’s 2006 article attempts to bring Spielrein’s dissertation “out of the shadow created by the brilliance of two great psychoanalytic pioneers, Jung and Freud,”13 and uses portions of McCormick’s translation of Spielrein’s dissertation.

However, the article does not present the work in its entirety and therefore does not do justice to its complexity. 14  Spielrein’s remarkable 1913 essay “Contributions to Understanding a Child’s Mind” is one of the earliest publications on child psychology.

Spielrein was one of the first psychoanalysts to publish on child psychology and the development of language in infancy.

However, her accomplishments in child analysis have mostly been ignored.

Still today, Freud’s daughter Anna Freud is considered the founder of child analysis, perhaps together with Melanie Klein who is credited with founding the field of object relations theory.

Yet, it was Sabina Spielrein and also Hermine Hug-Hellmuth (1871–1924), who, in 1912, published the first contributions on child psychology in psychoanalytic history. 15  Anna Freud’s first paper appeared at a time when Spielrein had already published ten papers on child-analytic topics. 16  This book presents English translations of Spielrein’s key works.

In her medical dissertation, Spielrein examined the psychic delusions of Frau M., a patient who entered the Burghölzi Clinic on November 18, 1905, a few months after Sabina Spielrein had been released from inpatient status.

Spielrein’s dissertation was a monumental task that she carried out in a

Preface  xiii most meticulous manner.

At the same time, it is in itself a carefully crafted piece of art that reflects a complex and sophisticated mind, as Spielrein’s broad and thorough education allows her to draw on a great variety of sources, including philosophy, mythology, and literature.

Spielrein’s 1912 “Destruction” essay demonstrates that, “in accord with the biological evidence, the reproductive drive consists, also on a psychological level, of two antagonistic components and therefore is simultaneously a creative and a destructive drive.” Spielrein regularly quotes or references German authors in her works, including Johann Wolfgang von Goethe.

It is likely that she was familiar with Goethe’s famous poem, entitled Selige Sehnsucht [The soul’s yearning] (1814).

This poem with its explicit sexual innuendo appears to represent a core idea in her “Destruction” paper.

The last stanza reads as follows: “And as long as you don’t have / This: Die and become! / You remain a gloomy guest / On the dark earth.” [Und so lang du das nicht hast, / Dieses: Stirb und werde! / Bist du nur ein trüber Gast /Auf der dunklen Erde].

It is noteworthy that the German word werden means “to become,” but also has the broader meaning of “to grow,” “to develop.” The German essay entitled in its original “Die Destruktion als Ursache des Werdens” has been translated as “Destruction as the Cause of Becoming,” in order to reflect this broad connotation of the German verb werden and its implied metaphorical meaning, especially in contrast to the equally metaphorical dimension of the German verb sterben (to die).

In her 1913 article “Contributions to Understanding a Child’s Mind,” Spielrein comments on three cases relating to two boys and a girl and also adds an appendix about clock symbolism.

She explains that the analysis of the girl is a self-observation and therefore rather comprehensive, whereas the case of the first boy is only a partial analysis, yet clearly shows the connection between fear and sexual ideas.

She points out that analyzing the second boy was difficult due to his unwillingness to volunteer information.

Nevertheless, all three children have in common that they are preoccupied with sexual questions.

Spielrein’s three key works present a rich source of materials and inspiration not only to psychoanalysts, but also to scholars or those interested in the humanities and sciences in general.

In this way, Spielrein’s writings are not only an avant-gardistic scholarly discussion, but also a mirror of her own psyche and struggles, while offering a cultural mirror of the time.

xiv  Preface Principles of translation Although Spielrein’s mother tongue was Russian, she wrote her dissertation in German.

At times, her choice of words is somewhat unusual for the modern German reader.

This could also be explained by the historical distance of the text.

The translations of Spielrein’s 1911 dissertation, her “Destruction” essay of 1912 and her 1913 paper on child psychology, are close translations of the German originals that try to be accurate and exact in readable English while preserving some of the flavor of Spielrein’s language.

Her footnotes have been rendered word for word with additional information deemed helpful by the translators presented in square brackets.

Spielrein’s dissertation, in particular, poses a major challenge to any translator due to the linguistic denseness of the text.

This might explain why it has never been published in full length before.

The dissertation contains numerous word-by-word comments of the patient as they were communicated to Spielrein.

At times, the thoughts in those sections appear disjointed.

In order to stay true to the text, the English version reflects this as well.

The same applies to Spielrein’s observations that are occasionally in the style of a medical report.

For the purpose of clarity, the translators sometimes had to break up sentences and change punctuation since Spielrein frequently uses convoluted sentences.

Equally, some minor additions, changes or explanations by the translators were added in square brackets in order to facilitate understanding.

The divisions into sections and paragraphs in these translations are, for the most part, those of the original writings.

In order to facilitate the reading of the text, at times longer quotations are presented in a free-standing block, indented, and in separate paragraphs.

The translations of Spielrein’s numerous quotes from literary sources are those of the translators.

Introduction John Launer Sabina Spielrein (1885–1942) was one of the most remarkable intellectual figures of the twentieth century.

She was also among the least recognized in relation to her achievements.

It is a fate she shared with Rosalind Franklin, Lise Meitner, and countless other women pioneers whose names disappeared into obscurity, while the men with whom they worked acquired fame for accomplishments that would have been impossible without them.

In Spielrein’s case, the causes for marginalization during her lifetime, and subsequent erasure from history, were multiple: Not only was she female.

She was Russian during a period when many in the west regarded the country and its inhabitants as backward.

She was Jewish at a time when anti-Semitism was rife across Europe.

She suffered physical and emotional abuse as a child and medical student, leading her to be seen by others at the time and subsequently as damaged.

She was eclectic in her interests in a field of study that was compartmentalized, and she tried to be a peacemaker and bridge-builder while others attained success through provoking schisms.

In her fifties, she was murdered in the Holocaust.

For decades afterwards, both her fate and her contributions to psychiatry, psychoanalysis, and child psychology remained almost completely unknown.

This volume, comprising her first three published papers, is a significant step on the long, slow journey to restoring her memory and celebrating her life’s work.

Spielrein was the eldest child of a wealthy mercantile family in Rostovon-Don, near the Black Sea.

She was a brilliant child, who spoke fluent German and French by her teens, as well as her native Russian, and was gifted in music.

She also had a passion for science and, early on, an ambition to train as a doctor.

Troubled family relationships, and in particular the death of her sister Emilia, precipitated an emotional breakdown when

2  Introduction she was eighteen.

She was admitted to the university mental hospital in Zurich, Switzerland (known as the Burghölzli), where she made a rapid recovery, probably due in large part to separation from her family and to the humane regime at the hospital, led by its director Eugen Bleuler.

This regime included scientific study and participation in community life.

She fell in love almost immediately with the chief physician, Carl Jung, and underwent some sessions of word association and brief psychotherapy from him.

She assisted Jung in the hospital’s psychological laboratory, and they began a friendship that continued when she entered medical school in Zurich the following year. 1  For much of her time at the Burghölzli, Spielrein was already doing the work of a clinical assistant, diagnosing patients with more serious and enduring conditions than she had suffered, and working alongside some of the distinguished doctors from abroad who had come to study there.

These included Karl Abraham and Max Eitingon, who later became two of Freud’s closest friends and colleagues.

After moving to Zurich University to study medicine, she continued to attend Jung’s ward rounds.

When she was in her fourth year as a medical student, she and Jung had an erotic relationship for several months, which has become the subject of much mythologizing and contentious debate.

This has distracted from other aspects of what she achieved.

More importantly, from the point of view of intellectual history, she discussed Jung’s own ideas with him as they evolved, helping him to develop them during these years, and hearing about his growing collaboration with Sigmund Freud in Vienna.

Bleuler was her supervisor for the dissertation for her medical degree, although many of the ideas in it were drawn from Jung’s thinking at the time.

Shortly after she qualified as a doctor in 1911, the dissertation was published in the Jahrbuch [Yearbook], the principal psychoanalytic journal of the time, as “On the Psychological Content of a Case of Schizophrenia”2 alongside important papers by almost every individual from the pantheon of major figures in early psychoanalysis, including Bleuler, Ludwig Binswanger, Alphonse Mäder, Sándor Ferenczi, Oskar Pfister, and Otto Rank, as well as Jung and Freud themselves.

Spielrein’s relationship with Jung was important in her own development, but in the long term, it was not nearly as formative as the one she soon made with Sigmund Freud.

She had first contacted Freud by letter while still at medical school, as part of an attempt to bring a satisfactory

Introduction  3 end to her emotional connection with Jung, which had become confused and messy.

So, incidentally, was Freud’s response to it: He covered up for Jung, while privately adding it to his growing concerns about his colleague.

The day she finished medical school, she left Zurich and Jung behind her and, after a short break in Munich, went to live in Vienna in order to become part of Freud’s circle.

Henceforth, she clearly identified herself as a Freudian psychoanalyst, although she never actually had an analysis with Jung, Freud or anyone else.

(In common with many of the analytic pioneers, she began in practice before a training analysis was required.) Spielrein joined the Vienna Psychoanalytic Society, and on only her second visit there, she presented her paper on “Destruction as the Cause of Becoming” that also appears in this volume.

The presentation was a phenomenal achievement for a twenty-six-year-old woman who had only qualified as a doctor the previous year, had been a psychiatric inpatient six years before writing it, and was now the sole female member of the Society, after the resignation of the previous one, Margarete Hilferding.

The published version of the “Destruction” paper in the Jahrbuch3 was followed almost immediately by a further paper in the Zentralblatt – the newsletter for psychoanalysis and psychotherapy.

It was her “Contributions to Understanding a Child’s Mind,” which is the final translation in this collection. 4  Although these three papers were her first to be published, Spielrein had always been a prolific writer.

She had kept a personal journal in Russian from the age of ten, sometimes written in code to prevent her father from deciphering it. 5  In the ensuing years, she wrote her journals in German. 6  They included intimate accounts of the vicissitudes of her relationship with Jung, interspersed at times with notes of her own reading.

These were prodigious and covered not only Russian and German literature, but also philosophy, psychology, biology, physics, and much else.

She also kept drafts or copies of her voluminous correspondence.

In some cases, her letters to Jung and Freud comprise twenty pages or more.

It was the discovery of these diaries and letters nearly forty years after her death that led scholars to realize what a central role she must have played in the lives of Jung and Freud during the years of their growing alliance and then their dramatic falling-out.

Of equal interest, her journals and correspondence, which continued for at least a further decade until she returned to Russia,

4  Introduction are a precious record of the private thinking that informed her publications.

They were the laboratory where she experimented with the memories, ideas, and theories that appear in the translations in this book, and in her later works.

The three essays in this volume were in fact the prelude to her overall life’s work: a total of around thirty-four publications.

The rest of Spielrein’s career can be summarized briefly.

After marriage to a fellow Russian physician called Pavel Sheftel, she moved to Berlin.

Then, at the outbreak of the First World War, she settled back in Switzerland, in Lausanne, where she stayed alone with her small daughter Renata.

These were not prolific years for her in terms of writing.

Nevertheless, she published ten short papers and reports in different psychoanalytic journals between 1913 and 1918. 7  Among other subjects, they covered dreams, mother love, mothers-in-law, and the first case report of brief psychotherapy with a child.

In the years following the war, she had a tremendous burst of creativity – the second in her career.

This coincided with her work at the Institut Jean-Jacques Rousseau in Geneva, the leading psychological institution in Switzerland and one of the pioneering centers in the world for child psychology.

It was there that she worked with its director Edouard Claparède and his disciple Jean Piaget and even became Piaget’s analyst for a while.

Among the prodigious numbers of papers that she wrote while there, one stands out as being of equal stature to, if not greater than, the ones in this book: “The origin of the child’s words ‘Papa’ and ‘Mama.’”8 It was a tour de force combining linguistics, child psychology, and psychoanalysis that – like so much else that Spielrein wrote during her life – anticipated or prefigured whole areas of twentiethcentury thought, including attachment theory, developmental psychology, and evolutionary psychology.

There was one further highly productive period in Spielrein’s career, although it mainly involved her personal influence rather than her writing.

After her return to Russia in 1923, she worked at the new State Psychoanalytic Institute in Moscow which housed the “OrphanageLaboratory” or Children’s House, founded by Vera Schmidt to combine the latest methods of early years education with research into child development. 9  During that time she was the most informed and well-connected psychoanalyst in Russia.

Her junior colleagues included Lev Vygotsky and Alexandra Luria, who later became leading figures in the development of twentieth-century psychology.

She and Renata finally returned to her home town of Rostov-on-Don, where she rejoined her husband

Introduction  5 and had a second daughter, Eva.

Until the late 1930s, she continued to work as a clinician across the boundaries of psychiatry, developmental psychology, psychoanalysis, and pediatrics.

Spielrein was probably the last person to defend Freud publicly in the Soviet Union.

In 1938, she was widowed, and her three brothers were murdered during Stalin’s Great Terror.

She and her two daughters were shot in the Nazi massacre of 13,000 Jews in the Zmeyevskaya Ravine outside Rostov-on-Don on August 11, 1942. 10  ***** The three essays in this volume are exceptionally multilayered and in some sections highly intricate texts.

They are the words of a woman in early adulthood who is writing on the one hand about scientific psychology, but also – in the manner of all psychoanalytic writings – grappling with her own inner demons and trying to make sense of her personal destiny as well as the wider human condition.

They are generic surveys of her subjects and, at the same time, metaphorical autobiographies.

Spielrein is writing for a readership who may know that she was a psychiatric patient and a survivor of family abuse, was hospitalized for a while with hysteria and later had an affair with her former psychiatrist.

She is no doubt aware that she might be judged precisely through that lens – as indeed she often was, not only by Jung and Freud but by many of their successors.

She is therefore writing about madness and fantasy, but also making a claim for her own sanity, in the knowledge that she may not be entirely believed.

The complexities of reading the texts do not stop there.

Spielrein is writing as an ambitious young woman who feels literally fated to join in the daring and innovative project of psychoanalysis, if not to be one of its leaders.

In pursuit of this, she starts by aiming as high as anyone in that situation possibly could: to become the first person ever to submit a psychoanalytic paper for a medical degree, the first to have her doctoral dissertation published in a psychoanalytic journal – both of which ambitions she achieves.

Yet behind this extraordinary act of confidence, and the ones that followed with her two subsequent papers, there are the personal torments she suffered at the hands of the two leading proponents of psychoanalysis: first as one of Jung’s serial mistresses, and then as a supplicant to Freud, who “led her up the garden path” to save Jung’s career and reputation. 11 

6  Introduction In spite of these humiliations, she cannot let go – neither does she want to – of her continuing infatuation with Jung.

In a further ironic twist, she also has a growing conviction that Freud is the greater thinker and the leader, and that he and Jung are unlikely to remain allies for long.

Among the currents and eddies of her first three papers, it is not hard to hear her struggles to defend the theoretical edifices of each man, to try forlornly to reconcile them with each other and, at the same time, to find her own distinctive voice that is able to speak about the deficiencies of both men, and to set out her own unique ideas.

Grappling with the polyphony of her writing, always challenging and at times impenetrable, many readers may feel that it is this voice of hers that is most authentic, most personal, and most eloquent.

The psychoanalyst Adrienne Harris has pointed out how the stages of Spielrein’s career, from working with Freud and Jung, to Claparède and Piaget, and then Vygotsky and Luria, seem to represent a series of “very hot spots, key locations at a time of intellectual and cultural synergy in the period from roughly 1911 to 1928, an important period in the history of psychoanalysis and a seedbed for the study of child development.”12 We are left wondering about the conversations between her and others that led to so many of the seminal ideas of the twentieth century – about madness, symbolism, sexuality, creativity and destruction, development, language and speech, and much else.

It is in this richly intertextual context that many readers will approach the three works included in the book, hearing not only the powerfully present voice of Spielrein herself, but also echoes of her past and current conversations, and the pre-echoes of future ones, reverberate through her words.

Although the three papers in this book were published in the space of only three years, they also document a pivotal transition in the history of psychiatry, psychoanalysis, and analytical psychology.

They span exactly the period between the apogee of the collaboration between Jung and Freud and their final, catastrophic rift.

There are distinct indications of this within the writings, as well as references to corresponding developments in Spielrein’s own life. “On the Psychological Content of a Case of Schizophrenia” is, first and foremost, a contribution to scientific psychiatry, in the tradition of Bleuler and his predecessor Auguste Forel.

It is a work of precise ethnographic and linguistic observation.

Spielrein even scrupulously avoids looking at

Introduction  7 the patient’s case notes in advance in order not to bias her observations.

Her stance no doubt arises out of the role modelling and clinical training she experienced at the Burghölzli, and anticipates the later work of observing children’s speech that Spielrein was to pioneer.

It also stands in the humane tradition of taking the utterances of people with psychosis seriously and seeking meaning within them – a tradition that later culminated in the work of psychiatrists like R.

D.

Laing and Thomas Szasz.

At the same time, it is a project explicitly aimed at supporting Jung’s view that undifferentiated phylogenetic patterns – what he was soon to call archetypes – could be detected in the manifestations of psychic disturbance.

It was a view to which neither Bleuler nor Freud was sympathetic, yet it is clear that it was important for Spielrein to adhere to it at that stage of her own development.

Later, she either abandoned the view, or lost interest in it altogether.

Reading the dissertation is in some ways like encountering a hall of mirrors.

Its timing includes the period when Spielrein was working through her own erotic experiences with Jung, reaching the realization that he was a “Don Juan”13 and resolving to leave him in spite of her infatuation.

What is unsaid in the paper therefore shouts out even more loudly than what is said.

There are suggestions that the breakdown of the young seamstress she has taken as her subject has occurred during some kind of marital crisis, possibly involving infidelities on the part of her husband.

Spielrein does not speculate on the facts of the case, but it is hard for a modern reader not to suspect that much of what the patient says is an attempt both to speak about what is going on and, at the same time, not to speak about it.

We are uncomfortably aware that the absence of precise inquiry into such biographical realities, and indeed their systematic silencing, is part of the problematical narrative of psychoanalysis itself, and that Spielrein – the subject of childhood physical and probably sexual abuse, and now emerging from an entanglement with her former psychiatrist – was herself one of its most significant victims.

The question arises: To what extent might she be participating in, and re-enacting, a flight from truth?

It is also hard for a modern reader not to speculate how Spielrein’s writing is influenced by her wish to please and impress Jung, and possibly still to achieve her overriding ambition at times: to prize him away from his wife.

Similarly, while Spielrein writes in the voice of

8  Introduction an “objective observer,” the reader is aware of a matrix of intersections between her own experience and that of the patient.

When the patient talks of religious differences between herself and her husband, we may think of those between Spielrein and Jung, richly chronicled and analyzed in her diaries.

Likewise, when the patient describes sexual and sadistic images concerning Jung, it is impossible not to connect these with Spielrein’s own whipping fantasies.

We are inevitably led to wonder how far Spielrein’s “observations” are colored by her own interests and preoccupations, and what her private thoughts may have been about that “material” she purports to be reporting.

Beyond these considerations, there is a further question lurking in the background.

How far might the patient have noticed flirtatious behavior on the part of Jung towards herself, other women, and Spielrein in particular?

How much might she have intuitively sensed that Spielrein herself was infatuated with Jung?

Is the seemingly clinical, even forensic, nature of this psychiatric “study” a cryptic, encoded cover-up by both parties, both “patients,” for the cauldron of confused desires, fantasies, and lusts that seethe beneath the seemingly calm and therapeutic ethos of the Burghölzli, and envelop at least one of the psychiatrists there as well?

If Spielrein’s dissertation is a work of great complexity, both in its content and context, “Destruction as the Cause of Becoming” is considerably more so.

It is rightly regarded as her most significant paper, but also seen as deeply problematical. 14  Spielrein herself regarded it as her “Siegfried”: a surrogate heroic child that would stand in for the one she fantasized about conceiving with Jung.

Rather than physically creating such a child, she replaces it with a work of intellectual creation.

It is both an analysis and an enactment of what she described as her “higher calling” – a recurrent motif in her thinking that probably gave Jung his concept of individuation. 15  Spielrein opens her paper with its central question: “Why does this most powerful drive, the reproductive drive, harbor, besides the anticipated positive feelings, negative feelings, such as anxiety and disgust?” After citing Jung about the danger of erotic activity, she gives a scarcely veiled account of her own disappointment in her sexual encounters with the man himself – described as if post-coital, but in reality almost certainly unconsummated. 16  Spielrein then proceeds to offer her own categorical answer to the question, one that is framed not in terms of psychology but of fundamental sexual biology:

Introduction  9 The male part merges with the female part, and the latter becomes agitated and assumes a new form due to the foreign invader.

This transformation affects the whole organism.

Destruction and reconstruction that always occur in normal circumstances as well, take place in an abrupt manner, and the organism rids itself of the sexual product as it does of any other excretion.

It would be unlikely if the individual did not surmise these destructive and regenerating processes, at least through corresponding feelings.

(p. 99) This assertion was the core of her theory: At some level, the act of procreation involves self-destruction – and this truth is represented not only in biology but in the unconscious mind and in mythology.

In what follows, Spielrein quickly appears to set biology aside to offer a tour de force of psychology, philosophy, literature, symbolism, scripture, and anthropology, in order to support her hypothesis.

It is an overwhelming – and at times frankly incomprehensible – intellectual expedition.

At different points it leaves the reader astounded at the breadth of her knowledge, or frustrated at her precocious efforts to construct a psychological “theory of everything,” or both at the same time.

Everywhere, there are hints of ideas that she, Jung or Freud will later develop, or that will appear one day in the work of later psychoanalytic theorists: In this respect, Harris mentions Viktor Tausk, Otto Gross, Sándor Ferenczi, Jeanne Lampl-de Groot, Josine Müller, Jean Laplanche, Wilfred Bion, Judith Butler, Ruth Stein, Peter Fonagy, and Mary Target, among others17 – and is it not hard to add further names to this list.

Often, these hints tantalize and then evaporate.

At times, reading the paper feels like plunging through the undergrowth covering the remains of a massive temple site, and one that might also serve as the foundations of a future city.

Meanwhile, it is neither wholly revealed, nor has the rebuilding properly begun.

Time and again, Spielrein touches base with biology.

She does so when she uses her own personal experience of masochism to assert: I strongly believe that the ego-psyche, also the unconscious one, is governed by impulses that lie even deeper and are unconcerned with our emotional reactions to their demands . . .

The wish for self-harm and joy in pain are, however, entirely incomprehensible if we only consider the life of the ego which only desires pleasure.

(p. 103)

10  Introduction She revisits biology at the end of her “Psychological Observations” and does so once more at the conclusion of the paper itself: I believe that my examples sufficiently demonstrate that, in accord with the biological evidence, the reproductive drive consists, also on a psychological level, of two antagonistic components, and therefore is simultaneously a drive of becoming and a destructive drive.

(p. 132) Her version of the “death drive” here, it should be noted, is not the same as the one Freud later proposed.

Whereas Freud’s was a metapsychological notion – a form of entropy operating in all human beings that stood in opposition to the positive instinct for pleasure – Spielrein was speaking of a biological principle: Old life must always be destroyed in order to create new.

Freud was indeed correct both in acknowledging her prior formulation of such an idea, but also in confessing he did not fully understand her notion of it. 18  I have argued elsewhere that Spielrein’s viewpoint in the “Destruction” paper is first and foremost biological. 19  Although she is staking a claim for greatness in the psychoanalytic world, her underlying epistemology belongs more with the evolutionary thinking of Charles Darwin and August Weissman (whom she studied at medical school), and of Forel and Bleuler, than it does with Jung and Freud.

This is what I believe later helped her formulate an incipient idea of attachment theory,20 and informed her affiliation with Claparède and Piaget, and then Luria and Vygotsky.

When she delivered her lecture, Spielrein may well have known that Bleuler had resigned from the Vienna Psychoanalytic Society only the previous day, on the grounds that it had distanced itself too far from science.

She regarded Bleuler as her “dear, good little father”21 and she may have been speaking on his behalf as much as Jung’s or anyone else’s.

Spielrein herself clearly had no doubt what underpinned her ideas.

In the somewhat confused discussion that followed her talk, she apologized that she had not made the biological fundamentals of her theory clearer. 22  It is most unlikely that it would have made any difference to her hearers if she had, and might even have made things worse.

If modern psychoanalytic commentators have failed to note this dominant biological theme in the paper (usually through ignorance of the field), it certainly did not elude Freud or Jung.

The day following Spielrein’s

Introduction  11 presentation of the paper, Freud wrote to Jung: “What troubles me most is that Fräulein Spielrein wants to subordinate the psychological material to biological considerations; this dependency is no more acceptable than a dependence on philosophy, physiology, or brain anatomy.”23 Jung replied: “I know, of course, that Spielrein operates too much with biology.

But she didn’t learn that from me, it is home-grown.”24 In contrast to her two earlier papers, there is an appealing simplicity about “Contributions to the Understanding of a Child’s Mind.” The prose is much closer to that of her diaries and letters, and conveys her own voice as it sounds in her personal writings.

Indeed, much of her paper is explicitly autobiographical, and she may well have extracted the passages concerning her own childhood from diaries that are no longer extant.

The two other children in the paper, Otto and Valli, were most probably unidentified members of her own family, or the children of friends.

We see once again her repeated preoccupation with the intersection between death and conception, this time reflected and confirmed in the fears and fantasies of the children she describes.

(The theme also appeared in one of the topics she chose for her final medical exam: lifethreatening bacterial infections in childbirth.)25 The transition from the first two papers to this one could scarcely be more marked.

Instead of taking psychosis in adults as her focus, her interest now is in normal childhood development and the fantasy life that accompanies this.

Rather than speaking of herself at one remove, she now appears in plain sight.

In place of convoluted sentences, there is a lucid style and a light touch that will entirely characterize her writing from now on.

She no longer gives the impression of someone knocking on the door, hoping to be admitted.

She is now an insider.

There is also little of Jung’s thinking here, which is also in keeping with almost everything else she wrote subsequently.

Only a few months after leaving Jung and Zurich behind her, she has moved on to Vienna in every sense.

In many ways, the topic, methods, style, and content of the paper represent not only the culmination of her development to date as a thinker and clinician, but also a new beginning.

The paper offers a presentiment of the articles and case reports she would submit to psychoanalytic journals over the next few years as she grew in maturity.

Before long, she would become a mother and start to take her daughter Renata as a subject for research, setting a precedent that Piaget would later follow with his own children.

Her observations on Renata would in time lead to her ground-breaking

12  Introduction paper on the development of language and speech in children: “The origin of the child’s words ‘Papa’ and ‘Mama.’” This would be heard by Anna Freud and Melanie Klein, who would develop the ideas in it without acknowledgment.

Spielrein would also challenge Piaget’s separation of cognitive development from the child’s inner world, anticipating the integrative work of her later colleagues Luria and Vygotsky.

Whether or not she realized it at the time, Spielrein was now about to embark on a long career that would span many of the great centers of Europe.

She would encounter many different schools of psychological thought but show sole allegiance to none, and try always to make connections rather than to endorse divisions.

She would embrace many different fields of learning including not only psychiatry and psychoanalysis but child psychology and psychotherapy, linguistics, and pediatrics.

There is no doubt that, in the course of her life, she influenced other major thinkers of the twentieth century more than we will ever know.

Chapter 1 On the Psychological Content of a Case of Schizophrenia (Dementia Praecox) (1911) Introduction In recent years, research studies1 have led to an understanding of schizophrenia (dementia praecox) which, in various respects, is in need of a broader empirical foundation.

It is my intention to investigate a case of paranoid dementia, initially without regard to the current scholarly opinions, but guided solely by the goal of obtaining a deeper insight into the psychic processes of this patient.

I chose this case because the patient – as an intelligent and well-read woman – offers substantial material, which, at first glance, appears to be a confused mix of completely nonsensical sentences.

I deem it best to present the material as completely as possible, that is, nearly word for word as communicated to me by the patient, so that the reader has the opportunity to verify whether my conclusions are justified.

I would only ask the reader not to make assumptions about my conclusions based on the arbitrariness of any fragment: Having the entire analysis before my eyes, I found myself occasionally moving on ahead with an explanation.

Nevertheless, the subsequent material will furnish the proof for the accuracy of the “interpretation.” In many cases, my interpretations were confirmed in spontaneous statements by the patient.

On other occasions, the patient was unable to provide straightforward answers to the questions, preferring rather to make vague statements that digressed into other topics which, in turn, one would have to decode, thereby risking getting lost in details.

Furthermore, pushing for a certain explanation deprives us of the advantage of examining the unconstrained chain of associations as a whole.

In addition, one would risk forcing the patient to reveal embarrassing facts, thus evoking feelings of displeasure about being examined.

While following these considerations, I had to draw my conclusions mostly from her previous statements and the

A Case of Schizophrenia  15 general context.

In the beginning, I had to pay close attention to details in order to ensure the correctness of my conclusions.

Later, however, for example in the chapter “Childhood impressions,” etc., when I was already quite familiar with the patient’s language, I chose not to trouble her with questions, but translated her words directly into our language.

In order to avoid influencing the patient, I did not look at her medical history and anamnesis until I had basically finished the examination.

Only then did I consider the extent to which my findings matched her medical history [clinical notes] and the description of her health history, and how my findings might be able to interpret the latter.

It was not easy to follow the confused mix of her thoughts.

For that reason, frequent repetitions seemed to me to be required at various points.

Nevertheless, in order to test the correctness of my conclusions, one must proceed like an investigating judge and familiarize oneself with the case meticulously, to the extent that one virtually empathizes with every word.

Anamnesis (from the medical history) The husband gives the following report: He has known the patient for eleven years.

He does not know of childhood illnesses she might have had.

She was always healthy, and he also did not notice any mental abnormalities.

While in school, she was intelligent and had many interests, particularly in literature.

She has always shown religious tendencies.

Thirteen years ago, they married.

In their sexual relationship she was frigid.

Soon after getting married, she became pregnant.

The pregnancy proceeded normally, the birth went well, and so did the days following the delivery.

Her child once almost swallowed a small ball.

This terrified her so much that she was agitated for several days.

Six years ago, she became pregnant again.

At that time, she had quite an upsetting experience: The doctor told her that her mother was suffering from cancer of the esophagus.

As a result, the patient had seizures during which she believed she was dying and thought her heart stopped beating.

The attacks lasted from half an hour to an hour.

Often, she calmed down the moment a doctor arrived.

She had no appetite and, apparently, already had stomach problems.

The birth was a difficult forceps delivery.

When they started the anesthesia, ether ran down her face and burnt her eyes.

This experience continued

16  A Case of Schizophrenia to haunt her for a long time.

When, two years later, her mother died, she handled her death well.

In spring of 1903, she had a spontaneous abortion (in about the seventh month).

After a dilation and curettage procedure, she ran a fever and was quite weak.

For three weeks, she was hospitalized.

While there, she was sedated a third time.

While being under anesthetic, she apparently had horrible dreams about which she later recounted ceaselessly.

(The reporting person [her husband] does not know any details about it!).

When she returned home, she showed, as in previous times, a strong desire for love which, however, appeared to the reporting person as exaggerated and pretentious.

Furthermore, she displayed a great urge to work, but was often quite tired.

In the summer before her illness, she enjoyed a happy vacation.

The patient was exuberant.

Afterwards, however, she felt drained and believed she was pregnant again, although she was not.

Since fall of 1905, she has passionately cared for a poor family and always showed effusive joy when something for the poor was sent to her.

On November 16, when a deaconess came to visit her, she was noticeably quite anxious and prayed with her.

At night, she woke her husband and told him he was not religious enough.

On November 17, her behavior was calm and normal.

The patient runs an embroidery business.

Once she had kept a piece of fabric from a dress that had been delivered.

She wrote the woman in question that she had kept some of the fabric (of which too much had been sent to her), and she felt guilty because of this.

In the evening, she again criticized her husband over religion and said she would not be quiet until her husband and his family had also found salvation.

On the morning of November 18, she was euphoric.

In the afternoon, she cried and suddenly made a big scene because, in her eyes, her husband did not have the right belief.

She alleged that her sister was sick and had to go to bed, considered herself dirty, and claimed she was not allowed to go to bed.

From then on, she continually spoke in a chaotic jumble and alleged that everyone was dirty, that everyone had to wash their hands, and that her feet needed to be washed.

Then she prayed again.

She continued in this way until she was admitted to the hospital.

That was all we learned from her husband.

It was impossible to come up with a consistent anamnesis by talking with the patient herself because, as her husband attests, her words were “a chaotic jumble.” Let us now look at the further progress of her illness.

I will mention only as much from her

A Case of Schizophrenia  17 medical history as is necessary for understanding the chapter in question.

For that reason, I should mention here that the patient is a Protestant, her husband a Catholic.

She mentions several times that her husband, a professor by profession, was seduced by two of his female students.

One student in particular, a beautiful rich girl, was to blame.

She refers to this girl as That Woman [Frauenzimmer].

The present state (status praesens) The patient looks a little pale and tired; otherwise – no noticeable physical disabilities.

Sense of orientation – for time and location – good.

Mental retention and memory – not disturbed.

Affectivity – inadequate.

The patient appears like a bad actress who cannot share her feelings with the outside world and, in order to compensate for that inability, acts in an excessively emotive way.

The pathos of the patient seems somewhat forced.

Her facial expressions range from masklike to serious to empty; at other times she seems to smile to herself.

The sound of her voice shows little modulation.

Its pathos seems empty and basically “without affect.” Her language – is highly confused, sometimes interspersed with an absurd play on words.

She frequently shuts down and holds back thoughts.

Hallucinations – primarily of her face, her hearing (voices), and her physical feelings, for example, of becoming electrified.

Delusions – highly absurd.

For example, she thinks she is being “catholicized,” contaminated with urine, “beaten through Basel,” is anesthetized and then wakes up as a horse, is a little Forel, is “dissected,” “phrenologized,” “mythologically treated,” etc. Mannerism – Sometimes she lies “on her stomach,” at other times, she falls on her knees before God and in a solemn voice whispers words to herself; in general, few characteristic manners.

Psychomotor abnormalities – like catatonia – almost altogether absent; no consistent negativity, no catalepsy; echolalia, echopraxia – absent; no stereotypical demeanor or perseverations.

18  A Case of Schizophrenia Usually stays in the ward for the restless, is occasionally quite violent.

Diagnosis: A paranoid form of dementia praecox. 1.

Catholicization We learn from the medical history that the patient did not love her husband in a sexual sense and had many fights with him.

She claims that he had preferred one of his students over her.

Her husband is a Catholic, the patient a Protestant.

The patient often mentions that she was being “catholicized” here in the institution.

Question:  “What do you mean by catholicizing?” Answer: “Michelangelo, Sistine art, and the Madonna have to do with art history.

The Madonna has come into contact with Lao art; this has to do with Laocoön.

Sistine art is sexual art.

Derived from Sistine art is Lao art or generation art.

Sistine art can elicit sexual art: By looking at a beautiful image, one can turn into poetry and perhaps forget one’s duty.

Sistine poetry is the poetry of the Catholics and must have to do with the Madonna, Raphael, and the whole poetry of Catholics.” This is what the patient said.

Everyone is familiar with the Sistine Chapel in Rome, a Catholic place of worship that also houses the frescoes of Michelangelo.

Another part of the Catholic worship is the Madonna, whose beauty is adored by the whole world.

One Madonna by Raphael is, as is well known, called the Sistine Madonna. “The Sistine art” (Chapel), or rather Catholic religion (= art = “poetry”), is associated with beauty (Madonna, Raphael, Michelangelo).

From the Sistine art, sexual art is derived: “By looking at a beautiful image, one can turn into poetry and perhaps forget one’s duty,” says the patient.

The phrase “forget one’s duty” out of a wife’s mouth openly reveals the erotic element of “poetry,” so that we may equate “poetry” with “in love.” In fact, the patient claims that her husband lets himself be blinded by beauty and thereby forgets his duties towards his wife and children.

Without being asked further questions, she continues: The psychology of vanity is not linked with the psychology of a mother, only insofar as aesthetics dictates that one dresses rather attractively.

I have no respect for a kind of psyche to which beauty matters more than inner purity.

A Case of Schizophrenia  19 After that, she says her husband had preferred “beauty” (“That Woman”) above “inner purity” (of the patient).

Since her husband is Catholic, his actual love and sexual love in general are called Catholic “poetry, “art,” “religion,” etc. In addition, the phonetic similarity between the words “Sistine” [sixtinisch] and “sexual” [sexuell] might have contributed to the formation of this symbol.

The patient even creates a verb that corresponds to the word “Catholic” (husband), “to catholicize,” which means more or less “to treat like a Catholic” (someone who gets enthused about sexual love = Catholic poetry).

From sexual art one can readily derive “generation art” (the creation of new generations), which is referred to as “Lao art,” since its symbol is Laocoön.

(“Derived from Sistine art is Lao art or generation art.”) The patient chooses Catholic religion and “religion” in general as a symbol for sexuality, primarily because she perceives religion as the spiritual element that represents the opposite of sexuality and thus the physical element.

This seemingly paradoxical statement has the following reason: By referring to the sexual through its negative (the spiritual), she most strongly rejects the former.

However, by claiming that the highest, that is, religion, signifies sexuality, she assigns the highest value to sexuality.

The patient repeatedly creates symbols by expressing an idea through its negative or by reversing the idea, as one can see in the following examples: In answer to my question whether she knows any Catholics outside the institution, she mentions a family in which the man was a Protestant and the woman a Catholic.

The two constantly fought, etc. She then adds that it was also possible that the man was a Catholic and the woman a Protestant.

The patient is enraged that her husband beat the children.

Shortly thereafter, she mentions a “case among the poor,” where the mother “perhaps” beat the children.

The people had the plague.

She knows this because the woman smelled of “impure surroundings, of prostitution.” Moreover, the patient claims that her husband infected her with prostitution, contaminated her, and made her ill, etc. She has the woman emulate in her examples all her husband’s behaviors that outrage her. 2.

Psychological-Sistine experiments The patient said that she was capable of being unfaithful to her husband, mentally and also physically, because she did not feel any respect for the kind of “psyche” that he possesses.

I asked her whether she had ever encountered a better psyche.

The patient replies:

20  A Case of Schizophrenia In the institution, it was Prof. Forel.

I found many psyches known through literature, which made me think of sexual love.

I have learned to know sexual love in a Forel-like nature as a navitic (?) [navitische] obligation.

There must be a higher religion somewhere, a higher psyche, skies in need of a divinity that long for Sunday.

However, That Woman (my husband’s student), has deprived me of religion and faith.

This is an animalistic dysentery, a dysentery of sexuality that has to do with Doctor Laocoön.

There is a psychological, animalistic, and vegetarian Marmite (French for pan2).

The vegetarian pan is the one that has to do with a contempt for flesh [Fleisch = meat, flesh].

When vegetable becomes impure and sexualizing, then the vegetarian pan is also inadequate.

Marmite is the pan and has to do with the gifts of God who sends food.

We see that the patient talks about three kinds of pans (while using the word “Marmite” instead of pan), in order to explain sexual “dysentery.” For the time being, we can assign a special meaning to the psychological pan, in contrast to the animalistic and vegetarian ones.

The patient herself is treated like a psychological-Sistine experiment to which a psychological and Sistine pan would correspond.

The latter could further be divided into two pans: a “vegetarian” one, which “has to do with the contempt for the flesh” (this would be the negation of sexuality), and an animalistic one (affirmation of sexuality).

The following sentence proves the connection between the pans and sexuality: “When vegetable becomes impure and sexualizing,” etc. Translated into our language, this means the following: When the negation of sexuality (vegetable) is contaminated by its affirmation, that is, turns into its affirmation, then what good is the vegetarian pan with respect to defence?

She then continues: “Vegetables can become impure, because farmers fertilize the earth with human excrements and urine.” “People can be dissolved in dust and water; this product can be absorbed by animals, for example, through the soil.

It is the genetics of mythology and mysticism.” I ask her: “How can a human being be dissolved in water?” Answer: “This has to do with the female anatomy.

To the stone that is formed from sand, we can give colors; this has to do with Professor Tino-Forel. 3  Forel has a sculptural sense.

He is the

A Case of Schizophrenia  21 sculptural artist of the psyche, who would loan his psyche to the dust in order to create a new human being.

The development of dust which emanates from the dust of the human being is turned into sculptural beauty through the purity of a color.” The patient sees the impurity in the fact that the soil absorbs, aside from seeds, human excrement and, particularly, urine.

At this point, I must jump ahead to something that we will later see more clearly: By earth, the patient means a woman.

The woman (earth) becomes impure because, in addition to semen (that is, during coitus), she also absorbs urine.

As a logical consequence, fantasies about the genesis of humans ensue.

In a subsequent comment, the patient explains why a human being is dissolved in water: “It has to do with the female anatomy.” In this sentence, the patient explicitly points out that the answer to “how” he is dissolved (my question is: How can a human be dissolved in water?), is found in the female sexual organs.

It is a beautiful idea: A stone is formed from dust that can then be animated.

From human dust, a new human being can arise by (here: Forel) loaning it one’s psyche.

(The sculptured beauty is created through the purity of the color – the blood, as we will see later.) In this context, we are reminded of the Book of Genesis, where God created man from dust and then breathed life into him.

The patient continues: “The stone figures must be dissolved in a mythological-pious manner, similar to a rigid soul on the cross.

This has to do with Laocoön poetry and the art of painting.” The preceding description suggests that the act of creating from stone must be equated to the creation of man.

The sentence, “The stone figures are dissolved,” parallels the dissolving of the human being in water.

Based on previous comments, the latter is perceived as a new creation. “Mythological has to do with the creation of a new human being” (patient’s expression).

The term “piety” (opposite of “sin”) – is, as we have previously seen,4 the escape from sexuality into religion (“compensation”), therefore affirmation through defence.

The expression “rigidity of the soul on the cross” represents an identification with Christ who dies on the cross rigid.

Yet Christ will be resurrected!

In the same way, humans (stone figures) will also be resurrected.

For this reason, both sentences can be interpreted in the following way: The stones are animated (a new human being is created), and through this the torturous rigor mortis (Christ-Laocoön5-Soul) is “dissolved”;6 this

22  A Case of Schizophrenia happens through procreation which is the result of sexuality (cf. “Sistine art is sexual art.

Derived from sexual art is Lao art or generational art”). “Mythological – this means through the magic of the power of fire: Fire purifies everything, it cleans the coals and the cinder.” This sentence is closely related to an earlier statement. “Magic of the power of fire” – is a poetic expression for heavenly as well as earthly love.

Jung made me aware of analogies in Persian myths.

The sun (fire) purifies the semen of the first human; equally, the semen of the holy bull [Urstier] goes into the moon (the female light) for purification. 7  “Ash can turn into a human.” This sentence, which the patient adds right away, proves that she also perceives fire as a fertilizing agent. “The cinder and spots that remain in the soul have to be dissolved through the fire of education,” the patient continues.

Then the patient claims that she once was treated with “enthusiasm,”8 but did not feel pure enough since she had fallen for the sake of her brother, sister, or girlfriend.

She then continues to talk about “complexes.” She had heard about “complexes” “from Prof. Forel, or perhaps from his brother” (note the uncertainty!).

She says his brother is Dr. J., a student of Prof. Forel: “He started off with the spiritualist solution of the religious question or the Sistine question.” We have now arrived at a deeper layer by the fact that, with great hesitation, she introduces a new person instead of Forel – Dr. J. treated her as a “psychological experiment.”9 The word “Sistine” is left out at first.

However, in the second sentence, the mistake is corrected by connecting the word “Sistine” with “spiritualist (hence spiritual) solution.” Once again, the superficial (negative) layer of the “religious question” is mentioned first, then follows the deeper layer of the “Sistine” question, connected by the word “or.” The term “question,” which the patient favors and with which she is familiar, at least by name, is probably borrowed from the “sexual question” of Prof. Forel.

Furthermore, we hear: Dr. J. has probably worked with suggestion and hypnosis in order to explore the topic of insanity; alcohol plays a role here. 10  The spiritual aspect of wine is passed on to the psyche.

In medicine, alcohol is used for purification.

Hence, the psychological treatment is connected to alcohol; that is, with “the spiritual aspect of wine that is passed on to the psyche.” By

A Case of Schizophrenia  23 emphasizing the asexual meaning of wine, both expressions allow us to assume something quite animalistic about wine that is “transferred” into something animalistic.

Alcohol, like fire, purifies everything and is therefore identified with the latter based on its function. 11  After purification through fire, we were shown new humans rising from the ashes.

Does the same happen after purification through alcohol? “This is a description of the Italian lakes,” explains the patient to us. “They become visible through a chasm in the earth.

This is how the mythological legends of the Italian arise . . . or how should I phrase it?” The word “Italian” is used by the patient as an expression for beauty, poetry, art, and the like (as we have seen, = love).

An Italian lake would therefore be a beautiful lake or a lake filled with love.

That she means woman by using the term “earth,” is later proven beyond all doubt.

Thus from a crevice in the woman’s body (in the earth), the water (lake) emerges.

This procedure, supposedly, leads to the creation of “mythological” legends, that is, legends which “have to do with” the creation of human beings. 12  It is quite possible that there is a connection between water emerging from a woman’s body and the creation of human beings if, for example, we think of the amniotic fluid!

The psychological treatment by Dr. J. would therefore represent a description of the processes involved in the genesis of humans.

For the time being, let us regard this interpretation not as a certainty, but as a possibility.

The assumption would also be supported by the barrier that the patient puts up before she continues to talk about Dr. J.: Dr. J. was pursued by love which his cheerful nature and the mystical treatment of the sexual question (the alcohol question) had brought him; that is, by affection that can change into antipathy.

I was beaten through Basel.

This has to do with the Schnitzelbank [lit. carving table].

Its symbol is the carnival which wants to drive out crudity, perhaps the crudity of wine.

In wine there is much crudity.

The patient also talks about the necessity of protecting children. “During carnival there is the Vehmic Court.

Schnitzelbank is the anatomic examination, dissection, or phrenology.

The Schnitzelbank can, so to speak, be a dissecting of the soul.” The ostensible jump from Dr. J. to Basel is justified because the patient knows quite well that Dr. J. comes from Basel.

This is where she is judged by Dr. J., as her own words indicate, because to her the Schnitzelbank13

24  A Case of Schizophrenia is a “soul-dissection,”14 = “anatomic examination.” Her words reveal the same mechanism as the sentence about the “‘spiritualist’ solution of the Sistine question”; that is to say, it is again a way of expressing the sexual by means of its negative15 (“soul-dissection”); its equivalent, the animalistic, is the “anatomic examination.” Earlier, we heard the statement: “The Sistine (sexual) has to do with the female anatomy.” As for phrenology – it will be explained later.

The expression, to run the gauntlet “through Basel,” refers, on one hand, to the institution of the Schnitzelbank, on the other hand, to the association experiment which is, however, as we saw, basically the same, since the satirical verses of the Schnitzelbank rebuke those involved for all their sins.

The same thing happens, as we know, during the association experiment.

The patient seems to have noticed this more clearly than certain critics of Jung’s works.

As a result, “Schnitzelbank” is a splendid satirical expression for the “association experiment.” During the Schnitzelbank, one is the object of gossip in all of Basel, and one has to run the gauntlet (“make one’s way through”).

In the same way, during an association experiment one is driven through a sequence of emotive words, while one or another word strikes a hard blow; that is, triggers a complex.

What does the patient make of this embarrassing situation?

In the case of “beating,” the patient immediately thinks of the need to protect children.

We know that her husband beat their children.

But we also know that in the examples that she mentioned before (in chapter 1, “Catholicization”), the woman beat her children.

In this case she is beaten by Dr. J.

Immediately thereafter follows a fantasy of procreation: The anatomic examination, the soul-dissection, or phrenology have to do with [the novels] Jörn Uhl and Frau Sorge; that is, the conquering of the earth.

The profits that one gains through work, the blessed money, has multiplied so much that it is further used and yields further multiplication.

The expression that money has “multiplied,” and, in particular, “the blessed money,” sounds peculiar.

It more likely belongs to the sexual sphere where terms like “blessed condition” and “blessed with children” are fitting words.

As the source for multiplication of the money, the patient names the earth which, previously, was also the starting point for procreation fantasies, and to which, as I mentioned, she later directly referred to as

A Case of Schizophrenia  25 “woman.”16 The patient adds that Basel has to do with the Indian mission, the proselytizing of heathens to Christianity.

As is generally known, Basel is the seat of the Basel Mission which has become famous in various parts of the world.

It is noteworthy that the patient thinks of this society after her procreation fantasy.

Yet of course, it seems natural to us, if we recall that in the first chapter “religion” meant sexuality.

As a result, “conversion to Christianity” would mean conversion to “sexual life.” The patient just referred to Dr. J.

She knows with certainty that he is from Basel.

I ask her whether she knows anyone from Basel.

Of course, “Dr J.” would have been the spontaneous answer to this question.

However, her answer is different: There is Mr. Lauers who is from Basel; I would not know whether this has anything to do with Dr. J.; Arnold Böcklin17 is also from there.

All this has to do with alcohol, but not with the physical psyche . . . or perhaps it does?

He is an alcoholic because, as a psychiatrist and teetotaller, he had to dedicate himself to the study of alcohol.

This likely has to do with the understanding of Italian art, not only the art of painting, but also music.

He probably misunderstood the nature of friendship by perceiving the sexual question in such a way that – in order to prove the experiments – he felt he also had to perform the function that has to do with the sexual question, the Sistine art.

In a sober state he would probably not want to contribute to the misfortune of children and women.

She had a dream that Dr. J. “performed” the sexual function on her, “to experimentally exist in the lower realm.” The initial sound of the word “Lauers” (a person who lives in Basel), shows great similarity to the word Lao as in Laocoön, just as Lao = Laocoön, Lauers = Lao.

As we have seen earlier, Laocoön is a symbol for “genesis.” At the beginning of this chapter, Laocoön is called “doctor.” Here, however, the patient mentions Lauers (Laocoön) instead of Doctor J., whose name we would rather have expected.

The idea “doctor” occurs to her, but she does not want to allow it, as the following sentence shows: “I would not know whether this has anything to do with Dr. J.” After this symbolic substitution of the main person, the imagination again uses a symbolic disguise.

A painter is mentioned who, as she claims, is connected with alcohol.

Why does the patient come up with the idea of an

26  A Case of Schizophrenia alcoholic?

Already at the beginning of this chapter, we learned that, to her, alcohol is a beverage of fertility.

Does Dr. J. as a fertility-bestowing artist perhaps give this beverage to her?

Since the patient cannot allow this, a compromise follows: “It has to do with alcohol, but not with the physical psyche,” whereby “physical” denotes the positive, “psyche” the negative affirmation of the fertility symbol.

The patient notices that the two terms used together basically cancel each other out.

Yet in a rather skillful mental maneuver, she overcomes the difficulty: “Dr. J. is an alcoholic because, as a psychiatrist and a teetotaller, he had to dedicate himself to the study of alcohol.” Of course, if he embodies the negative component, that is, he is a teetotaller, he also must possess the positive component from which the negative is derived.

There has to exist in the teetotaller a positive component that craves alcohol, against which he fights.

In the same way, we would not have light, if there were no darkness.

By negating light, darkness actually creates it. 18  In this way, the positive sexual component gains increasing importance, and, finally, we learn that Dr. J. did not only study the function that has to do with sexuality, but he also performed it on his patient.

She also had dreams about this.

When I mentioned to her that it was just a dream and not reality, she became quite enraged.

Dreams contain symbols that have to be interpreted. 19  However, what, if we do not comprehend or do not want to comprehend the symbolism?

What if we have to surrender to the dreams?

If I talked about dreams, I would have to re-experience everything all over again.

Dreams are real experiences.

At another time, she is annoyed about bad “assumptions” people have about her.

In this context, she mentions that “the assumption could become reality, in order to prove that it rightfully exists.” We also find comments such as: “Probably the assumption wanted to be right.” These views, or rather feelings, are certainly not suggested, for she spontaneously expresses them while showing strong emotions, and they also correspond to the psychological state of a person with dementia praecox, who gladly claims to be surrounded by a strange secret power.

As we see here and will see later, the “assumptions” that, in the patient’s eyes, other persons often express, are always affectively toned groups of ideas, therefore “complexes.”20 The patient feels the power that her complexes have over her so strongly that she perceives them almost as independent living beings,

A Case of Schizophrenia  27 or rather beings that can come alive through their intrinsic volition.

(I would like to point out that the patient’s notion of the independent existence of complexes represents an essential proof for the frequently contested Autonomy Doctrine. 21 ) “The assumption can become reality.” This thought is the basis for her fear of other people’s “evil eye.” Whatever has deep roots in folk superstition is always justifiable.

In our patient’s understanding, “assumption” is, of course, more or less “the imposing” of certain intentions, that is, of certain complexes.

As affectively toned groups of ideas, they obviously exert great influence on someone’s actions.

Therefore, under certain conditions, it is quite possible that assumptions that prove to be correct instantly become reality, since such assumptions are, as I mentioned, not merely random possibilities.

Rather, they make us “objectively” aware of complexes as psychic quantities that strive to shape reality in their own image.

A complex always represents a possibility that, by being activated in a special way, receives the right to exist; ergo, becomes reality.

Thus, under favorable circumstances, the patient’s “assumption” has the potential to become reality.

Her erotic complex also strives for ways to express itself.

These expressions evoke corresponding reactions in another person.

However, in the other person, this idea is completely pushed into the background by many other ideas.

In contrast, the patient who lives exclusively in her complex only sees what corresponds to her wish and therefore overestimates the possibility that her wish is realized.

According to the patient, Dr. J.’s alcoholism does not only correspond to an understanding of Italian art; that is, not only to the art of painting that would correspond to the poetry of the Catholics,22 but also to music.

The patient has musical talent; as she says, the poetry of the Protestants (she is a Protestant) is related to music.

Love for music would therefore mean love for the patient.

We must now draw from our material, in order to be able to understand the “question of alcohol.” “Man can be dissolved in water” (we have already heard this).

Now we learn: He can also be dissolved in wine (spirit or soul), in alcohol, or through a chemical process.

This can turn into a kind of Novozoon that can be passed to other bodies.

When good people are treated with anatomy and mythology, then good qualities of a dead person can be transmitted to healthy people.

28  A Case of Schizophrenia Another remark follows: Novozoon, produced from a kind of hydrogen, is a specimen made from dead substance and can be derived from any animal.

However, it must be a healthy animal.

In order to create a new generation, its whole body needs to be prepared.

A new generation rises from the head and from the spermatic development in the animal.

Unfortunately, I neglected to ask the patient how she came up with the word Novozoon.

Nevertheless, by “hydrogen” she means water which the patient continuously praises as the origin of life. 23  The origin of Novozoon easily lets us identify hydrogen as sperm.

The new generation is derived from the head and the sperm development in the animal.

As we know, the head is the seat of the soul, the symbol for the intellect.

The intellect is the opposite of the animalistic.

It is the component that negates sexuality.

The “sperm development,” obviously, is a direct affirmation.

Therefore, the creation of a new generation requires, besides affirmation, negation.

I want to draw special attention to this formulation, as well as to the idea of Novozoon as a “dead substance” which plays the role of the life-giving sperm.

The patient continues to tell us the following: The color of the wine may be red, white, or golden.

The Bible is the wine of the Lord.

Wine is the blood of Jesus (cf. “Man can be dissolved in wine”).

Question: “Where is the human being present in the blood?” Answer: “In utero.” She continues: It (the child) should be present in the marriage, but when the sanctity of marriage does not exist, it had to come from the Holy Grave, just as it was in Mary’s case.

The saints are turned into stone or wax.

But man must be saved by the cross.

Jesus created disciples for our salvation, male and female ones.

In the patient’s case, the sanctity of marriage was missing.

For that reason, she has to bear a child through the Holy Spirit.
