62  A Case of Schizophrenia misunderstood the friendship to the woman thinking that, in order to prove the experiments, he also had to perform the function that has to do with the Sistine (sexual) art.” The patient continues her explanation: There is a poetry of the white cross and of the black cross.

Jesus Christ died for the pure form of religion, for purity, since we, purified from all [sin], prove our filiation.

We have people sick from childbearing.

They are treated by educators with the spoken and written word in Volapük, in a translation of Emperor Charles.

We had an excellent teacher in history.

It was about the histology of Emperor Charles in history.

The humoristic element most deserves to win the prize.

The histology of Emperor Charles is probably due to a misunderstanding in music.

He was not satisfied with cheerfulness among his people; rather, he looked for serious people, also for serious women who were capable of lifting his melancholia.

He was a great melancholic.

Histology is a disease.

Maybe there is something Platonic to perfected music.

The black and white crosses symbolize martyrdom for an unjust and just cause.

Jesus died for the just cause, for the “examination” of “childlikeness”; that means, for carrying out the “function” that has to do with the “childlikeness.”97 The patient is “sick from children” since she had a spontaneous abortion.

For that reason, she is being treated by a psychiatrist (“educator in the spoken and written word” – opposite). “Histology” is a “disease of the tissue,” for which a lack of love is to blame. 98  The patient is musically talented, and she is misunderstood. 99  This is the reason why Emperor Charles’s histology is based on a misunderstanding in music.

The patient herself has always been serious and preferred seriousness to excessive cheerfulness.

Emperor Charles also looked for serious people, or rather females with a disposition similar to that of the patient.

He is a melancholic; he searches for something “Platonic” (opposite) “with the perfected music” (the patient). “The noblesse of the Physe [body] is obliged to give itself to those who have been neglected by another religion,” the patient continues, since she, too, was neglected by the “Catholic religion” of her husband who preferred a beautiful rich girl over her.

For that reason, the “noble body” of Emperor Charles = Dr. J. must dedicate itself to her.

Now follows the opposite: “The psychological purity of the animal has an educational value.” This

A Case of Schizophrenia  63 sentence needs to be understood in the following way: The value (the meaning) of education consists in its striving for sexual (“psychological”) activity with those who deserve it (sexual “purity”). 100  The patient continues: It would be the forest into which God the Father sent his rays for his male and female disciples.

Thank you, Professor,101 now I have the memory of the Physe [body] and pure efforts, which through Job’s action become sufferings that are alleviated by a life in purity, by Professor Forel’s life: I once had pure water.

Here I had pure water.

After the baths had been psychologized, I had pure water again.

Question: “Baths were psychologized?” Answer: “The spermatic psychologies led to various things that informed the Physes [bodies].

Spermatic psychologies are the same as the psychology of the man: The spermatic psychology, through spermatic purity, leads to the study of religion.” It is rather obvious that “to psychologize” describes a sexual activity.

The mere choice of a word that stresses something “psychic” (opposite to the animalistic) suggests this interpretation.

Furthermore, the psychologies are “spermatic” and tell the Physe, that is, the body, something. “Through spermatic purity one arrives at the study of religion”; that is, if (or: while) one has pure spermatic water, one can be sexually active.

(“Religion” = sexuality, as we know.

Furthermore, we know that studying the corresponding sexual function is followed by performing it.) Now the patient claims that she herself had “psychologized” the baths.

Question: “How did you psychologize the baths?” Answer: “It is the psychology that perhaps has to do with music or dingle-dangle things.

This can result from the lack of means among artists.

People who otherwise do not find work, search for a way to make a living.

This has to do with the child question and the question of money for living expenses.” She says that she had to use her children to cover living expenses due to poverty.

The patient does not answer the part of my question that refers to the how, but only the why.

We already heard that the stitchery (the patient)102

64  A Case of Schizophrenia comes in contact with prostitution due to her “lack of means.” We also heard that Kocher wanted to force her to “enjoy sexual pleasure” and asked her what she wanted for it in return.

If we recall that “bread” has the same meaning as “childlikeness in the water” (cf. “The bread turns into a child”), then her words clearly express that the patient is treated with “spermatic baths”; that is, with water that contains sperm, due to her childlessness.

Obviously, lack of means also implies poverty, from which the patient suffers indeed, due to her shortage of money.

However, she also suffers as a woman, because her husband preferred a richer woman over her. 103  In this way, the gap in identification between money shortage and sexual want is bridged, and the means that removes one evil, at the same time, removes the other.

We already encountered money symbolism in the context of her procreation fantasies. 104  What follows are previously mentioned fantasies that express the necessity that children of the poor should receive their needed nourishments with the appropriate disinfection from “men.” 7.

Poverty complex and garment symbolism The patient thinks she hears the voice of Mrs. K.

(a female inspector of a factory and a school for home economics).

The voice sounds hostile: “Perhaps she is afraid of competition.” God’s voice says that the patient should become a factory inspector. “The inspector has to verify jobs and prevent overproduction.” Furthermore, God’s voice says: “Amen in the mission.

The heathens may no longer go to the Christians who curse.” Here everyone is an atheist.

The stitchery always has to do with God.

I am not at odds with Ms. Z. 105  Ms. Z became hostile towards me.

My social endeavors make me an enemy of the aristocratic movement; that is, the improvement of the situation.

I am an enemy of certain items that are perhaps not practical enough, of luxury items and unnecessary jewellery.

One has to fight against them when necessary, and when the trades* are harmful to one’s health.

It is mainly those trades that attract dust; the way in which the wool is made makes it catch too much dust.

(*At first a slip of the tongue: “tissues.”) By her slip of the tongue of “tissue” [Gewebe] for “trade” [Gewerbe], the patient reveals herself.

The chapter “Histology and its treatment”

A Case of Schizophrenia  65 described at length that by “tissues” the patient meant the tissue of the female sexual organs.

The dust that these tissues (that is, organs) catch is the “human dust.” In the chapter “Psychological-Sistine experiments” we encountered dust as a symbol for sperm.

As a factory inspector, the patient wants to fight against the over-privileged owners of such luxury trades (tissues).

The following shows the direction into which her thoughts go: I am a great enemy of silly stitchery which is not even worth the effort.

God is probably also against stitchery for the purpose of shipping it to the tropics where light garments are treated in the way they ought to be treated.

Simple laundry with pretty stitchery – it would be fitting for countries where garments are burnt after one has worn them several times.

Yet, it is not part of treating tuberculosis, if one gives [to the infected] unbleached garments to wear and beds in which the sick lie.

In the chapter “Poetry of the tropics” we learned about “mission dangers” that consist in mothers sacrificing themselves with their children in order to save the father.

The children were eaten by animals, which were nourished “by genesis of the human fetus.” We noticed there that the act of creating life was represented by death symbols.

Here, the patient has the “stitchery,” that is, herself,106 treated for the purpose of being sent to the tropics; that is, for her resurrection through death.

Immediately thereafter, she adds the thought about treating garments.

As we have seen numerous times, “to treat” in the patient’s mind means to have sexual intercourse.

Does the patient perhaps compare herself to the garments?

Let us find out: There are voices rebuking her because she “lies down on her womb all day long.” Question: “What does this mean, ‘to lie on one’s womb’?” Answer: “Have you ever had visions?” I: “Yes.” Patient: “Have you ever seen eyes in the snow?” I: “I don’t quite know what you mean by that.” Patient: “You must know that water is tears from heaven?” I: “Yes . . . ”

66  A Case of Schizophrenia Patient: “The water freezes to ice, to snow.” I: “What then are the eyes?” Patient: “The eyes are an expression of the soul that, due to pain, has frozen to snow.

One has frozen into a snow crust through the pain one has suffered.

And Christ wants to free us from all these sufferings.

This is the holy mission.” This symbolism is reminiscent of similar ideas in Nordic mythology where, as we know, the earth, frozen in snow and ice in the cold winter, is redeemed by the rays of the sun god, and fertilized.

The patient moves from the image of breaking the frozen snow crust to ideas of penetration: “With the glowing iron, one can penetrate the mountain,” she continues. “Do you know iron in medicine?

Iron glows when one pushes it into a stone.

Do you know Benjamin Franklin who discovered the lightning rod?” We discussed earlier the penetration fantasies, as well as the relationship between iron and stone.

The patient compares them to lightning. 107  From all of this, one may conclude that the patient identifies herself with the earth (as shown above); specifically, with the earth that is covered by a layer of snow the surface of which looks like a white sheet widely spread out.

(This calls to mind the corresponding poetical comparisons.) Question: “What do you mean by ‘lying on the womb’?” Answer: “It is like a sheet when it is bleached.” (As we know, linen sheets are laid out in the sun so they bleach.) Question: “What happens with the sheet when it is bleached?” Answer: “It is bleached in the water of the vagina.” Question: “What does this have to do with ‘lying on the womb’?” Answer: “Bleaching water and pale as corpses.” The patient is “as pale as a corpse” (indeed she is very pale), like a sheet that has been submerged in bleaching water.

In other instances, by “water,” the patient has always referred to “spermatic water”; that is, the man’s water.

She is separated from her husband.

She once said that Mrs. K. blamed her for being a “separated” woman.

The patient is suggesting a connection between “separated” and “vagina water” [linguistic connection between ‘to separate’ = scheiden, and the German word for vagina, Scheide].

She is separated from coitus with her husband.

She has become pale like a corpse from his “vagina water,” that is, from intercourse with him.

A Case of Schizophrenia  67 In any case, it is certain that the patient is lying there like a bleached sheet.

In fact, she often lay for weeks in the isolation room on a carefully spread-out blanket covered with a linen sheet that was as carefully spread out.

(The earth underneath the snow layer!) She wants these bleached garments to be worn, and the garments will, of course, be burnt.

For the patient, as we know, is looking for the fire of love, the sunray that saves her soul which lies frozen underneath a crust of ice.

The patient once says in a solemn voice: “I have to lie on my womb in order not to crush the children.

The soul of my God is in the hay” (in the mattress on which she lies). “From there he sends brightness through transparent bodies.” The brightness that comes from God indicates that he has the nature of light (sun). “Jesus Christ has shown me his love by having a ray hit against the window,” adds the patient.

Thus, the patient is indeed saved by a sunray or, in essence, fertilized.

Again, by focusing on the anti-animalistic element, she points to the sexual nature of divine love: “The soul of my God is in the hay.” After having had numerous opportunities to convince ourselves of the sexual meaning of the psychical element, I believe that, even without further clues, we may read the sentence in the following way: “The body of my God is in the hay.” The patient continues: I have to lie on my womb so that the souls that lie in the bed don’t get crushed.

The deceased are able to rise as purified souls, as the souls of saints and children who have died.

With what will I keep the children occupied?

In her words, the preoccupation consists of the necessary toys with the necessary disinfection. “Disinfection” we have encountered as coitus.

As a result, the souls (= bodies) of the children are saved (= “purified”) through the sexual act.

In another place, the spread-out body is represented as a “blanket” instead of as a sheet. “The genesis (genesis of humans) is a Roman secret from the sheets.

The genesis in the cells are the quilts.” The secret of genesis is called “Roman” because the Sistine Chapel, which belongs to the Sistine (= sexual) art, is located in Rome.

We also recall the square blanket in the cell, from which the divine judgment, the fertilizing serpent, emerged.

Discourses from the medical history At this point, I will add several statements from the patient’s medical history, in order to show that she also uses analogous chains of associations when talking with other persons; furthermore, that one may apply the same analytical approach to those statements that have nothing to do with me.

I was shot through the head.

There is too much electricity in me that needs to be destroyed by the shot.

Once I was electrified on a chair, as if swaying.

Afterwards I felt quite relaxed.

The bed would move back and forth with me.

A female guardian overpowered me and generated the electric current, and I was knocked about.

Question: “Who electrified?” Answer: “It was an experiment.

When I have electricity in me and touch the iron bed frame, there are electrical effects, perhaps through the pulverized iron specimens [acting] as medication.” The patient experiences electrical feelings commonly connected with erotic ideas, in an excessive way, and she embellishes them within the meaning of the complex.

The expression “electrifying” is an analogous term for puncturing and hitting with a ray of lightning, etc. Jesus has to force this electricity (“life element”) out of her, as in former times her worldly desires.

Here he only shoots her through the head to achieve this.

(The shots through the lungs and the eyes are unmentioned.) That which creates the illusion, the complex, continues in the main symbols, only the details are changed.

Upon my question as to who electrified her, the patient answers: “It was an experiment.” This reminds us of the “Psychological-Sistine experiments” of Dr. J., when she “was beaten through Basel.” Here she is “knocked about.” The “electrical effects” are produced by “pulverized iron specimens” (cf. “stone and iron symbolism” [chapter 6], where it was shown that iron powder means sperm). “Once I had a vision: I had to lay my body on the floor, all spread out.

Then I had to touch the iron.” Here one can distinguish the spread-out sheet (the earth that is frozen underneath a crust of ice), which is saved by the hot iron (= the sunray). “Then an electrical surge went through my whole body and into the fingertips.

The limbs were twitching (movements expressing fear).

The

A Case of Schizophrenia  69 orders came from the floor,” that is, from below, because the patient is lying on the hay (cf. “The soul of my God is in the hay.”).

The power came through the iron, through the opening of the heating device.

I probably had snake venom in my body.

In the morning, a saving angel came to me.

On the color of the walls were images of female figures.

They comforted, lauded, and praised me saying that I was there to comfort the spirits that live in the cell.

The voice of H. asked me about medication against cholera, while I was the student, or was it the voice of the female professor, Professor M.,108 or the female medical advisor?

It said that I was like a little Forel and that I was so enlightened.

I myself was surprised about how much science was in me.

I even thought that you should not hold it against those people who knocked my head about so much, if this could produce such great science.

I needed to be prodded a bit because my functions were too phlegmatic; from all of this I became lackadaisical.

The result of the touching of the iron, the beatings, is the transformation into a little Forel.

Let us recall how Professor Kocher’s son hits her in order “to provoke something.” The patient mentioned to me as well that her functions were too phlegmatic; namely, due to masturbation.

According to the patient, masturbation leads “to a limpness of the mental nerves,109 the sexual nerves” (again we encounter the juxtaposition of two opposites, the psychical, “mental nerves,” and the animalistic, “sexual nerves”).

Furthermore, masturbation leads to a hard stool.

(Both she and her children suffer from hard stool = “Vehmic blockage.”) In this case, too, her illness is cured through coitus: She is electrified, “on the stool” [Stuhl, lit. meaning “chair”].

The latter probably refers to sexual feelings when passing stool.

In her cell, she has a mattress and a night stool.

She is asked about remedies against cholera.

Cholera, as a form of diarrhea, could be considered the opposite to constipation which the patient, as we know, traces back to the coldness during coitus and the ensuing masturbation.

Diarrhea, therefore, should occur as a result of an abundance of love.

Thus “the little Forel” fulfills her wish to have a child (from “Professor Forel”), who would also have his intelligence.

We may say the same about “the female student,” “the female Professor M.,” and “the female medical advisor.” The patient first uses a more modest title and gradually moves to more prestigious ones. “Science,” especially practicing “medicine,” also

70  A Case of Schizophrenia has the meaning of having sexual intercourse.

(She also refers to “science of the tissue” and “histology” as an “illness.”) Many times we have observed that the patient assigns to the doctor the role of the husband, that his treatment consists in coitus with the patient, and that the “medicine” which provides “the purity of health” is “spermatic water!” 9.

Childhood impressions, ideas on transformation, dreams It is of special interest to learn to what extent the patient’s illness was already determined in childhood and which role the closest relatives played in it.

The patient mentions that, as a child, she possessed the “ability to dream”; for example, she could see a toy in any piece of paper.

She says that she did not have many toys since her father was not rich.

As a young man, her father was very good; he was funny, never drank, and kept himself busy with singing and playing the guitar.

His relationship to her mother was good, but he did hit the children.

At first, the patient was quite fond of him, but then she did not like him anymore, because he beat her.

She claims that in her childhood she saw God, or she had “God-like dreams.” (Equating dream and reality!) At the age of eight, she was once unjustly beaten, and God revealed himself to her.

He “looked like a giant toy,” she says. “The giant toy could, for example, be Switzerland.

Like a giant toy it shines from a height which one cannot climb.” “He stood on the roof terrace.” In her dream, she looked down into a valley. “There were lovely little girls whom one was not allowed to hit.

Everything was reduced in size, cute, and lovely: little horses, cows, wagons.” (From the medical history.) “Father died from a religious disease, he became impure,” says the patient. “During my mother’s illness, he visited a brothel.” After her mother was cremated, she took a few bone fragments home.

She was in an exalted mood and blamed her father that he had caused her death through his bad conduct.

Finally, there were arguments at home about the sale of a house and the takeover of an enterprise.

Part of the estate was lost at that time.

According to the medical history, during the time when the patient regarded herself a “little Forel,” she heard voices talking of love, tempting voices.

Dr. S., a friend of her brother, was following her.

On another occasion, she heard the “father’s voice”: “You are probably strong, Woman Garden!” She frequently saw him, in all kinds of situations, friendly and rude.

A Case of Schizophrenia  71 “Father was an alcoholic,” she tells me, “Father was a horse friend; the horses liked his purity.” This is all we hear about her father.

The father was an alcoholic.

Physical violence is a common consequence of alcoholism.

The patient says that bed-wetting (masturbation) is the result of a bladder that caught a cold or was hit.

We also know of cases where hitting children triggered them to masturbate.

Therefore, the patient’s etiological explanation is not unfounded.

However, one can certainly observe next to masturbation a strong masochistic component that, as always in the illness, is compensated by noticeable sadism observed by caregivers.

The patient seeks guilt and atonement while simultaneously fleeing from them.

She is beaten by those whom she loves and hates, and for her, the hitting has a sexual connotation.

Whenever she uses “the hitting,” it produces an intense affect.

Sometimes she denies that her father hit her (as we know, we have frequently observed this uncertainty when the complex occurred), other times she remembers each detail.

She is also aware that she sought rescue in fantasies; for example, when she looked into a valley with “lovely little girls” whom one was not supposed to hit.

Alcoholism, her father’s vice, was also transformed into a sexual symbol.

Alcohol became the “spirit of the wine,” or rather, “of the blood.” In this way, alcohol ought to give new life, and it became something fertilizing, the seminal fluid. 110  The patient’s husband, who corresponds to the prototype of her “father,” is also an alcoholic, is “overpowering.” The transference to Dr. J. takes into consideration that he is a teetotaler and turns him into the opposite, an alcoholic.

Her father had musical talent, Dr. J. “enjoys perfected music.” She says her father had succumbed to prostitution and competed with Zeus, and that Dr. J. is a “Mormon friend who wants to enter into a new marriage every year.” Once her father himself appeared before her as the tempter.

Her father impresses her because of his “horse spirit”: He is tall, strong, and clean.

She appreciates these qualities in people to whom she “transfers.” She sees God, Jesus Christ, and Forel approach her in the shape of horses.

She once saw her father in a vision where he was overpowered by three gentlemen.

According to the above-mentioned symbolism (“horse spirit”), one could probably explain this vision as an expression of the patient’s need to see her father’s power broken by “three gentlemen.”111 There was a time when the patient longed to liberate herself from her parents.

72  A Case of Schizophrenia Before she entered the institution (she now mentions the time), she dreamt that the small house of her parents “went up in smoke.” Shortly thereafter, she had a dream: The area had turned into a sand desert, and she did not see any way out.

Then she came through a flowery gate where she found a silver spoon.

Question: “What does ‘sand desert’ mean?” Answer: “A bad situation.” Question: “What does the flowery gate mean?” Answer: “An arc of triumph.” Question: “And the silver spoon?” Answer: “That of which I was worthy, the right to reign over a kitchen.” We do not need to ask what “kitchen” means, for we know that it is a symbol for the creation of humans.

In order to “cook” = to create new human beings, one needs something that corresponds to the male organ: It is the spoon.

This interpretation is supported by the fact that the patient suddenly talks about her husband and undresses because she feels tired and wants to go to bed.

The dreams that she just mentioned take her to W. where, as a sixteen- or seventeen-year-old girl, she “yearned for love.” She says that she wanted to liberate herself from her parents and that the desire to do so was so strong that she wrote a personal ad for the newspaper.

A lieutenant answered who inquired into the dowry and ended his letter with the following words: “Therefore, may everyone who enters into a long-term relationship, examine well.” Back then, the patient became quite fearful that one of her parents might find out about this. “Or did I already know my friend back then?” she suddenly asks.

By “friend” she means her sister’s current husband whom she claims to have loved.

Apparently, he was two years older than the patient and did not reciprocate her love.

Later he fell in love with her sister.

My brother let him sleep on a box.

Now we also understand why, in her hallucinations, the patient develops the idea of destroying her sister and, at the same time, turns her into her own ideal personality. 113  For if she marries the young man, she will have to take the place of her eliminated sister.

The elimination of her parents appears equally suitable.

The patient hallucinates about her father being overpowered by three gentlemen.

Among them, she names “Dr. W., a friend of my husband and a teacher of my sister.” The patient’s husband was a teacher (professor).

However, teacher also has the meaning of “doctor,” and vice versa.

In the “schoolpath-poetry” with “Charlemagne,” we saw that the latter is the teacher who wants to have sexual relations with the patient’s sister.

At the same time, this teacher turned out to be Doctor J.

The “young man” plays the same role, with regard to the sister, as the teacher (the patient’s husband) and Dr. J. in the patient’s fantasies.

Instead of proclaiming as the winner the young man whom we just mentioned, the patient names a teacher = doctor whom we suspect is Dr. J.

And, indeed, we find associative connections between the “young man” (also called “friend”) and Dr. J.

There was a youth festival.

I was in a shoemaker’s family, and there was a dance until nine o’clock at night.

74  A Case of Schizophrenia (The patient kneels down.) “I did not like it there, I was always so tired.” The slip-of-tongue “balls” pointed us towards a connection that exists in the unconscious.

The idea that wants to emerge from deep inside is at first rejected by the conscious as inappropriate.

The patient corrects herself to “baths,” and continues to talk about the “young man,” but it does not take long, and the suppressed idea gains the upper hand.

The patient begins to talk about a merry-go-round and fairground booths which form the transition to the carnival fantasies.

As the chapter “PsychologicalSistine experiments” showed, she is “judged” and “beaten” by Dr. J. during the time of carnival which, in her thinking, is a sexual act.

Here, too, she eventually thinks of Dr. J.

While doing so, she kneels down as if she were praying to a heavenly judge.

What is it then that bridges and connects the content of the ideas of “baths” and “balls,” and has allowed the unconscious to replace one word with the other?

To the patient, both the mythological baths (that is, the baths that have to do with the genesis of humans) and carnival plays are symbols that realize her desire to have sexual relations with Dr. J.

It is interesting that through shared symbolism, the new love object, Dr. J., is completely blended with the old one.

The new experience is forced into the path of the old complex, so that the patient is struggling with the same resistances that occupied her earlier: The new lover, like the old one, is fighting against the power of the parents, and both have sexual relations with the patient’s sister.

We have additional supporting evidence: Already in her earliest childhood, the patient particularly liked two of her father’s horses.

The brown one, Dory, she compared to herself; the white one, Fritz, to her brother.

What do these beloved become in the unconscious?

All persons whom the patient likes are compared to horses. “It would be good,” says the patient, “if one could find in each human the one animal that is his protective spirit.

I always loved horses.

One horse ate all the mice in my apartment.” (She does not know whether this is real or a dream.) But if one has the spirit, the love of such an animal in oneself, then one has to suffer greatly: In my case, my head was split, the spirit came into me and raged within me.

If one burns the bones of the dead and grinds them, dissolves them in water and fertilizes the earth with them, they will be dispersed in the air.

A Case of Schizophrenia  75 Question: “Why so?” Answer: “Through the vapor.

Then one can see the head of the animal in question on the tree.” At first the patient emphasizes the spiritual: The animal is called a protective spirit.

Love is a spirit that comes into the patient’s split head (seat of the soul).

Then the physical follows, “the bones of the dead.” When discussing Novozoon, the patient explained that it was a dead substance that comes “from the head and the spermatic products of animals.” In the chapter “Histology and its treatment,” we saw that the patient suffered from a lack of breast milk production due to masturbation.

She blamed the artificial milk that contains a lot of calcium for “calcifying the bones.” The latter “illness,” which is brought about by an impure cause, is also understood as impure and, therefore, “sexual dysentery” (since the patient only knows one kind of impurity, the sexual one).

The contaminated object, the bones assume the meaning of a sexual product that is at the same time dirty and fertilizing.

This has to be burnt (fire of love, because coldness leads to masturbation), it has to be dissolved in water (like the childlikeness that is dissolved in the spermatic water of Jesus), and it is used to “fertilize” = insemination of the earth. 114  As we know, to the patient, earth is a woman.

Earlier the patient described the process of the genesis of humans: She interpreted the amniotic water that flows out of the woman’s genitals as a form of “Italian lakes,” which come about “through a splitting of the earth” (body).

Here the new animal is born from the dead bones (dead substance = sperm).

It is brought into the world through the evaporization of its watery solution (from the woman’s body = earth, which it “fertilized”).

Earlier we saw that Jesus through “the pure air of his earth” (= the body), “saves” (= inseminates), while the “air” (psychical) becomes “spermatic water” (the physical).

Here we have the reverse process.

The water, which is “saturated” with elements of animals, emits vapors, and the elements of animals, so to speak, crystallize in the air into the new animal.

Corresponding to the transition from the physical to the psychical, one can see the “head” of the animal.

The fertilizing spiritual love came into the patient through the head.

For this purpose, her head needed to be “split,” as if something physical that expands in space had come into her.

I want to mention here that the patient calls this splitting of the head “phrenology.” As the science that studies head shapes, phrenology

76  A Case of Schizophrenia is the opposite of the “teachings of anatomy.” The latter, as we know, deals with the female sexual organs.

In the chapter “Psychological-Sistine experiments,” the patient talked about the “sectioning of the soul, anatomical examination, or phrenology.” There we learned to regard the first two terms as symbols for sexual aggression (on the part of Dr. J.) towards the patient.

After the above-mentioned, we may assume that “phrenology” has the same meaning.

Just as the patient becomes a little Forel, she also turned, as she once said, into a horse through the mythological (= sexual) treatment, “the study of science.”115 “I was put under anesthetic and woke up as a horse.” On another occasion, she was killed and woke up as a horse.

The patient says: The body of the horse is turned into a human body.

The animal is slain, and from this a human being is created.

This belongs to the mythology of acts of war.

Professor Forel has also experienced the stage of the horse?

Question: “What, in this instance, is death?” Answer: “Death is only salvation if it is physically dissolved.

I was put under anesthetic,116 and when I woke up, I felt as if I were an animal.

It is the science of Professor Häckel, the research of Darwin’s theory.” Here, too, death is connected with sexual fantasies.

The ideas on transformation (which are present in the mythology of each people) seek to find support in Darwin’s theory in a way that is quite logical to the unconscious.

One night, the patient sees horse heads (the spiritual!) approach her; they tell her that there is danger in Upper Zurich and that they are lost and need to be treated mythologically for the purpose of genesis, and that they would die if they were not used for science.

Upper Zurich is the area where the patient experienced her first love story. “The brown horse symbolized me; the white one, Fritz,” she says. “Fritz” was the name of the horse that, as was mentioned above, she compared to her brother.

It follows then that she and her brother, or rather her later lovers who take the place of her brother, have to be treated “mythologically” or, translated into our language, have to have sexual intercourse.

A Case of Schizophrenia  77 “When I was married, a big horse stood in front of the apartment and ate all the mice,” the patient explains. “It had a magnificent head, a long neck, and a heavy body.” “In the institution, I had a vision; it was a kind of horse race.

The horse had a big stomach.” Question: “When does one have a big stomach?” Answer (with a smile on her face):  “Perhaps the mare was pregnant.” Questions about her children follow.

As in the second hallucination, the horse with the big stomach symbolizes pregnancy.

Equally, the “heavy body” in the first hallucination probably has the same meaning.

The patient makes the horse eat mice.

So far, in the case of the patient, eating has always turned out to be a reference to the sexual act, and here, too, it probably means the insemination after which one becomes heavy; that is, pregnant. 117  Another dream: “In the mountains, horses were tumbling.

While falling, they managed, with a presence of mind, to bite the people who led them, thereby causing their death.” The patient explains: “History has something to do with war.

Wars are waged in areas that have many horses, so that the horses carry the riders.

It’s about the wars from the year 1870” (wars against Turks).

Turks are Asians.

We have the saying among us, “to deceive” [türken] in court.

Corn is also called “Turks.” It is a war because of a fruit.

Turks, like other animals, probably also have semen, spermatic fruits.

The semen goes into the ground from which the fruit grows.

The latter cannot fertilize, but heal someone, if the fruit is healthy.

A fall in the mountains is reminiscent of the abortus complex.

The fall of the horses elicits the idea of war; specifically, of the Turkish war (about which the patient dreamt during the anesthesia that was administered when she had the spontaneous abortion).

Undoubtedly, “Turks” represent sexual symbols, for the patient says that “Turks” mean corn and that wars are waged because of the “spermatic fruits.” She also mentions the fantasy of the fertilization of the earth.

The patient is impressed by the Turks because of their passion during coitus, which is the reason why the patient chooses them as symbols.

The patient once says she is not Turkey,

78  A Case of Schizophrenia not a Mormon-friendly country (cf. “Dr J. is a Mormon friend who would like to enter into a new marriage every year”).

The following sentence is peculiar: “Wars are waged so that the horse carries the rider.” I would point to Jung’s work, “Psychic Conflicts in a Child,”118 where little Anna, in a dream, realizes her wishes with regard to her father by way of riding on her uncle’s stomach.

In the patient’s thinking, the riders seem to have a similar relationship to the horses.

Let us listen to what follows: “The fallen horses are then looked for.

Connected to this is the Mariazell Valley.

Horses – they belong to mythology.

The horses that ate human heads are used for science.” The horses eat human heads, and this belongs to “mythology,” which, as we know, has to do with the genesis of humans.

This does not surprise us when we recall that the head represents the spiritual, which the patient needs, rather than the physical. “The new generation rises from the head and the spermatic development in the animal,” she said.

In this way we indeed have a sexual relationship between the “horses” and their “leaders.” “While falling, the horses managed, with a presence of mind, to bite the people who led them, thereby causing their death.” Question: “How do you interpret the eating of the head?” Answer: “These are experiments.

One fed the horses corpses that one had exhumed.” We learned about the “Psychological-Sistine experiments” of Dr. J. in the pertinent chapter.

The patient compares the eating of the head with the eating of corpses, since “Novozoon” (= sperm) that comes from the head is a “dead substance.” The patient continues: “Salvation from Catholicism can take place through mythology, because insanity has come about through mythology.

This has to do with industry.” “Industry” has to do with the working classes which are poor.

The Catholic man, who made the patient insane, preferred a rich girl over her. “Biting to death means to save from life or to examine the character of a person”; that is, transmittance of humanness to the animal.

There are happy horses that go to heaven where they become protective spirits.

When the animal bites a human, humanness is transmitted to the animal.

Biting to death is a judgment of God.

Only those with a clear conscience can endure it.

A Case of Schizophrenia  79 The symbolism of causing death by biting is not foreign to us.

In the chapter “Poetry of the tropics,” we saw that the patient throws herself in front of the animals, and we learned that being devoured was a sexual act expressed through death symbolism.

The patient calls the “biting to death” “God’s judgment.” This meaning of judgment (sexual overpowering), too, is familiar to us.

Obviously, during the sexual act with the “animal,” the “humanness,” that is, a product that contains the seeds of the human being or rather that from which a human being can develop, is transmitted to the animal. “The animals can symbolize humans and God, if God’s love goes into an animal and if God leaves the judgment up to the animals.” Here the patient herself explains quite correctly what a symbol is: It is an object into which one projects one’s own being (= love) and that acts in the same way in which one would act oneself (God “leaves” his action to the animals).

We have seen sufficient examples of how the horses, the child’s favorite things, become sexual symbols for her as an adult woman.

In this case, the white horse, Fritz, corresponds to her brother.

On the whole, the prototype of the brother plays a big role in the patient’s thinking.

We analyzed the dream where her brother makes peace with his “serpent.”119 The patient told me other, similar dreams where a beloved person is called “brother” of a “sexual person” (such as, for example, Professor Forel’s brother).

Her “boyfriend” was her brother’s friend.

The patient calls the brother who is married for the second time “doctor for the insane,” because one of his wives suffered from “uncontrolled laughter,” the other one of “irreligiousness.” By calling him “doctor for the insane” the identification of the brother with the later object of her love, Dr. J., is bridged, with all the consequences that the term “doctor” has for her.

The brother is said to have musical talent and high artistic standards.

At the same time, the patient depicts him as suffering from a “weak bladder,” “due to a cold or rough treatment.” Like the patient, he is also “infertile” and, therefore, needs to be “treated.” With a smile, the patient says that That Woman doubted the patient had a sibling relationship with her brother, and suspected a different “intercourse,” and thought they had learned it from their parents. “These impure assumptions had to become reality in order to be alive,”120 the patient explains. “My brother said I was not his real sister.” For the patient, the “assumptions” that could “become reality” place the patient’s brother in the role of the father, while she herself assumes the role of the mother.

80  A Case of Schizophrenia The mother plays a big role in the patient’s fate in that the latter constantly identifies with her mother and has the same experiences as her mother.

The patient also mentions that her mother’s nature and character had been transmitted to her.

Conclusion I cannot claim that I have produced a systematic and exhaustive analysis of this case.

An in-depth analysis is simply impossible with the means available to us these days, because this patient is highly dissociative and does not have the least interest in revealing her internal world.

Instead, she stops at the raw materials and leaves it up to the listener to make sense out of them.

For that reason, I have confined myself to presenting within certain categories this rich and multilayered material from my observation of the patient.

A reader experienced in psychoanalyis can gather his own impressions and draw his own conclusions from the patient’s words which I have recorded in as much detail as possible.

To one not trained in psychoanalysis, I have tried to present in a clear fashion the interpretations of symbols which are often self-explanatory, and to explain their meaningful context.

Freud and Jung have clearly shown us that the sick person’s system of delusions is not at all absurd, but follows the same laws as, for example, a dream which always reveals itself to be logical work on a complex.

Freud, Riklin, Rank, and Abraham have drawn attention to the similarity between current dream mechanisms and the mythological way of thinking.

The similarity has also been noticed by researchers unfamiliar with the new psychiatry.

Otto Mock, for example (in his book “Germanic Mythology”), attempts to explain the belief in transformations by way of corresponding dream images that the naive person perceived as reality.

Its correspondence to the mythological way of thinking points to a special relationship between the dream mechanism and archaic thinking.

This thought presented itself invariably during the examination of this patient.

While Freud and Jung, in particular, were the first to see parallels between neurotic and dream phenomena and the occurences of schizophrenia, I believe that with my phylogenetic approach, I can add an essential piece to the FreudJungian interpretation.

Humans have two pathways to experience, a conscious and an unconscious way: Unconscious experience possesses the important

A Case of Schizophrenia  81 function of creating emotional accentuation (cf. Jung, Diagnostische Assoziationsstudien [diagnostic association studies] 4.

Beitrag [Contribution], and Psychologie der Dementia Praecox, p. 42 ff.).

Only when the unconscious joins the conscious experience does the latter become true experience.

The unconscious adds all the appropriately correlated memory material.

For example, when we enjoy a certain fairy tale or poem, we do so because it evokes pleasure-oriented ideas in our unconscious; at that moment, we experience these only insofar as the new content of ideas is associated with old content and thus participates in a shared feeling of pleasure.

When this happens, it appears to us that the feeling of pleasure belongs to the content of new ideas and that we are happy in the present.

However, the joy is, in fact, a joy about past experiences, and not only our own personal experiences, since we have inherited and carry within us the condensed experiences of our primal ancestors. 121  To researchers who examine the similarity between dream, psychosis, and myth, my collection of materials will provide plenty of evidence.

Such a connection seems to me only conceivable if one assumes that long-gone ways of thinking survive and still continue to have an impact on those of the present.

I feel compelled to share a paradoxical thought that invariably became apparent to me during my work: Numerous times I was under the illusion that the sick had merely become victims of a deep-rooted folk superstition.

For example, let us look at our patient’s ideas on transformation, or let us listen to another patient who talks about her husband’s peculiar and hellish impact on her.

When I ask the latter for more details about how he accomplishes this, she states that he puts his “nature”122 into a little bottle and then he could do with her whatever he wants.

She specifies that “nature” is his “water.”123 Don’t we have numerous analogies of this folk superstition in the form of different “little magic bottles” through which one, for example, can force love, rejuvenate someone, or rather destroy someone, and so on?

Our patient, too, uses comprehensive water symbolism which is often closely related to mythological superstition.

Let us recall the “water of Jesus saturated with childlikeness” where, corresponding to the Christian belief, the bread becomes the body (child), and the wine, which is also present in water, becomes blood.

Alternatively, let us think of the “spermatic baths,” an idea that plays a role in Persian mythology.

Zarathustra’s semen is kept in a lake.

Every thousand years, a virgin is impregnated while taking a bath in the lake and, in this way, becomes the bearer of a savior.

(As related by Jung.)

82  A Case of Schizophrenia However, in all of this, the patient takes her own conflicts as a starting point and does not attempt to resolve them in reality through unconscious thoughts.

Let us take a specific example: To the patient, coitus with her husband is repulsive.

In this case, conscious reflection would consider different possibilities of the real world that could help her free herself from the distressing situation.

After weighing the pros and cons, she could perhaps decide to get a divorce or find another way out.

As Jung, in particular, has demonstrated, schizophrenia does virtually the opposite: As in the dream, it replaces the real external world with an inner world that has the value of reality.

My material shows that these depths of the self consist partially of “ideas” that seem to belong to a past that stretches beyond the individual.

Into this past, the patient takes her present experience.

She no longer says: “I was contaminated through the sexual act.” No, rather she dissolves her pain into the numerous analogous ideas of general Weltschmerz that we all harbor as an heirloom of our ancestors.

This is also the reason why she speaks the language of the mythological thought process: Not she, as an individual, but women in general, were contaminated, because with respect to the past, she is only one of many women.

The “earth” was contaminated, because our ancestors perceived the earth as an especially powerful woman; as it were, the summation of the term “woman,” the “Mater genetrix.” Patients with schizophrenia, in general, like to use vague and abstract terms, as we know, and this has a reason: An abstract term is an extract of many individual specific ideas that have been gained through long experience.

For that reason, it expresses much more than a precise and specific term.

When processing an object in a detailed and conscious way, abstract terms are often a burden to us because everyone perceives the object in his own way, so that it loses any preciseness.

However, it is exactly this lack of preciseness that makes these generalized terms so suitable for the dream life of schizophrenia.

The less precisely a concept is described, the less it signifies something distinct and specific, and the more mental ideas it can include.

Thus it appears to me that a symbol, in general, owes its origin to the propensity of the complex to expand and to dissolve into the general totality of the thought process.

The smallest expansion is the representation of one’s own complex in another person.

Instead of “I experience,” one says “she experiences.” In this way, the complex is deprived of the personal. 124  The vague general terms just mentioned are highly suitable for this dissolution.

The unconscious dissolves the present into the past.

However, we also know that the dream is the fulfillment of a wish and,

A Case of Schizophrenia  83 therefore, concerns itself with the future.

The future, too, is turned into the past, for the conflicts are represented through age-old symbols and also resolved through this symbolism, as if they had already occurred and been settled a long time ago.

Thus the unconscious also deprives the future of an independent meaning: The personal future becomes the general phylogenetic past, and the latter simultaneously represents the future to the individual.

In this way, we see in the unconscious something that is beyond time and is simultaneously present, past, and future.

Accordingly, the unconscious gives us information about present personal conflicts, about conflicts of the phylogenetic past from which personal experiences emanate and, finally, about the future development of things since the future develops from the past (is basically only a form of the past).

Finally, I would like to point out the enormous importance that “representation by means of the opposite” plays in the emergence of illusions, a concept that was discovered by Freud. 125  A particularly important example of this is the representation of sexual activity by death symbolism.

In my view, the reason for this phenomenon lies in the nature of sexual activity itself or, more precisely, in the two antagonistic components of sexuality.

In conclusion, allow me to express my most humble gratitude to Professor Dr. E.

Bleuler, for his generous permission to use his materials and the valuable interest that he has shown in my work, as well as to Dr. C.

G.

Jung, for the scholarly stimuli which I have received from him during my time as a student.

The patient’s unconscious might have chosen this as the opposite of the physical, as a symbol for the inseminating organ. 118 This Jahrbuch [2], 1910 [“Psychic conflicts in a child”].

A Case of Schizophrenia  89 119 Cf. chapter “Iron, fire, war.” 120 Cf. “The assumption could become reality, in order to prove that it rightfully exists”; and the explanation in that context. 121 Jung showed in his work “Die Bedeutung des Vaters für das Schicksal des Einzelnen” [The meaning of the father in the destiny of the individual], Jahrbuch 1, 1909, that children have the tendency to experience the fate of their parents.

As a parallel to this, I would like to mention the drama Die Ahnfrau [The ancestress] by [Franz] Grillparzer.

It describes the fate of a girl who is destined to experience the horrible fate of the ancestress, and experiences it in spite of her resistances and the rules her father laid out to her in order to prevent this. 122 Sperm. 123 Schizophrenic euphemism for sperm. 124 This tendency for dissolution (transformation) of each individual complex is the driving force of poetry, painting, and every form of art. 125 Cf.

Freud: Traumdeutung [Interpretation of dreams].

Furthermore Freud: “Über den Gegensinn der Urworte” [On the antithetical meaning of primal words].

This Jahrbuch [2], 1910, and [Eugen] Bleuler: “Zur Theorie des schizophrenen Negativismus” [The theory of schizophrenic negativism], Psychiatrisch-neurologische Wochenschrift [Psychiatric-neurological weekly magazine 9], 1910, where the ambivalence concept is explained.

Afterword Adrienne E.

Harris, PhD It is gratifying to see progressively more evidence that the process of finding and refinding Sabina Spielrein is now firmly located in theoretically sophisticated and subtle readings of her work.

Thankfully, we have moved away from an overwrought initial discovery, beginning in popular and often too gossipy books (Kerr, 1994; Carotenuto, 1982) and a decidedly lurid film.

For a number of scholars, this way of initiating interest in Spielrein via gossip and scandal has always been a source of distress.

At last, we have reached a point where we are deepening our understanding of her thoughts, her work and her contributions to the emerging theory and practice of psychoanalysis.

In this current 2018 moment of attention to sexual harassment and the targeting of women stepping into power or visibility, it is hard not to feel that lurid focus on personal and sexual life is too often the fate of an intelligent women moving into public life and visibility.

Even amidst the erasures of her presence and importance and a diminishing of her scholarship there has been a history of serious scholarship and appreciation for Spielrein’s writings and ideas (Cooper-White, 2014 Covington and Wharton, 2003; Launer, 2015; Lothane, 2003; Richebächer, 2012; Wharton and Ovchenko, 1999).

This work evolved even as English translations of her writings have lagged behind her scholarly essays available in German and Russian.

In this volume, we finally have a complete English translation of Spielrein’s doctoral thesis.

Her medical dissertation (both the clinical work and the thesis writing) was under the direction of Eugen Bleuler, the director of the psychiatric hospital in which Spielrein was first a patient and then a treating analyst and doctoral student.

One certainly wonders how rare such a trajectory was then or is now.

Launer notes, in his biography of Spielrein, that the road to accomplish this thesis

Afterword  91 was actually quite rocky.

Bleuler was supportive of Spielrein’s work as a medical student, but finally not of a psychoanalytic project.

Nonetheless, she proceeds in this careful and meticulous analysis, producing the first dissertation with psychoanalysis as its methodology and the promotion of psychoanalysis both as theory and practice.

In this work, Spielrein is actually most focused on making the case for a psychoanalytic interpretive method.

The details of the actual clinical practice are quite sparse.

In spite of my rather comprehensive knowledge about Spielrein’s career and writings, I was stunned and immediately deeply engaged by the writing and thinking of this dissertation.

Spielrein writes and presents the case and her theoretical constructions concerning the patient and psychotic states rather generally, with a mixture of ambition and authority, always mixed with a sense of compassion and attunement to the patient.

Surely, there would have been plenty of aspects in this patient’s material for Spielrein to identify with, even as the patient might be almost a decade older than Spielrein, already married and a mother, and a highly religious if also obsessed Christian.

Spielrein draws on this complex detailed clinical study because she has something particularly theoretical to argue.

She wants to demonstrate that sexuality is rooted in our genetic inheritance.

Sexuality, inevitably, takes on ancient and profoundly regressive forces with which individuals and groups and couples struggle and engage.

This is, perhaps, drive theory in its purest form and we can see her attunement to the inherent and inherited excess of sexuality itself.

In this way, one might see Spielrein, in this thesis, exploring ideas which over time have increasingly compelled psychoanalytic writers: Ferenczi, of course, but later Anzieu (2016), Botella and Botella (2005), Aulagnier (2003, 2015) and others.

It is interesting to think of the quite comparable work Ferenczi was to do just five to seven years later, in 1917, when he wrote Thalassa: A Theory of Genitality (Ferenczi, 1989).

In the introduction to that work, he tells us that it was written while he was an attending psychiatrist at a military hospital outside Budapest, treating shell-shocked veterans.

We might just note the complexity of Ferenczi’s crafting such a powerful theoretical appreciation of the force of life, reproduction and sexuality in a context where the death drive must have seemed everywhere.

In her study, Spielrein makes a profound case for the role psychoanalytic theorizing plays in the work on psychosis, a perspective with which

92  Afterword Freud only briefly engages in his writing about the Schreber case.

I think it is fair to say that in this period, as psychoanalysis is unfolding and emerging (the pre-World War I era), Freud, generally and increasingly, holds the view that his method and metapsychology were best directed to patients with neurotic systems, ego structures and defences, and not trauma.

Yet, importantly, in this dissertation, trauma is present and so is psychic structure, uniquely disfigured and transformed.

Throughout the dissertation, we experience Spielrein’s extraordinary sensitivity and attunement to language and to the intricate transformation in human speech, as experiences and affects and desires are simultaneously claimed and avoided.

She is attentive to the way that language can simultaneously deny and assert, that through the workings of the unconscious, experiences are named and erased, muddled and unmuddled.

Even in this early work, Spielrein is thinking of how affect and excitement infuse speech.

When she moves to Geneva, she begins to work with Claparède (1923) and Piaget (1923, 1926) on a new model of child development, There is a strong developmental press even here in this dissertation.

It is interesting to think that by 1914, she will be folding together the process of language development and a psychoanalytic model of the development and emergence of sexuality.

In her work on child language, she links speaking explicitly with orality, with pleasure, nursing, and also surely frustration and gaps in experience (Spielrein, 1914; 1920a, b, c, d; 1921; 1922a, b).

Here, in her dissertation, we see her explore in the clinical process with her patient, the way speech carries conflict, gaps in thinking, and the strange elisions of meaning and sound and sense that reveal the internal world of the speaker.

In a way not unfamiliar if we remember Freud on Schreber, Spielrein tracks the collapse of difference, the fusion and the erasures of experience and longings as they appear in the fractures and adhesions in the patient’s speech and communication.

In this way, she captures one of the essential insights of psychoanalysis: the power of desire and longing that will be both occluded and revealed by what is forbidden, degraded and subject to projections.

The patient is terrified by what and whom she desires.

This is true while also the patient is invaded by traumatic history which may certainly have included sexualized violence.

Given the appearance of the term ‘poetry’ in so much writing by and about Spielrein, the appearance of this word in the patient’s material and associations and in Spielrein’s comments, is notable.

Perhaps this term was

Afterword  93 commonly used as a marker for sexuality or love or passion of various kinds.

It also appears in Spielrein’s correspondence, first in a letter to her mother: “We stood still in the most tender poetry . . .

Poetry again . . .

So far we have remained at the level of poetry that is not dangerous” (see Launer, 2015, 84).

Furthermore, the term appears in her letter drafted to Freud on June 11, 1909, where she explains it; this suggests that it was not a common German idiom: “Four and a half years ago Dr Jung was my doctor, then he became my friend, and in the end my ‘poet’ i.e., my beloved.

Finally he came to me and everything went as it usually does with ‘poetry’” (Launer, 2015, 99).

In a rich and detailed series of chapters, Spielrein provides a close textual reading of the complexities, the agonies, the confusions of her patient’s experience.

Throughout the work, we see Spielrein think psychoanalytically and carefully about the powerful evacuations of desire and traumatic experience from the patient into other people, other circumstances, or mythological characters.

With her, we experience the deeply ‘logical’ inversions in which health becomes illness, desire a source of infection and dangerous retaliations, and body parts morph and transpose into people, creatures, forces of danger and madness.

However, Spielrein also gives us a clear indication of the real-life events, the childhood disturbances and parental violence and disorder that become the ground of the patient’s manias.

It is interesting to see how clearly Spielrein was able to think about the patient’s delusions and their meaning within her childhood and adult life.

The patient’s experiences in pregnancy, late pregnancy miscarriage, and the surgical sequelae are graphically encountered and understood both compassionately and keenly by Spielrein.

One must remember and consider the practices of managing pregnancy in the early twentieth century and wonder in general how these experiences, in fact or in anticipation, figured into women’s experiences of sexuality.

We are given a deep, unusual insight into a young woman analyst’s early work, but also into the particular conditions of women – poor in resources while educated – with regard to matters of sexuality and reproduction.

Spielrein is already deep in her studies of speech and language, undertaking what is essentially a semiotic approach to analytic work where meaning is so fluid and transformative and where signs are as important in their material sensuous conditions as in their symbolic movement.

It seems quite natural to feel reminded of Spielrein’s early interest in early cognition and her sensitivity in thinking of the relation of speech to orality, to mouths, and to sucking.

94  Afterword Spielrein is both resilient and careful in her assessment.

She presents an interpretive grid by which to understand the mad and tragic chaos into which her patient has fallen.

We do not get a good idea about the clinical work and interventions per se, but there is one interesting, moving moment where we see the analyst Spielrein’s sensitivity and tact with her patient.

The patient is talking about bedwetting and pain and medical treatments for bedwetting, sometimes personally, sometimes deflected onto someone else: a child, or a relative.

Spielrein makes a connection between bedwetting, fear and masturbation, but she hesitates to speak directly about it to the patient, fearful of suggestion or perhaps also the risk of frightening the patient.

She tells us she consults with Bleuler and he counsels tact, which she uses to good effect so that the patient is able to link these factors, bedwetting, pain, medical intervention, to her own experiences, including masturbation.

This is yet another example where we feel the weight of history in this work: the powerful fears and shames around masturbation, which psychoanalysis was seeking to interrupt.

In all my work on Spielrein, whenever I immerse myself in her ideas and clinical or intellectual projects, I see her writing very much in the roots of other later theorists.

There lies a danger in doing this since I can seem to want her to be the mother of everything.

It is part of the pain one feels at her erasure, in the field of psychoanalysis and, of course, in her life.

I link Spielrein to Ferenczi and to work on sexuality that stresses the deep unconscious, the primal regressive aspects of sexuality that seem beyond the personal.

At this moment, when many analysts are encountering and finding such importance in the work of Jean Laplanche, Spielrein is an interesting interlocutor.

Laplanche stresses sexuality in the encounter with the other, with what is taken in unconsciously by the child from the surrounding social figures.

Spielrein asks us to see the timeless, the potent force of internal and genetic history.

Don’t we need both, in just that way that the intersubjective perspective leaves and then refinds the intrapsychic to build a more comprehensive and complex theory of sexuality, both ‘early’ and ‘deep’ (Scarfone, 2012)?

Spielrein knows that she has produced an extraordinary document, a patient’s history and struggle to represent and manage her own madness.

Drawing on psychoanalytic concepts of dream interpretation, the movement of words into their opposites, the condensation of discrete experiences into psychotic and primitive presentations, Spielrein illuminates the terrible conflicts – over love and sexuality and loyalty – that have

Afterword  95 fractured her patient’s mind.

She makes the case for a methodology of interpretation that sees the links and parallels between mythology, dream mechanics and psychic structure.

This is the bedrock of a psychoanalytic methodology and metapsychology.

Even in this early work, Spielrein is thinking about sexuality and mind as always proceeding on conscious and unconscious levels.

This will lead her to interesting speculations over the presence and function of libido and destruction, a death drive that is more phylogenetic, and libido and selfpreservation which are aspects of ego functioning and conscious longing.

I want her to have the last word: Finally, I would like to point out the enormous importance that “representation by means of the opposite” plays in the emergence of illusions, a concept that was discovered by Freud.

A particularly important example of this is the representation of sexual activity by death symbolism.

In my view, the reason for this phenomenon lies in the nature of sexual activity itself or, more precisely, in the two antagonistic components of sexuality.

Chapter 2 Destruction as the Cause of Becoming (1912) Throughout my studies of sexual problems, one question in particular has aroused my interest: Why does this most powerful drive, the reproductive drive, in addition to the expected positive feelings, harbor negative feelings, such as anxiety and disgust?

It seems to me that the latter need to be overcome in order to arrive at positive activity.

Naturally, the individual’s negative attitude towards sexual activity has been particularly noticed in neurotics.

To my knowledge, several researchers have sought to explain this resistance as a result of our customary methods of child-rearing that strive to keep the reproductive drive within limits and teach every child to look upon the fulfillment of sexual wishes as something dirty and forbidden.

Others have noticed the frequency with which sexual wishes are bound up with ideas of death; however, death was conceptualized as a symbol of moral failure (Stekel). 1  Gross derives the feeling of disgust at sexual products from their spatial coexistence with lifeless excreta.

Freud traces the resistances and anxiety back to the repression of otherwise positive affectively toned wishes.

Bleuler sees in the defence the indispensable negative, which must also be present alongside the positive affectively toned idea.

In Jung’s work, I find the following passage: Passionate longing, i.e., the libido, has two sides: It is the power that beautifies everything and, in certain circumstances, destroys everything.

Frequently, one gives the appearance of being unable to comprehend fully what might be the destructive quality of this creative power.

A woman who, especially in today’s cultural environment, abandons herself to passion, experiences its destructive quality only too soon.

One simply has to think a little outside the current bourgeois norms of decency, in order to understand the feeling of immense uncertainty that overcomes someone who unconditionally surrenders

98  Destruction as the Cause of Becoming to fate.

To be fruitful – equals self-destruction; for with the rise of the next generation, the previous one has passed its high point.

In this way, our descendants become our most dangerous enemies whom we cannot overcome, since they will outlive us and take the power from our enfeebled hands.

The fear of one’s erotic destiny is completely comprehensible since there is something unpredictable in it; destiny, in general, hides unknown dangers, and the perpetual hesitation of neurotics to dare to live can be explained by their desire to be allowed to stand to the side in order not to have to participate in the dangerous battle of life.

Whoever renounces the chance to experience must stifle in himself the wish for it and commit a form of suicide.

This explains the death fantasies that frequently accompany the renunciation of the erotic wish. 2  I purposely quote Jung’s words in such detail because his comment that an unknown danger lies within erotic activity corresponds most closely to my own results.

Furthermore, it is quite important to me that a male individual, too, is aware of a danger that is not merely a social danger.

Jung certainly does not reconcile death images with sexual images but puts them in contrast to the latter.

Based on my findings in young women, I can say that, normally, it is a feeling of fear that moves to the forefront of repressed feelings when the possibility of fulfillment of the wish becomes an option for the first time.

It is a quite specific form of fear: You feel the enemy within yourself, it is the fervent love within you that relentlessly compels you to do what you do not want to do; you feel the end, the transient, from which you want to flee in vain to an unknown land.

One might want to ask: Is this all there is?

Is this the high point, and is there nothing more beyond?

What happens to the individual during sexual activity that would justify such a sentiment? 1.

Biological facts During the act of conception, a union of the female and the male cell takes place.

In this process, the unity of each cell is destroyed, and from the product of this destruction, new life originates.

Some primitive organisms, e.g., the mayfly, lose their own lives and die off after they produce a new generation.

For these organisms, the act of creation simultaneously means that they are destroying themselves.

This destruction, in and of itself, is

Destruction as the Cause of Becoming  99 most devastating for the living being.

However, if this self-destruction is placed in the service of the new creation, then the individual yearns for it.

In higher organisms, which do not only consist of one cell, obviously, not the whole individual is destroyed during the sexual act.

Yet, the sex cells, which disappear in their union, are not just indifferent elements to the organism; rather they are intimately connected with the entire life of the individual.

They contain, in a concentrated form, the entire procreator who continuously influences their development and, vice versa, whose development they continuously influence.

These most important substances are destroyed during fertilization.

Corresponding to the fusion of sex cells, during copulation the most intimate union of two individuals takes place, in which one pushes into the other.

The difference is merely a quantitative one: Not the entire individual is absorbed, but only a part of it that, at this moment, represents the organism in its entirety.

The male part merges with the female part, and the latter becomes agitated and assumes a new form due to the foreign invader.

This transformation affects the whole organism.

Destruction and reconstruction that always occur in normal circumstances as well, take place in an abrupt manner, and the organism rids itself of the sexual product as it does of any other excretion.

It would be unlikely if the individual did not surmise these destructive and regenerating processes, at least through corresponding feelings.

Just as the sensual delight of becoming is part of the reproductive drive, defensive feelings, such as fear and disgust, do not result from an incorrect association with excreta that are in spatial proximity; they are not a denial, which would mean renouncing sexual activity.

Rather, these feelings correspond to the destructive component of the sexual drive. 2.

Individual psychological observation The statement that psychically we experience nothing at all in the present sounds paradoxical, and yet, it is correct.

An event is for us affectively toned only to the extent that it is capable of stimulating previously experienced contents that are affectively toned and now lie hidden in the unconscious.

One can see this best in an example: A girl reads stories of witches with great joy.

It turns out that, as a child, she liked to pretend that she was a witch.

The analysis shows that the witch in the girl’s fantasies represents the mother with whom the girl identifies.

Therefore, the witch stories are pleasure-oriented to the girl only insofar as she perceives

100  Destruction as the Cause of Becoming her mother’s life, which she wishes to emulate, as pleasurable.

The witch stories are merely allegories that substitute for what she wishes and what has already occurred in her mother’s life story; the emotional accentuation is merely shifted onto these allegories.

Without the experience of the mother, the witch stories would not be pleasurable for the girl.

In this sense, “everything transient” is only an allegory of any primal experience unknown to us that seeks analogues in the present.

With this in mind, we experience nothing in the present, although we project an emotional accentuation onto a current image.

In my example, the present image of the witch was conscious, whereas the assimilation to the past took place in the unconscious (experience of witch = experience of mother), from which the present has differentiated.

Each content of a conscious thought or image is accompanied by the equivalent unconscious content that transforms the results of the conscious thinking into a language that is unique to the unconscious.

This parallel train of thought is best seen in states of fatigue described by Silberer.

Two of Silberer’s examples may make this clear.

Example no. 1: “I think of my plan to repair a rough spot.” Symbol: “I see myself planing a piece of wood.” Example no. 2: “I think of the human mind pushing forward into the difficult dark realm of the Mothers-problem.” (Faust, Part 2) Symbol: “I am standing on a lonely stone pier that extends far out into a dark sea.

The waters of the sea almost merge at the horizon with the equally darkly tinted mystical black air.” Meaning: The extension into the dark sea corresponds to the pushing forward into the dark problem.

The blending of the air and water and the mixing of above and below may symbolize that, in the Mothers’ realm (as Mephistopheles describes it), all times and places converge so that there are no boundaries between “above” and “below,” and Mephistopheles may say to Faust, who is ready for departure, “Sink then – I could also say soar.” These examples are quite instructive.

One sees how in the unconscious, the train of thought that has been adapted to the present is assimilated to the “events” that occurred over many generations.

The expression “rough spot” in the work (example 1), is adapted as an allegory from a different image content, the planing of wood.

In the conscious, the expression is, in

Destruction as the Cause of Becoming  101 essence, adapted to the present and therefore is differentiated with regard to its origin.

The unconscious, however, reassigns to the words the original meaning of planing the rough spot.

In this way, it transforms the current act of repair into an act of planing wood, something that has already been carried out rather often.

The second example is also interesting in that it regards – as peoples of old did – the sea as the Mother (the mother-like, creative water from which all life originates).

The sea (the “Mother”), into which one pushes forward, is the dark problem, the condition in which no time, no place, no opposites (above and below) exist, because it is still something undifferentiated that does not create anything new and, therefore, is something of eternal existence.

The image of the sea (Mother) is at the same time the image of the depths of the unconscious which exists simultaneously in the present, the past, and the future, hence outside time,3 and for which all places converge (to the origin) and opposites mean the same. 4  In this Primeval Mother (the unconscious), each image that has been differentiated from her wants to be dissolved, that is, it wants to be transformed back into the undifferentiated state When the patient5 whom I analyzed, for example, says, “The earth was penetrated,” instead of “I was inseminated,” the earth represents the Primeval Mother in the conscious or unconscious image of all peoples.

Into this Primeval Mother, the mother which differentiated from her = the patient, transforms herself.

Not without reason did Greek philosophers, such as Anaxagoras, trace the source of Weltschmerz to the differentiation of all that exists out of the primal elements.

This pain originates in the fact that every single part of our being longs to transform back into its origin from which, in turn, something new emerges.

Freud traces our subsequent direct or sublimated stirrings of love back to the time when, as infants, we felt our first pleasurable sensations through our caretakers.

We constantly seek to experience these pleasurable experiences anew, and when the conscious has long developed a normal sexual goal for itself, the unconscious still busies itself with pleasurable images of our earliest childhood.

Freud’s opponents, for the most part, vehemently refuse to accept the sexualization of innocent pleasurable sensations in children, whereas those who have conducted analyses do not doubt that, consciously or unconsciously, the erogenous zones in innocent children become the source of sexual pleasure in adults.

The reason why a person prefers one or another of the zones may depend on the individual’s personality.

In any case, we observe with particular clarity in neurotics

102  Destruction as the Cause of Becoming that the erogenous zones during childhood become, with corresponding unconscious symbolism, the source of sexual arousal towards the caretakers.

This allows us to maintain Freud’s thesis that the seed of sexual pleasure in the adult can be found in the infant’s sources of pleasure.

With respect to the debates about the role of sexuality, I was told that one could just as readily derive everything from the food drive, if one is willing to do so.
