At this point, I do not want to leave the views of a French author unmentioned, who derives all mental impulses from the drive for selfpreservation.

For he thinks that a mother loves her child because the child relieves the mammary glands when sucking; equally, that one loves a man or a woman because during coitus excretions that are bothersome for the organism are released and neutralized.

The pleasurable sensation is then transferred to the object that brings about relief.

These considerations do not challenge the correctness of Freud’s teachings: Freud does not even discuss what pleasurable sensation is and how it comes about.

His starting point is the stage at which pleasurable sensation already exists, and at that stage, we indeed see that pleasurable sensations in infants are precursors of later pleasurable sexual sensations.

It is the same as if one grew fond of the nurse’s comforting hand that satisfies our need to be nurtured.

The drive for nourishment and self-preservation are, without doubt, closely linked to the drive for the preservation of the species (hence also the sexual instinct).

It is an empirical fact that during sexual arousal, eating is sometimes substituted for coitus.

In that case, two factors are at work: On one hand, the pleasure of the act of eating; on the other hand, the frequently increased appetite resulting from the general excitement.

The opposite has also been observed: Of course, the need for nutrition cannot be entirely replaced with coitus, but we often see overwhelming sexual desire in undernourished individuals.

In our search for the causa movens of our conscious and unconscious self, I believe that Freud is correct in his assumption that the pursuit of pleasure and the suppression of displeasure are the basis of all psychic creations.

Pleasure originates in infancy.

However, the question arises whether our entire psychic life consists of this ego existence.

Do we not possess powerful drives that set our psychic content in motion, heedless of the welfare and misery of the ego?

Do the well-known basic drives, the drives for the preservation of the self and the species, represent the same to the whole of psychic life that they represent to the life of the ego, that is, the

Destruction as the Cause of Becoming  103 source of pleasure and displeasure?

I strongly believe that the ego-psyche, also the unconscious one, is governed by impulses that lie even deeper and are unconcerned with our emotional reactions to their demands.

Pleasure is merely the affirmative reaction of the ego to these demands pouring out from deep within.

In fact, we can feel real pleasure in displeasure or pleasure in pain that, in its own right, is not at all pleasurably accentuated, since, to the individual, pain means harm that the instinct for self-preservation in us tries to avert.

Hence, there is something deep within us that, as paradoxical as it may sound, wants to inflict harm on the self because, in fact, the ego reacts to it with pleasure.

The wish for self-harm and joy in pain are, however, entirely incomprehensible if we only consider the life of the ego which only desires pleasure.

Mach advocates the idea that the ego is something entirely unessential, continually changing, merely a momentary grouping of eternally existing sensations of elements.

As a philosopher, Mach is satisfied with this concept.

I closely associate Mach’s name with that of Jung because, according to the latter, the psyche is composed of many individual beings.

Jung talks about autonomous complexes.

In his view, we do not possess an undivided ego, but various complexes which compete with each other for predominance, We find the most splendid confirmation of his views in patients who suffer from dementia praecox and experience the power of individual complexes detached from the ego so strongly that they regard their own unconscious wishes (my patient calls her wishes “assumptions”) as hostile beings who really exist. “The assumption has the potential of becoming reality, in order to prove that it rightfully exists,” said the patient whom I analyzed.

I have arrived at the conclusion that the main characteristic of an individual is that it is “dividual.” The closer we get to our conscious thoughts, the more differentiated our images become.

The deeper we push forward into the unconscious, the more universal and typical the images become.

The depth of our psyche knows no “I,” but only its summation, the “We,”6 or put another way, the present ego is considered an object that is observed and subordinated to other similar objects.

A patient was trepanned and, during anesthesia, gradually lost ego-consciousness and, therefore, the sensation of pain.

Yet he still perceived impressions from the external world to such a degree that he exclaimed, “Come in,” at the chiseling of his skull.

This shows that he was aware of his skull, but as an object, apparently a room, separate from the ego.

Thus, the individual parts of the

104  Destruction as the Cause of Becoming personality are objectified.

In the next example, we see the objectification of the entire personality.

My patient7 reports that, during anesthesia, when she no longer felt the pain caused by the surgery, she saw instead of herself wounded soldiers for whom she felt pity.

We also find this in the soothing effect of the children’s rhyme, where the dog or the cat, etc., but not the child herself, experiences the pain.

Instead of seeing her own injured little finger, the child sees it as someone else’s.

Instead of “my finger” we see the more general image of any finger.

Often when we experience personal misfortune, we comfort ourselves with the thought that it happens to many or even everyone, as if our pain could be relieved by thinking of the regularity with which personal misfortune occurs and by the thought of eliminating the personal bad luck.

What in general happened and what happens, is no longer a personal misfortune, but an objective fact.

Pain is the result of the differentiation of the separated ego-image.

By this I mean an image that is connected with the ego-consciousness.

As we know, compassion originates from putting oneself in a state of suffering.

In patients with dementia praecox who transform ego-images into objective or collective images, we observe inadequate affect and indifference.

The latter disappears when we succeed in establishing an ego-relationship, for example, when the patient, instead of saying, “The earth was contaminated with urine,” says, “I became contaminated by the sexual act.”8 In my opinion, this is the essence of symbolic expression.

After all, the symbol means the same as the painful image, but it is less differentiated than an ego-image: One can probably imagine more meanings for the word “woman,” since they only need to reflect basic similarities, than for the much more narrowly defined ego-image of a particular Martha N.

One could respond to this in the following way: If the dreamer substitutes himself for another person, this other person is not less clearly differentiated than the dreamer himself.

However, this is only objectively correct: To each person, other people exist only insofar as he can access them with his own psyche.

In the full range of things, only that which corresponds to us exists.

When the dreamer substitutes himself for another person, representing the person in question as clearly as possible is of the least importance to him, since various persons are blended into one.

The dreamer’s only interest is to represent in the substituting person the quality that corresponds to the fulfillment of his own wish.

Should the dreamer, for example, want to be envied for his beautiful eyes, then he blends various people with beautiful

Destruction as the Cause of Becoming  105 eyes into a hybrid person so that, here too, the result is a type, rather than an individual.

As studies of dreams and patients with dementia praecox have shown, a type corresponds to archaic ways of thinking.

In patients with hysteria, which includes a “hypertrophy of the ego,” there also exists a corresponding increase in sensitivity.

However, it would be incorrect to claim that the psychic life of the hysteric is richer in content than that of the patient with dementia praecox, because we find the most meaningful thoughts among patients with dementia praecox.

However, due to the lack of ego-activity, we typically encounter in them archaic and analogous modes of thinking.

Freud believes that dementia praecox is about libido withdrawal or the reemergence of libido and about the struggle between withdrawal of libido and libido cathexis.

In my opinion, it is a battle between the two antagonistic tendencies of the collective psyche and the ego-psyche.

The collective psyche wants to turn the ego-image into an impersonal typical image.

However, the ego-psyche of the patients fends off this dissolution by anxiously displacing the emotional accentuation of the disappearing complex onto any lateral association to which the patients attach the “ego” (inadequate affect).

Nevertheless, the patients themselves notice that the emotional accentuation does not correspond to the image onto which it is displaced and that they “create” the previously existing affect.

This explains why they often simultaneously laugh at their own pathos and treat everything as a comedy.

During the onset of the illness, we often observe severe anxiety and states of depression because the patient perceives the tendency that the affectively toned portion of the ego is leveled out as an antagonistic act against the need to maintain an ego-relationship and to adapt to the present.

It is as if the previously awakened emotional accentuation had not yet subsided, while the objects have already lost their ego-relationship.

In the process, the prevailing sensation is that the world has changed and is mysteriously foreign and that it is like a theater play.

At the same time, the inevitable realization surfaces, “I am estranged from myself.” The thoughts become depersonalized and are “made” for the patients, because they come from depths outside the ego, from depths that have already transformed the “I” into a “We,” or rather into a “They.” Any feeling that still remains is expressed with pathos, because it no longer finds an object, just as an orator shows too much pathos when he presents the feeling itself instead of images that reflect it.

The anxiety will continue for as long as the remaining feeling, that is, the

106  Destruction as the Cause of Becoming need for an ego-relationship, allows the patient to notice the disassociation of the ego (foreign force).

When the illness progresses, the well-known indifference sets in: The patients no longer take anything personally; even when they say “I,” they are speaking of themselves as an object, which does not mean “I” and also does not obey the ego-wishes.

Thus, a woman who would like to have many children, might talk about her 22,000 boys with a smile, as if she did not really have that wish.

Yet occasionally, patients can also have genuine and adequate feelings, and I have observed this when the non-symbolic direct ego-relationship was restored.

In patients who are institutionalized, the disorder, evidently, has progressed to such a degree that the patient immediately slips back into his inadequate mode.

Only the future will tell whether analysis can improve that.

Thus, with the decrease of feelings of pleasure and displeasure, the psychic life does not die down to the same degree.

Certainly, the need for differentiation and fulfillment of personal wishes disappears, while, in contrast, the ego-differentiated images (hence dissolution) are assimilated to images that whole nations have formed and, therefore, transformed into archetypical collective images.

These molded images which lack affect teach us about the content that accompanies our drives.

The egopsyche can only desire pleasurable feelings, but the collective psyche teaches us what exactly we desire and what is affectively toned for us in a positive or negative way.

In this process, we observe that the collective wishes living within us do not at all correspond to ego-wishes, and that the collective psyche wants to assimilate the more recent ego-psyche, whereas the ego, indeed every little part of the ego, strives for selfpreservation in its present form (inertia).

However, the collective psyche, by denying the present ego, actually creates it anew, for the submerged ego-particle reemerges clothed in new images, richer than ever before.

We observe this best in artistic productions.

Of course, the inward regression of the ego consists of the desire to relive pleasure-filled experiences from infancy, but why are these experiences so filled with pleasure for us?

Why do we feel the “joy of recognizing the familiar?”9 Why does this stern censorship that attempts to modify our experiences persist long after we no longer feel the power of our parents over us?

Why do we not simply experience and constantly reproduce the same? 10  Hence, in addition to the wish to maintain our present condition, we possess a wish for transformation.

The latter means that a personal image content is dissolved in

Destruction as the Cause of Becoming  107 content from times past and thus, at the expense of the individual, shall become a typical and therefore collective wish projected by an individual to the outside as a work of art.

We seek that which resembles us (parents, ancestors), and in which a small part of our ego, a particle, can dissolve, because dissolution in the similar is not abruptly destructive, but happens unnoticed.

Yet, what does this dissolution mean to the ego-particle, if not death?

Of course, it reappears in a new and perhaps more beautiful form.

However, it is not the same ego-particle, but something different that has come about at the expense of this particle.

Similarly, a tree sprouting from a seed is the same as but not identical with every other tree with respect to the species.

In fact, it is a matter of preference whether we want to see emphasized in the new product, created at the expense of the old, its continued existence or the disappearance of its former life.

The same applies to pleasure and displeasure in the context of the dissolution of the whole ego-complex.

There are indeed examples of neurotics who explicitly state that they are afraid of sexual intercourse because a piece of the individual is lost during ejaculation.

Everything that moves us wants to be communicated, understood, and thus felt: Each image that we share with fellow human beings directly or as a work of art is a product of differentiation from primal experiences that make up our psyche.

As an example, let us take a previously differentiated experience; for instance, a sunny day in the spring, which has delighted endless generations before us.

To reproduce this experience, we must differentiate it by giving shape to the trees, the grass, and the sky according to the contents of the conscious mind at that moment.

We are no longer concerned with a generic spring day but with a specific spring day that has a personal character.

Vice versa: When this differentiated product enters into another individual’s psyche, a retransformation takes place; the other individual, while consciously processing the spring day, gives it his own personal character.

In addition to conscious processing, the image sinks into the unconscious which deprives it of its individual character, takes it down to the “Mothers,” and dissolves it.

In the unconscious, we might find the spring day broken down into its components: the sun, the heavens, and the plants.

The latter are reshaped or, more precisely, remolded into mythological structures, which we know from folk psychology.

Just by expressing a thought or describing an idea, we make a generalization since, as we know, words are symbols that

108  Destruction as the Cause of Becoming virtually serve to shape the personal into a universally anthropomorphic and comprehensible image.

In other words, it is deprived of its personal character.

Other people can never understand the purely personal.

Therefore, it is not surprising that Nietzsche, a man of powerful ego-consciousness, concludes that the purpose of language is to confuse oneself and others.

Nevertheless, we feel relief when we speak, when we shape a collective image at the expense of our ego-image.

The artist as well enjoys his “products of sublimation” when he creates the typical, instead of the individual.

Each image searches, so to speak, for non-identical yet similar material into which it can be dissolved and transformed.

This similar material is an understanding based on shared image contents through which the other person makes sense of our images.

This understanding evokes in us a sympathetic feeling.

It simply means that one wants to share even more about oneself, until one’s affection, especially when involved with individuals of the opposite sex, increases to the point that one wants to surrender totally (the whole ego).

This phase of the drive for self-reproduction (transformation), which is most dangerous to the ego, is accompanied by sensual delight because the dissolution takes place in the soulmate (= in love).

Since in the lover one loves the parents who are similar to oneself, it is comprehensible that, as a result, one also seeks to experience in reality the fate of the ancestors, specifically the parents11 (cf. Jung, The significance of the father in the destiny of the individual).

Coincidence plays a role in life only insofar as a sexual experience, already predestined in the psyche, is activated or continues to exist in the psyche as a possible experience.

In the first case, the complex is satisfied; in the other case, however, the tension-stimulating element is not removed and must continually free itself from each newly added analogous image content by letting it flow out.

Accordingly, for the psychic life, the activation of the experience only has a negative meaning that removes both the image content and the accompanying tension.

Let us, for example, assume that the desired union with the love object was achieved.

As soon as it becomes reality and the word becomes deed, the corresponding cluster of mental images fades producing a pleasurable feeling of released tension.

At this moment, one is psychically entirely unproductive.

Each image attains its peak of existence when it anticipates most intensively its transformation into reality.

The moment it becomes reality, it is destroyed.

However, this does not mean that with the fulfillment of a powerful complex, all psychic life stands still,

Destruction as the Cause of Becoming  109 for a complex is but a tiny portion that has been differentiated from primal experience.

This actual experience continues to create new products of differentiation that are sometimes psychically transformed into abreactions, other times into works of art.

It is quite important to emphasize that all products of sublimation, in essence, do not form a contrast to the wish for self-reproduction that has been adapted to reality.

They merely seem to represent more of a contrast because they are less adapted to the present and less differentiated.

In form, they have more the character of types like, for example, ideas of the “higher” love for nature or for Christ.

Jung points out that in the sun we revere our own libido, the Father dwelling within us. 12  Because these images are not destroyed through activation, they remain in the psyche as an anxious longing for a return to the origin, specifically, for the dissolution in the parents (which shall be proved below).

This explains why religion as the highest is so readily symbolized by the lowest, i.e., sexual activity, as in Pfister’s analysis of Count Zinzendorf, or in the case of my analysand Frau M.

By completely denying the love object outside the ego, one merely ends up becoming the object of one’s own libido and, therefore, destroying oneself.

Stekel, in his “Contributions” to the interpretation of dreams, states: In the same way as a dream does not know of denial in a general sense, it also does not know of denial of life.

In a dream, dying is the same as being alive.

Thus, the highest joie de vivre frequently expresses itself in a wish for death.

Incidentally, similar psychological aspects apply to suicide; the choice of the form of death, too, is influenced by specific erotic fantasies.

Poets have repeatedly expressed these ideas, and philosophers, too, have shed light on these connections between Eros and Thanatos.

Even murder in a dream is, as is often the case in real life, nothing but sexual murder, and often represents merely an intense sadistically tinged sexual act.

Up to this point, I can agree with Stekel.

However, he then continues: A typical dream of young girls is about standing naked in the street.

A large man throws himself on the girls and thrusts a knife into their stomach.

In this case, murder serves as an illustration of defloration by force.

Honor is irrevocably destroyed, while, in turn, the death of virginity denotes the life of the woman.

110  Destruction as the Cause of Becoming I see absolutely no indication that would permit us to conceive of death in these dreams as of a moral death, especially, since Stekel himself has perceived actual death merely as an intense sadistically tinged sexual act.

Because, during sexual intercourse, a woman is penetrated, the girl, but also the woman in the dream perceive themselves as victims of a sexual act with sadistic tendencies.

For this reason, the experience of war goes well with the outbreak of neurosis that, as we know, results from disturbances of the sexual life.

War is full of destructive images.

Since an image evokes other related images, images of war and destruction prompt images associated with the destructive component of the reproductive drive.

These images could cause even a normal person to perceive her existence as something transient and futile, and make it unbearable; even more for the neurotic, in whose mind images of destruction outweigh those of becoming, and who is waiting for suitable symbols to depict these fantasies of destruction.

Young individuals, especially girls, often have dreams with fantasies of lying in a coffin.

Freud teaches that lying in a coffin is a symbol of dwelling in the womb (coffin = womb).

Stekel rightly adds to this teaching that the word Grab [grave] means the same as the word “coffin”: whereby graben [to dig] has an unmistakable meaning similar to the words “to bore” and “to be borne” (to dig [graben] and to bury [begraben]).

In this way, the grave becomes Heaven.

This corresponds to the common idea among people that from the grave (through death) one enters into Heaven.

The patient Frau M. 13  presents a complex symbolism: She achieves new life by dying in Christ, as it corresponds to Christian belief.

Through numerous fantasies that relate to Christ, the patient proves that she perceives death as sexual union.

Consequently, she would need to identify with Christ (the beloved) and transform into Christ, as I explained earlier.

She actually becomes Christ by lying stretched out on the floor claiming that she was crucified and wanting to redeem all those who are sick; ultimately, she is, like Christ, the life-bestowing grave.

Prof. Forel = Dr. J., to whom she developed a “transference,” comes to her death chamber (her room) as Christ.

He is “buried alive” and is reborn as a grapevine.

This vine, representing new life, is, by analogy, a child.

At times, the patient says that she was changed into a little Forel.

She explains that she turns into a little

Destruction as the Cause of Becoming  111 Forel when she is treated roughly and beaten, thus by a destructive act.

At another time, her reproductive organism (organ) is a glass coffin or a broken porcelain bowl containing the bones of her stillborn child.

The small porcelain pieces, the child’s bones, and other fertilizing substances must be finely ground, cooked, etc., in order to produce a child.

The point is that death is necessary in order to create life and, corresponding to Christian belief, that through death the dead receive life.

In mythological imagery, “to bury” means “to impregnate.” The veracity of this statement becomes obvious to someone who studies mythology. “In order to create a new generation,” the patient says, “the whole body needs to be prepared; the new generation originates from the head (mind) and from the spermatic development in the animal.

Novozoon (= semen) is a dead substance.” The last sentence also shows the perception of semen as a dead excretion.

Irma, who was analyzed by Binswanger, finds coitus and the eating of carcasses disgusting.

Just as eating represents sexual intercourse in her thinking, the dead meat represents the semen that one receives during the act.

Irma also uses an elaborate coffin symbolism.

However, in contrast to a normal individual, she is continually horrified at these images.

For a normal young woman, the image of burial becomes a blissful image as soon as she imagines losing herself in the beloved.

For a young woman, Binswanger claimed, “the greatest bliss would be to linger in the body of the beloved.” At times, Irma imagines that “death is a handsome man,” but these moments last only for a short while because soon after, images of pure destruction prevail, accompanied by understandable anxiety.

Irma describes the feeling as “a feeling of wildness, of blowing off steam, of surrendering and becoming overpowered, a feeling of not knowing what she does and what will become of her.” One is poisoned (for this reason, the elongated shape of a serpent goes so well with the sexual animal), and becomes dangerously ill, as the symbolism used by Frau M. and other patients reveals.

Then, during pregnancy, the child developing like a malignant tumor at the mother’s expense destroys her.

To my female medical colleagues, material for the formation of corresponding symbols was readily available, and their unconscious was capable of using it.

For example, one of them dreamt that her little brother (a wish personality) had a dove-shaped tumor (dove is a symbol for innocence) in his stomach; then the dove came out of his mouth.

Another colleague, like Frau M., dreamt she had abscesses growing

112  Destruction as the Cause of Becoming on her neck, and yet another dreamt that she had cancerous growths on her fingers, or some university lecturer, to whom she had developed a “transference,” asked her in the dream about cancerous growths (exhibition dream); others develop scarlet fever in their dreams, etc. Each sexual symbol in a dream, as in mythology, signifies a god that brings life and death.

One example can stand for all: The horse, one of the well-known sexual animals, is the live-giving animal of the Sun-God, but also the animal of the dead, and even a symbol of death. 14  Mental images of destruction as part of various forms of self-gratification are particularly instructive.

Psychic autoeroticism can easily be identified in Nietzsche’s work.

Nietzsche, who remained lonely throughout his whole life, directed his entire libido towards his own person.

How did Nietzsche conceive of love, or more precisely, how did he experience love?

The poet was so tormented by loneliness that he created an ideal friend for himself, Zarathustra, with whom he identified.

As a result of his longing for an object of love, Nietzsche became man and woman within himself, and the two were combined in the person of Zarathustra.

For already she arrives, glowing, her love comes to earth.

All solar love is innocence and the desire to create!

Look there how she approaches impatiently over the sea.

Do you not feel the thirst and the hot breath of her love?

She wants to suck at the sea and drink its depth into her heights: and the sea’s desire rises towards her with a thousand breasts.

It wants to be kissed and sucked by the thirst of the sun.

It wants to become pleasure [here “Lust” = pleasure, in German original “Luft” = air] and height and a footpath of the light, and itself light.

Verily, like the sun I love life and all deep seas.

And this is what knowledge means to me: All that is deep shall rise up to my height!

Thus spoke Zarathustra.

To Nietzsche love means sucking the deep sea into himself, just as the sun does; the same applies to knowledge.

Accordingly, to Nietzsche, knowledge is nothing else but the craving for love and creation.

The glowing sun sucks on the sea like a lover, and the wildly moving sea lifts itself to the sun with a thousand breasts, thirsty for kisses, like a woman drunk with love.

The fantasy of sucking on the breast suggests that the sun behaves towards the sea like an infant.

Let me remind you that Silberer, too, in his second example of hypnagogic phenomena, represents the Land

Destruction as the Cause of Becoming  113 of the Mothers as a sea.

Just as the sun sucks up the sea, Zarathustra reaching knowledge sucks up the depths (the deep sea) into himself.

Therefore, to the poet, the longing for knowledge is essentially the longing for the Mother living in his depths.

If the Mother represents his own depths, union with the Mother must be understood as an autoerotic event, the union with one’s self.

In another passage, Nietzsche mocks the preachers of so-called “pure love,” of untainted knowledge free of desire, who deceive themselves by disguising the serpent as a god (cf. Jung: Godhead – one’s libido – serpent). “Verily, it is not as creators, procreators, and as those who become that you love the earth!” he shouts. “Where is innocence?

Where there is a will to procreate, and he who wants to create beyond himself has the purest will.

Where is beauty?

Where I must want with all my will; where I want to love and to perish so that an image may not remain a mere image.” (Cf. the earlier discussion: Through activation a psychic content, “image,” is destroyed, or it is activated through destruction.) “Loving and perishing, that has rhymed for eternities.

The will to love: that is, to be willing also to die.” Through a love union with the Mother, Nietzsche himself becomes a begetting, creating, and transforming Mother.

This state of being a Mother expresses itself even more clearly in the following speech: You creators, you higher men!

Whoever has to give birth is sick; but whoever has given birth is unclean.

Ask women; one does not give birth for pleasure: The pain makes hens and poets cackle.

You creators, there is much in you that is unclean!

That is, you had to be mothers.

As it seems, this has helped us understand many aspects about Nietzsche, and I believe that this approach can elucidate why we frequently, if not always, encounter the homosexual component in dementia praecox patients who live in autoerotic isolation. 15  Nietzsche becomes a woman in that he identifies with the Mother by sucking her into himself.

A contributing factor is that Nietzsche, as a result of his autoerotic isolation, does not live in the present, not even in his consciousness.

Rather he lives in his own depths that still belong to the time when the sexual life of a child is insufficiently differentiated and behaves in a passively feminine way by suckling on the mother’s breast.

If Nietzsche is a woman, his Mother behaves towards him like a man, as does the depth that later replaces the Mother, or his “abysmal thought” which we will discuss later

114  Destruction as the Cause of Becoming and with which he struggles as if he were fighting with himself.

To Nietzsche the Mother is he himself, and he himself is his own mother.

In each love, one must distinguish between two kinds of ideas: the first – how one loves, and the other – how one is loved.

In the first kind, one’s self is the subject and loves the externally projected object.

In the second kind, one is transformed into the beloved person and loves oneself as his object.

For the man, who plays the active role of conquering the woman, subject images predominate.

For the woman, however, whose role it is to attract the man, reductive images normally prevail.

The well-known female coquetry is related to this: The woman thinks about how she can appeal to “him.” Increased homosexuality and autoeroticism of women are related to this. 16  By transforming into her beloved, the woman, to a certain degree, has to feel like a man herself.

As an object of the man, she can love herself or another young woman who then represents her “wish personality.” That is exactly how the woman in love wants to see herself, obviously, always beautiful.

I once met a colleague who was greatly agitated about a number of letter envelopes that she had addressed.

None of them had the same beautiful handwriting that she had achieved on the first envelope.

The handwriting looked familiar to me.

When I asked her what the desired handwriting meant to her, she suddenly correctly remembered that her beloved wrote in this fashion.

Her desire to identify with her beloved was therefore so strong that she could only tolerate herself as him.

We observe the same phenomenon in “Tristan and Isolde.” Tristan: “Tristan, you – no longer Tristan, I – Isolde.” Isolde: “Isolde, you – no longer Isolde, I – Tristan.” The child, too, is autoerotic because she plays a passive role towards her parents; she must strive for the parents’ love and think of ways to please them.

The child must imagine how she is loved and place herself in the position of the parents.

In later years, the girl sees her mother as a rival, but also as her “wish-personality” whom she loves as such; likewise, the boy, his father.

When a child is irritated by the parents, the normal reaction would be an act of revenge.

Since the child may not risk this, he directs his anger towards some object or, if he does not think of anything better to do, against himself and, for example, pulls his hair.

At that moment, the child substitutes himself for the angering parents.

For instance, in Gogol’s “The Inspector General,” a governor, who is described as overwhelmingly conceited, shamelessly exploits his subjects.

However, in the end, he himself is tricked by a young impostor whom he assumed to be the long-awaited inspector

Destruction as the Cause of Becoming  115 general.

When, in a letter that everyone can read, the impostor pokes fun at everyone, including the governor, the governor turns the mockery against himself. “Look at the old fool,” etc., he shouts.

In this case, too, the failed aggression evokes a reductive sequence of images and a transformation into the scoffing subject that targets itself as if it were an object.

Corresponding to the destructive component of the sexual drive, the more activity-oriented man also possesses more sadistic wishes: He wants to destroy his beloved.

The woman who sees herself more as a love object, wants to be destroyed.

Of course, the boundary cannot be clearly drawn because every human being is bisexual, furthermore, because women also possess subject-images and men object-images.

For that reason, a woman can be sadistic and a man can also be masochistic.

If object-images become more intense by putting oneself in the position of the beloved, then the love that is directed to oneself leads to self-destruction, such as self-denial and martyrdom, and even the entire extinction of one’s own sexuality, as it is in the case of castration.

These are merely different forms and degrees of self-destruction.

The act of begetting is in itself an act of self-destruction.

Nietzsche’s words point to this: “Man is something that must be overcome,” Zarathustra teaches, “in order for the Übermensch to come about.” “And if you now lack ladders, then you must know how to climb on your own head: How else would you want to climb upward?” This sentence wants to say that you must understand how to overcome (destroy) yourself.

Otherwise, how could you create the higher, the child?

In the chapter “Involuntary Bliss,” Zarathustra complains: “I lay there chained to the love for my children; desire set that snare for me – the desire for love that I might become my children’s prey and lose myself to them.” Zarathustra’s child, the “abysmal thought” of the eternal reoccurrence of all things, threatens to die unborn in Zarathustra, but Zarathustra calls it to life.

You are stirring, stretching, wheezing?

Up!

Up!

You shall not wheeze but speak to me.

Zarathustra, the godless, calls you!

Zarathustra, the advocate of life, the advocate of suffering, the advocate of the circle!

Hail to me!

You are coming, I hear you.

My abyss speaks, I have turned my deepest depth inside out into the light.

Hail to me!

Come here!

Give me your hand! – Ha!

Don’t!

Ha-ha! – Disgust, disgust, disgust – woe to me!

116  Destruction as the Cause of Becoming Just as Zarathustra as a sun (the highest) sucks the deep sea into himself, he now turns his own depths inside out into the light (analogy, sun-love).

We know that Nietzsche himself is the light (the height) which sucks his Mother, the deep sea, into himself.

Through this union with the Mother, Nietzsche has become a childbearing mother.

In this instance, too, he turns his depths into his light and delivers them into the world, as if they were his child.

This is reminiscent of the children-fountains in mythology where the deceased are transformed back into children and reborn as such. 17  Wünsche,18 who gives us numerous examples for this, suggests that the image of taking newborns out of fountains and ponds is based on the idea that plant and animal life arises from the underworld.

This is correct, but if the unconscious uses plant symbolism to describe human birth, something quite analogous has to happen during the latter: Children come from ponds, because they are indeed in ponds (= amniotic fluid) while in the womb, from which they shall emerge into the external world.

Jung, in his paper “On Conflicts of the Child’s Mind,” shows how little Anna, who busies herself with the question of where babies come from, seeks the solution to the problem in the plant kingdom.

She is interested in how the eyes, mouth, and hair have grown, and, finally, how her little brother Freddy has grown from her Mama (Mama = earth), and she asks her father: “But how did Freddy get inside Mama?

Did someone plant him there?

Did someone put a little seed in there?” She also observes other processes in the world of plants, to which her unconscious turns her attention, because they are suitable symbols for the secret that preoccupies her.

At the age of three, Anna heard that children were little angels who live in heaven and are brought to earth by storks.

One day she asks her grandmother: Anna: “Grandma, why are your eyes so dim?” Grandma: “Because I am already very old.” Anna: “But you will become young again, right?” Grandma: “No, you know, I will increasingly grow older, and then I will die.” Anna: “And then you will be a little baby again.” It is quite interesting that the idea of her old grandmother becoming a little infant again seems entirely natural to little Anna.

Even before her grandmother speaks of death and little angels (which, as Anna heard, come to earth), she spontaneously asks her grandmother whether she would

Destruction as the Cause of Becoming  117 become young again.

Therefore, she is not surprised at the thought that her grandmother will become an angel.

Rather, she interprets the response in the sense of a transformation back to a former state of being.

There are certainly enough known examples where sick people who want to have children regard themselves as transformed back into children.

Good examples are the nun in the Temple of Amida19 in Riklin’s work, and Frau M. who, through a sexual act with Dr. Forel, becomes a little Forel.

Rank calls attention to dreams where birth symbolism is expressed in reverse.

Instead of pulling a child out of the water, one puts it into the water.

This symbol comes about by way of identification.

One evening, a medical colleague told me how much she would like to have a child.

The following night, she dreams that she had to crawl into a narrow passage that did not have an exit but ended in a building (like the birth canal ends in the uterus).

I let her explain to me how she crawled, and she remembers that she carried out exactly the same movements a baby in the first and second cephalic position makes during birth.

While doing that, she feared that she would not be able to go in further, since the passage was too narrow and became increasingly narrow, so that she would almost be crushed.

The patient Frau M.

(dementia praecox) sees herself exposed in the water together with her children, and their souls are then saved by Christ, that is, they return to the world again as children (since destruction leads to becoming).

Nietzsche, too, uses similar destruction symbolism in the birth of his thought that, in his case, is a substitute for a child.

Zarathustra fends off the act of creation with expressions of disgust, as if creation were something impure.

This is reminiscent of his words: “Whoever must give birth is sick; however, whoever has given birth is unclean.” Obviously, the thought that replaces the child needs to be shaped in such a way that it contains, in addition to the most desirable, the most dreadful element, so that it is capable of satisfying Zarathustra’s longing to lose himself in his children.

This is also the case: The thought expresses the highest, that the Übermensch will always return, and the lowest, that the most inferior man will always return.

Since Nietzsche continually preoccupies himself with the highest affirmation of life, his wishful thought simultaneously tells him that this affirmation cannot occur without negation, and that the highest contains the lowest.

This dreadful component is indeed capable of overwhelming Zarathustra: For seven days, he lies motionless, as if he were dead.

During that time, he battles a horrible animal which is his own depths and his own sexual personality.

He bites off its head, thereby killing his own sexuality, and

118  Destruction as the Cause of Becoming by murdering himself, his abysmal thought, and with it the resurrected Nietzsche, reaches its highest vitality.

The legend of the Russian Prince Oleg is interesting.

It had been prophesized to him that his beloved horse would kill him.

To avoid the fulfillment of this prophecy, he gives the horse to his servants who have to take particularly good care of it.

After a while, he finds out that his horse is dead.

Mournfully, he stands at its grave and rants about the deceitful fortune-teller.

While he is wailing, suddenly a serpent emerges from the horse’s skull and fatally bites the hero.

The horse is Oleg’s sexuality.

It dies and with it Oleg, because the serpent = sexual desire turns against him.

In this legend, destruction does not lead to creation as, for example, in the work of Nietzsche.

Rather, it suggests that the most beloved, the lifegiving sexual animal, can turn into the source of death.

It is remarkable how the passionate poets like to die in their works.

Let us look at Shakespeare’s Romeo and Juliet, for example.

The motif of the origin of the love between the descendants of two feuding sets of parents is a lesson in itself.

In a psychological sense, hate and love are the same since hate and passionate love lead to the same actions.

With regard to the conscious present and with regard to activation, hate is a negative love.

Since hate tries vehemently to fend off the destruction of image content through activation, images of love in the unconscious of a person who hates are unusually powerful.

Normal controlled libido is coupled with weak images of destruction, for example, teasing and hurting – which led to the proverb, “The ones who are in love with each other, tease each other.” However, the unbridled passion of a sadist is discharged in horrific outbreaks of violence that may escalate into sexual murder.

When causes that inhibit positively accentuated libido-images do not exist, moderate dislike may turn into moderate affection.

In that case, an outburst of burning passion follows and images are unleashed that were prevented from being activated due to hate.

This form of passion must be destructive, because it is too strong to be capable of maintaining any boundaries of self-preservation.

Shakespeare describes this: The heroes, who are fervently in love, are not satisfied with the activation of a small part of libido that would be necessary in a regular union of love.

Rather, they need more and more obstacles through which they may discharge their urge to destroy.

However, no obstacle is big enough to satisfy their passion which finds peace only in complete destruction, in the death of the personality.

Just as a strong fixation of libido on parents

Destruction as the Cause of Becoming  119 makes transference to the external world impossible, because no object fully corresponds to the parents,20 so, too, unsatisfied libido re-fixates itself on the parents.

This leads to incest fantasies that relate to reality or to more sublimated symptoms of fantasy, for example, the worship of nature or the symptom of religion.

At the same time, the unsatisfied urge to destroy, which is contained in the reproductive drive, gains tension and produces more concrete or more sublimated death fantasies.

However, the death image associated with the incest wish does not want to say, “I am dying, because I do not want to commit the sin,” but rather, “I am dead,” which means, “I have attained the regression to the parent with whom I become one.” The rather pronounced wish of destruction corresponds to the rather strong wish of becoming in the case of incestuous love, which is less differentiated.

Yet, dreams and myths in which we have children from parents or siblings are fantasies of becoming and sufficiently prove that the origin of death images does not lie in incestuous thoughts.

Freud showed that every dream image simultaneously signifies its opposite.

Furthermore, he demonstrates that linguistics is aware of an “antithetical sense of primal words.” Bleuler, with his concept of ambivalence, and Stekel, with his concept of bipolarity, say that in addition to the positive drives, we possess negative drives.

Jung believes that both drives are equally strong if we do not notice them.

However, when one drive or wish predominates even only slightly, we perceive it as our exclusive wish.

This theory yields itself quite well to explain why we overlook the death instinct within the sexual instinct.

Under normal circumstances, images of becoming ought to prevail slightly, and for that reason alone, becoming is the result of destruction and conditioned by destruction.

Of course, it is always easier to think of the result instead of constantly searching for the origin.

However, it does not take much to assign to children or emotive people a preponderance of destructive images.

In neurotics, the destructive component is predominant and expresses itself in every symptom of resistance to life and natural fate.

Summary Each content that appears in our consciousness is a product of differentiation from other, psychologically older contents.

This content is adapted to the present and becomes something specific and direct that lends it the character of the ego-relationship.

Accordingly, there is within us a

120  Destruction as the Cause of Becoming tendency to differentiate.

If we want to make others understand the specific content that only we ourselves can access, that is, make it accessible to them, then we de-differentiate.

We strip the content of the idiosyncratic and express it in a collective and symbolic form.

By doing this, we follow the second tendency within us, the tendency to assimilate and to dissolve, which is opposite to the first.

The effect of assimilation is that from a unity that refers to the “I,” a unity that refers to the “We” is formed.

The dissolution and assimilation of a personal experience in the form of a work of art, a dream, or pathological symbolism, transforms this experience into a collective experience and turns the “I” into a “We.”21 The appearance of pleasure and displeasure depends on whether an ego-relationship has been established or has diminished.

If the personal experience has already been transformed into a collective experience, then we react towards it like spectators who can only empathize if they can identify with the image.

Such spectators are patients with dementia praecox and we when we dream.

The tendency to differentiate, and the endurance of a tiny portion of the ego that has emerged or the entire ego-personality, correspond to the drive for self-preservation.

The drive for preservation of the species is a reproductive drive that also expresses itself psychically in the tendency to dissolve and assimilate (transformation of the “I” to a “We”), followed by the new form of differentiation from the “primal matter.” “Where love reigns, the ego, the dark despot, dies.” In love, the dissolution of the ego into the beloved is simultaneously the most powerful affirmation of the self, a new ego-existence in the person of the beloved.

Without love, the mental image that the psychic and physical individual is altered under the influence of a foreign power is, as in the sexual act, an image of destruction or death.

The drive for self-preservation is a simple drive that consists exclusively of a positive component.

Yet the drive for preservation of the species, which must dissolve the old in order to create the new, consists of a positive and a negative component and is, in its essence, ambivalent.

For that reason, the stimulation of the positive component, simultaneously, elicits the stimulation of the negative component, and vice versa.

The drive for self-preservation is a “static” drive in that it must defend the already existing individual against alien influences, whereas the drive for preservation of the species is a “dynamic” drive that strives for change, the “resurrection” of the individual in a new form.

No change can take place without the destruction of the former condition.

Destruction as the Cause of Becoming  121 3.

Life and death in mythology Experiences with dreams and dementia-praecox patients have taught us that our psyche harbors deep within itself images that no longer conform to our present conscious thought processes and that we cannot directly understand.

However, we find these images in the consciousness of our ancestors.

We can conclude this from mythological references and other products of the mind.

Accordingly, the mode of thinking of our unconscious corresponds to the conscious mode of thinking of our ancestors.

Instead of referring to it as inherited “modes of thinking which lead to the formation of corresponding images,” I refer to it, for reasons of brevity, as inherited “images.” The idea of life’s origin from the four elements (earth, water, fire, air) is already present in oriental symbolism.

For my purposes, I would like to examine life and death as it occurs in earth and water symbolism, primarily by using the historical material collected by Wünsche and Kohler.

We know from the Bible the two trees (of Knowledge and of Life) that grow in Paradise.

In older myths, however, there exists only a “Tree of Life.”22 The “Tree of Life” plays a twofold role: To the dead or severely ill person, the tree and its fruit give life, yet to the healthy and strong person, death.

If one wants to enjoy the forbidden fruit, that is, if one wants to give oneself to the sexual act, then one is doomed to encounter death from which, however, one will be resurrected to new life.

Adam and Eve, who fell into sin, shall be saved from death when Christ, the son of God, suffers death for them.

Christ takes upon himself the sins of humankind, he suffers in the way in which humankind should suffer, and he comes to new life, just like the deceased.

Therefore, Christ is a symbol of humankind.

The Tree of Life becomes the source of death for men and Christ alike.

Wünsche provided ample material that shows that the Tree of Life was used to make Christ’s cross.

Among other things, he mentions a riddle from the Middle Ages, which says: A noble grown tree in the garden that was laid out with great artistry.

Its roots reach all the way to the bottom of hell (in the Anglo-Saxon poem, hell is called the Hall of Worms and is filled with serpents and dragons).

The treetop touches God’s throne, and the broad tree branches embrace the entire world.

The tree with marvelous foliage stands in full glory.

122  Destruction as the Cause of Becoming This is a description of the Tree of Knowledge (the Tree of Life).

The form of the tree is depicted as a cross.

When Adam becomes seriously ill, he sends his son Seth to Paradise to bring back the oil of mercy.

Instead of the oil, the angel gives him three twigs, according to other legends, three apple seeds, and asks him to plant them under Adam’s tongue.

Adam is about to die, but from the twigs trees will grow, one of which will later save humankind, and that includes Adam.

(In some examples, only one twig is planted.

The triplication points to the tree’s relationship to creation.) When Adam learns about his pending death, he laughs for the first time in his life. 23  Since he is now dead, he no longer needs to die but is born again as a new being through fertilization.

The tree is planted into his mouth (Freudian displacement above).

The twig symbolizes the phallus, as Riklin24 showed in the context of fairy tales and as such, is the symbol of the highest power. 25  In the hands of Moses, it performs miracles.

The twig is transplanted in the garden of the ruler who is the father of Moses’ future wife.

Only he who can rule over the tree that has grown from the twig, may court his daughter.

It is, metaphorically, a test of sexual potency: Moses who received the tree from the girl’s father is, from now on, as her husband a substitute for the father.

According to Wünsche, the king’s scepter is also derived from the Tree of Life.

Therefore, the king’s power is essentially a symbol of sexual power.

According to most legends, the life-giving tree is used as a bridge over water.

Nietzsche comes to mind, as he conceives of man serving as a bridge for the Übermensch. “Man [Mensch] is something that must be overcome,” Nietzsche states.

In the same way, the old tree, the bridge that the new generation crosses, must be overcome.

Since the tree is a symbol of sexuality and of the lifegiving phallus, we overcome ourselves when we step over the tree.

After the tree has rendered its service for a while, God submerges it in water.

Water, as well, is a procreative primeval force, like Adam in whom the torn-off twig was planted.

From this restitution emerges the new birth.

The submerged tree had been forgotten by everyone, and only when the time of Christ’s crucifixion arrived, did one of his enemies remember the tree.

Well, he thought to himself, the tree trunk is suitable To be the best weight for Christ’s cross.

So waterlogged, almost like a stone

Destruction as the Cause of Becoming  123 It will be a burdensome load when carried.

It grew on the first man’s grave The tree that gave life to mankind And like death, we received the salvation From the Tree of Life.

Which role does Christ, the Son of God, play in this?

How does he save humankind?

Wünsche mentions various Germanic fairy tales in which an ill father or an ill mother is redeemed from death through holy water, that is, the fruit from Paradise.

I will discuss water later.

The fruit derives from the Tree of Life.

Wünsche views these fairy tales as springtime myths: The fruit of the Tree of Life, or rather the water of life, are symbols for life energy through which nature becomes rejuvenated each year.

According to Wünsche, the ill father and the ill mother represent nature suffering under winter’s force.

In northern legends, we find many springtime myths where the sun god saves the earth by fertilizing it with its rays.

In the “Song of the Nibelungs,” Siegfried and Brünhilde represent the sun and the earth. 26  Brünhilde (earth), in a state of hibernation, is saved by the victorious light of Siegfried (sun), when he pierces her suit of armor (ice crust) with his sword and in this way inseminates her.

Here the process is not called fertilization as in the earth-sun tales.

Instead, the act of fertilization is depicted in a more discernible way as a “cutting through” while a kiss highlights its erotic meaning.

It is significant that in Brünhilde, Siegfried fertilizes his mother.

Obviously, Siegfried’s mother is Sieglinde, but Brünhilde is her sister.

She loves what Sieglinde loves, that is, Siegfried.

Therefore, she identifies with Sieglinde’s role.

In this way, Sieglinde becomes her “wish personality,” or rather, her sexual personality.

By saving Siegfried, she saves her own wish, her child.

Dr. Graf’s work confirms the accuracy of the interpretation that Brünhilde represents Siegfried’s mother.

Like Eve, Brünhilde acts against her father’s commandment and, just as Eve is expelled from Paradise, she is banished from the kingdom of the gods.

Trespassing against the commandment (defending her wish personality for whose sins she is willing to take responsibility), Brünhilde falls into a deathlike sleep from which she is redeemed by the springtime sun Siegfried. 27  The longing for death is more often the longing for dying in the act of love, as is the case in Wagner’s work.

Brünhilde dies in the fire (love’s fire), united with her horse, and in dying proclaims:

124  Destruction as the Cause of Becoming Not wealth, not gold, Nor divine splendor; Not house, not yard, Nor masterful glamor, Not bleak treaties’ Treacherous bonds, Not insincere custom’s Stringent rules: Bliss in pleasure and pain – love alone grants.

Grane, my steed, I salute you Do you know, friend, Where I am taking you?

Glowing in the fire There lies your heart, Siegfried, my blessed hero, You whinny with joy To follow your friend?

Does the laughing flame Lure you to him?

Feel my breast How it burns; Bright fire Captures my heart To embrace him To be held tightly by him In powerful love To be wedded to him!

Hi ho, Grane!

Greet your friend Siegfried!

Siegfried!

In bliss I salute you!

In this case, death is a victorious song of love!

Brünhilde, so to speak, perishes in Siegfried: Siegfried is the fire, the redeeming glow of the sun.

In this primeval creator, Brünhilde is dissolved and becomes fire herself.

In Wagner’s work, death is frequently nothing other than the destructive

Destruction as the Cause of Becoming  125 component of the instinct of becoming.

We see this clearly in The Flying Dutchman, who can only be redeemed if he finds a woman who is able to be faithful to him.

This woman is Senta.

Her absolute loyalty to him can be found in her willingness to be destroyed through her love for the Dutchman, that is, to experience death with him.

She is the “savior prototype” that Freud described.

Freud points out that there is a typical fantasy of rescue from water.

With regard to the man, it means that he turns the woman whom he saves into the Mother; “when the woman saves someone (a child), she professes, like the king’s daughter in the Moses legend (Rank), to be his mother who gave birth to him.” We already saw how Nietzsche in his work becomes his mother by sucking the sea (Mother) into himself.

Also in dreams of birth, we have seen a similar process.

Senta, too, can become the Mother if she dissolves herself in the Mother (sea).

In the same way, the Dutchman can become procreator through restoration (death) in the procreator.

As if newly born, Senta and the Dutchman rise up from the water in an embrace. 28  What Wagner’s heroes have in common is that they all love as savior prototypes and sacrifice themselves in death for their love, like Siegfried and Brünhilde.

The similarity between Siegfried and the oriental Christ is striking.

Christ, too, is a savior prototype who sacrifices himself for humankind.

Siegfried is the sun god and his beloved is – Mother Earth.

Christ, too, is a sun god.

Christ dies on the Tree of Life; he is nailed to it and hangs there as though he were a fruit.

Like a fruit, Christ perishes and enters into Mother Earth as a seed.

This fertilization leads to the formation of new life, to the resurrection of the dead.

Through Christ’s death and resurrection, Adam’s guilt is atoned for.

Let us now turn to the question of what punishment Adam and Eve received.

They wanted to eat the forbidden fruit of Paradise, but were denied it, because they were only permitted to eat it after death.

Therefore, if God had condemned Adam and Eve to die, he would have given them permission to enjoy the forbidden pleasure.

The other punishment has the same meaning.

Adam is condemned to till the earth (Mother) by the sweat of his brow, and Eve to bear children in pain.

What is the punishment in essence?

It is damaging to the individual because the reproductive drive demands destruction from the individual.

Therefore, it is entirely natural that images of punishment tend to have a sexual overtone.

In order to avert God’s punishment, one makes a sacrifice to him; that is, instead of giving oneself to him, one offers him another being

126  Destruction as the Cause of Becoming for destruction, so that one can become oneself.

That which formerly was the most valuable, is replaced always by less important symbols that serve the unconscious in the same way, since symbols have the value of reality for the unconscious.

The most precious sacrifice was Christ himself who took the sins of humankind on himself and saved men through his death.

However, Christ does not need to die for humankind repeatedly.

It is sufficient to bring his actions back to life in our memory.

One identifies with Christ by consuming his body and his blood in the form of bread and wine, which is to say: I, who am now one with Christ, have made the necessary sacrifice of death, which will lead to my resurrection.

The manner in which one conceives this identification with the sacrifice (here Christ whose flesh and blood one has consumed) emerges from Eysen’s [Eysn’s] interesting reports29 which I would like to mention here.

On the votive tablets in St. Mary’s Church in Grossgmain, we find many depictions of misfortunes that provide information about the motives of those who offered sacrifices, of requests and wishes, and the beloved victim.

One of them reads: “A child had drowned in a bathtub.

When the mother found out about it, with a sad heart, she made a living sacrifice for the child, and the child lived again.” Or: “Once a sow bit off a child’s head and tore it apart, and with a living sacrifice the child became healthy again.” In these cases, the sacrificial animal represents perishing, and the person who had the accident, becoming.

The same is true in the next example: “A child, born of a dead mother, was baptized.

The father pledges right away to make a living sacrifice.” There again, instead of the child, a living sacrifice is made.

Christ, the child, who died for the father is “pars pro toto,” wherein the father essentially becomes at the moment of begetting, for it is always the father who dies in the child, and it is, in turn, the father who is reborn in the child.

Eventually, the living animal sacrifices are replaced with inanimate symbols.

In the same work, Eysen mentions vases that look like human heads.

The vases are filled with grain and serve as a remedy against headaches.

With these pots (called “lil’ heads”), one receives a blessing, for one takes them from the altar and places them on the heads of the suffering, just as one usually blesses by laying hands on someone’s head.

The significance of the “lil’ heads” becomes even clearer, when we learn of heads that were modeled after the image of saints who, like Christ, died out of love,

Destruction as the Cause of Becoming  127 therefore sacrificed themselves, like Christ.

One can find such sacrificial heads, modeled after the head of St. John, in the museum of Reichenhall (Eysen).

In principle, they are seed-filled fruit, an image that represents how one imagined Christ, as we have surmised earlier.

They should have a healing effect through fertilization, and they do: A finding of J.

Arnold gives an account of wooden heads that he interpreted as offerings against headaches and for the purpose of marriage.

The juxtaposition of two evils, a headache and being unmarried, show that one has to interpret headache, according to Freud, as “displacement above”; the same is true in the choice of the head-shaped container for the seed.

In other regions, clay heads were used as a remedy against infertility.

These heads contain three types of grain.

The number three is, as we know, a symbol for procreation.

Instead of using the shape of a head, other sacrificial symbols imitate the shape of the viscera.

In the visceral image, the sick organ is represented greatly enlarged: In that case, too, the destruction that God requires for life is diverted to a something else that is less valuable.

A children’s rhyme expresses this nicely: “May the cat feel pain, the dog, or the rabbit, etc., while the pain in X.X. subsides.” Then you spit three times to the side, out of fear of the evil eye.

The number three is a symbol for procreation, and the spitting is the equivalent of the sprinkling with holy water as a means to repel demons.

Related to the sacrifices are the reverential apologies and greetings.

By simultaneously falling on one’s knees or even throwing oneself before the ruler, one wants to say: “Look, my life is in your hands.

I am lying before you, already perished (image of death), now grant me life again (rebirth).” When Seth comes to Paradise to ask for mercy for his father Adam, he sprinkles his head with earth. “You are dust and shall return to dust,” God said to man.

By sprinkling his head with earth, Seth shows that he has already become dust (is back in the earth since there is already earth on his head).

However, new life emerges from the return to the origin (earth).

Kohler’s work,30 which I would like to consider now, provides an interesting symbol of man’s origin from earth.

Rabbinical literature mentions field-men and forest-men buried in the earth up to their navels and thus drawing their nourishment from the earth.

As Maimonides mentions in his commentary on the Mishnah, these man-like creatures also possess a humanlike voice.

In Arabic, they are called “little men” or “pigmies.” “According to Solomon Buber, this fabulous creature is a

128  Destruction as the Cause of Becoming plant in human shape whose humanlike head only appears after it has been pulled out of the ground.” Simeon of Sims31 states that the animal is identical with Jadua, shaped like a pumpkin, and connected to the ground by a long cord that had grown out of the root.

Nobody is permitted to cross the boundaries of the animal’s cord; otherwise, he is torn to pieces.

One would kill the animal by severing the cord.

In that case, it would scream loudly and die.

It is clear that this little plant-man is sticking in the ground like a child in the womb, connected to its place of origin by means of the umbilical cord.

Just as in algebra, a mathematical quantity does not structurally depend upon whether or not it is denoted by the symbol α or β, it does not matter to the unconscious whether it describes an essential concept, in this case the origin of the child, with symbols of plants or symbols of men.

Just as we emphasize by the term “TraubeHering” for the waves [in blood pressure] that both these researchers contributed equally to this discovery, so, too, the conscious acts with regard to the plant- and animal-men and other similar composite images (cf. Freud, Interpretation of Dreams).

The plant screams like a child at birth.

This scream is a death wail.

As long as the child is in the mother, it has no life of its own.

In mythology, this condition is often referred to as “suspended animation” or “shadowy existence,” for example, in the kingdom of Persephone, where one experiences merely a reflection or presentiment of life and everything only appears in shadow-like outlines.

In the “Mothers,” there exists no light or dark, no above or below, there are no opposites, because differentiation out of the primal matter, the Primeval Mother, has not yet occurred.

Only after differentiation into an independent organism is one doomed to live and experience death (reverse differentiation).

Life itself is the source of death, just as death is the source of life.

The development and origin of a child happens at the mother’s expense.

The mother is the one who is most at risk during birth.

She is harmed.

In order not to destroy her entirely, one has to offer a substitute for the death component, one has to make a sacrifice.

One pulls out the growth (births it) while pouring either blood of sacrificial animals or urine over it.

These are all products of death (urine-excreta).

In Jewish antiquity, there is a plant of fiery brilliance, called baarah.

Its root has the power to expel demons and spirits of the dead.

The uprooting of the plant causes instant death and is therefore carried out at night by a dog, while one pours urine and menstrual blood over the plant.

The

Destruction as the Cause of Becoming  129 equivalence of urine and menstrual blood shows that both products are, so to speak, sexual products32 that possess healing and fertilizing power.

The equivalent Persian herb is haoma, (according to Kohler, a plant- or tree-man worshipped as a god for his magical power, a kind of Tree of Life, the latter often represented instead as herbs of life).

At night, it is ground in a mortar while invoking Hades and Darkness, and blood of a slaughtered wolf is poured over it.

The herb haoma was used for killing demons.

Its potion bestows immortality and fertility.

Just as Jesus, the fruit of the Tree of Life, had to die in order to arrive at his resurrection and to confer life on those who identify with him, the divine herb haoma, which is likewise a tree-man, also has to be destroyed in order to become the fertilizing seed and fertilizing potion, like Jesus.

Due to its perilousness, Arabs considered cultivating this plant as dangerous. 33  According to Arabic belief, each year at harvest time, a worker must die.

They attribute the death-bringing property of the soil to the “earth-people.” For that reason, people usually pour the blood of a peace offering over the land.

On one hand, the earth plays the role of the mother who nourishes the little man through the umbilical cord; accordingly, extracting the child represents a birth.

On the other hand, the earth bears fruit (children), such as the tree that often is thought of as being masculine.

While earlier discussing the tree symbolism, I explained that the child and the genitals correspond to each other34 and that the act of giving birth can represent coitus.

I owe to Professor Freud the information that circumcision is a symbol of castration.

Certain Australian aborigines have the circumcision ceremony, while neighboring tribes conduct a ceremony in which two incisors are extracted.

These are sacrificial ceremonies: One emasculates oneself; that is, one symbolically kills one’s sexuality in order not to be destroyed in reality, because without destruction, becoming is impossible!

A woman once told me that she was dreaming about her parturition while anesthetized for a tooth extraction.

It does not surprise us that the extraction of a tooth appears in dreams as a symbol for parturition.

Parturition = tooth extraction = castration; that is, procreation is interpreted as castration.

Tausk informed me of a case where the patient interpreted coitus directly as castration: In the process, the penis is cut off in the vagina.

Selfgratification, in particular, is represented (in dreams) as extracting a tooth = castration.

Therefore one can draw the following equation:

130  Destruction as the Cause of Becoming Self-destruction can be substituted by sacrificial destruction.

In the Christian belief system, Christ suffers a sacrificial death in the place of humankind which, through religious contemplation, figuratively dies with him.

This figurative self-destruction, so to speak, achieves what Christ achieved by his self-destruction, that is, resurrection.

In the Christian belief system, self-destruction takes place in the image of a burial, a restitution to Mother Earth.

Resurrection is rebirth.

Plinius mentions the Greek custom “that someone who is believed dead is considered unclean until he has experienced a rebirth” (Kohler).

As Liebrecht showed, the act of rebirth occurs through an opening in a roof that resembles the mother’s womb.

In India, the instrument of rebirth is a golden cow: The person to be reborn is placed in the cow and pulled out from its birth canal.

In Jerusalem or Mecca, the one to be reborn may enter, but instead of leaving him for restitution in the mother, one sacrifices a sheep or a goat for him.

This proves that the sacrifice is perceived as an analogue to the restitution in the mother’s womb.

The sacrificial procedure, according to Kohler, is as follows: Before entering through the house door, the person stands with his legs spread apart so that the offering can lie between them.

Then one turns it on its left side.

The Moslem then turns its head to the south or towards Mecca; the Christian, however, to the east, or rather Jerusalem.

He slits its throat right before or on the threshold.

If the one to be reborn is Christian, one draws a cross on his forehead with blood.

Then he steps over the offering and the blood into the house and takes the garments that he shall wear to church where the priest blesses them.

The position of the offering between the spread legs corresponds to the position of the child at birth.

The bloodstained cross that one draws on the forehead of the one who is to be reborn, indicates a connection to Christ’s death.

Like Christ, the subject dies and is reborn.

This chapter, too, showed us that becoming has its origin in destruction: The procreator = the life-giving god, transforms into a child who is returned coitus procreation‹            ›= castration birth

Destruction as the Cause of Becoming  131 to the womb.

Death, in itself, is something horrible, yet death in the service of the sexual drive, that is, as its destructive component that leads to becoming, brings salvation.

However, eternal life does not bring salvation to men, as we can also see from the legend about the fountain of life.

I will describe a related passage from the Alexander Saga (according to Friedländer): Alexander’s cook arrived by chance at the fountain of their quest and wanted to rinse a salted fish in the water, when the fish suddenly came back to life and slipped away from him.

The cook himself takes a bath in the water and by doing so achieves immortality.

However, this immortality brings him no good, for the king, whom he tells about the miracle, becomes enraged because he had not known about this earlier.

He has the cook, who could not be killed, thrown into the water again.

The cook becomes a dangerous sea demon to whom (according to other legends) sacrifices were made.

The cook, who wanted to achieve his goal, is punished, and his punishment consists of being placed back into the water, that is, into the primal element (mother’s womb). 35  His vital energy, no longer subject to destruction, becomes dangerously destructive.

We find an analogy to the cook in the mandrake plant or in the small dangerous earth-men who have not yet been born.

By killing the dangerous plant, one turns it into something that brings salvation (to kill = birth).

Another analogy to the cook, restlessly raging in the water, is the Flying Dutchman.

Friedländer, too, notices this analogy.

According to Graf, this restless sailing around expresses his mental state: He yearns, in vain, for a suitable object.

The cook yearns for death, and the Flying Dutchman shows us that the death one desires is erotic death, that is, a death that leads to a new becoming, for Senta and the Dutchman emerge from the waves in an embrace.

According to an old tradition, when Adam left Paradise, he did not receive a staff (according to Wünsche, = Tree of Life), but rather a geomantic ring bearing a world cross (⊕ ♁), which he handed down to his descendants.

Through them, it came to Egypt and was regarded as the secret of all knowledge. 36  According to Wünsche, the ring takes the place of the Tree of Life.

Thus, the ring, like the tree, is a symbol of genesis.

Wünsche, in this context, points out a passage in Goethe’s “Reynard the Fox” (Song 10, 5, 7ff.), about the golden ring that had three engraved Hebrew words and that the fox pretends is a gift to the king: The pious Seth brought the three

132  Destruction as the Cause of Becoming engraved names down from Paradise, when he was looking for the oil of mercy.

As we know, Seth brings three apple seeds back from Paradise, or three twigs, from which later the Tree of Life develops.

As a result, the three engraved names are symbols of the ring’s life-giving power.

In this way, we can better understand the significance of the ring in the “Song of the Nibelungs” as a symbol for procreation and rebirth and the vital force that brought about the downfall.

The world can only be redeemed when life returns to its origin.

This is symbolically illustrated by the return of the ring (life) to the place of its origin from where it was stolen.

In this second section, I have limited myself to some rather diverse examples with the intention to show that the views developed in the first part of the paper can be applied to mythology.

It will require a more comprehensive in-depth study to prove the destructive component of sexuality also in more recent psychological or mythological forms.

However, I believe that my examples sufficiently demonstrate that, in accord with the biological evidence, the reproductive drive consists, also on a psychological level, of two antagonistic components, and therefore is simultaneously a drive of becoming and a destructive drive.
