Chapter 3 Contributions to Understanding a Child’s Mind (1913) The great teaching of psychoanalysis is in need of extensive and easily comprehensible evidence, particularly from child psychology.

Therefore, I would like to comment on three cases relating to two boys and one girl.

The analysis of the girl is a self-observation and, as such, rather comprehensive.

In the case of the first boy, out of consideration for his mother, I had to be satisfied with only a partial analysis that shows the connection between anxiety and sexual ideas.

In the case of the second boy, his young age made the analysis difficult: The little boy had no interest in giving us any information.

All three children come from “well-to-do families” with the corresponding “proper upbringing.” 1.

Analysis of a girl My parents were proud of the “purity” and “naivety” of their daughter; or rather my mother was.

My female colleagues as well did not want to “contaminate” me through sex education.

In secondary school, the topic of reproduction among animals was eliminated from the natural science curriculum in consideration of a proper upbringing.

In the end, I liked my “innocence,” and I was a bit worried that knowing such things would contaminate me.

As a result, it was only after I entered the university and attended zoological lectures that I learned anything about sexual matters.

During my clinical semesters, I developed a fear of diseases, a fear common among many beginning students.

However, I noticed that there was something idiosyncratic about my anxiety: I was only afraid of certain infectious diseases that I personified in my imagination.

For example, the plague, in my imagination, was a dark figure with glowing red eyes, etc. I knew that this kind of anxiety originated in my childhood.

136  Understanding a Child’s Mind Before I reached the age of six or seven years, I was not afraid of “any devil.” In comparison to my brother, I was always held up as an example of bravery, and I exploited this by taunting him and scaring him by jumping out of a dark hiding place, or by telling him horror stories.

My imagination was unbounded: I was the goddess and reigned over a powerful empire.

I possessed a power that I called “Partuns-power,” which I derived from the French verb “parter,” = to fly.

This non-existent verb is probably the result of blending “partir,” = to set out, and “porter,” = to carry, hence a power that carries me away.

By means of this power, I was capable of knowing and achieving anything I wanted.

Although I did not completely believe in the reality of my fantasies, it was too beautiful not to believe it to some extent: If “Abraham” could ascend into heaven, why should the same miracle not happen to me?

I had so much power within me, which no one suspected, and I most certainly was God’s Chosen One.

My parents did not know anything about this part of my inner life, although I was convinced that I did not conceal anything from them; I believed it was not that important, and I was afraid of being laughed at by the adults.

There was always a critic inside me who knew the difference between reality and fantasy.

In those days, I did not want to hear any fairy tales from others, for I could make up plenty of those myself.

What I wanted to know was the truth.

It did not escape my parents’ attention that I was scaring my brother, and once my father said to me: “Just wait, sooner or later, life will punish you.

One day you, too, will feel anxiety, and then you will understand how your brother felt.” I do not think I took his threat seriously, but nonetheless, it apparently had consequences, for one day I became greatly frightened when I saw in the next room two black kittens sitting on a chest of drawers.

It was probably an illusion, but nonetheless so clear, that I still have a distinct image of the little creatures in my mind.

They were quietly sitting next to each other. “This is Death,” or “the Plague,” I thought to myself.

Instantly, the period of my anxiety began: Whenever I found myself alone in the dark, I saw many frightening animals.

I felt as if an unknown force wanted to pull me away from my parents who had to hold both of my hands.

With great anxiety and great interest, I was eager to listen to descriptions of various diseases.

Then, at night, I discovered them on myself; in the shape of human figures, they wanted to “attack” or “get” me.

To the non-psychoanalyst everything would be clear: The child was

Understanding a Child’s Mind  137 frightened by her father, his threat had a suggestive effect, and she developed anxiety.

Yet, to the psychoanalyst, the questions only now begin, especially the question of why the anxiety was introduced specifically by a vision of kittens.

What were the “fantasies” that preoccupied the child?

Did they perhaps have a connection to sexuality that is noticeably absent in this description?

I have to respond to the last question unequivocally in the affirmative.

As far back as my memories reach, and I could confirm this with my parents, that is, as far back as my third or fourth year of life, I have been aware of questions that tormented me: Where do we humans come from?

(Children.) Where is the beginning of all beginnings and the end of all ends?

The thought of infinity was particularly unbearable.

I was also interested in the fact that not all humans were alike, and I was especially curious about Americans, because I imagined that, since the earth was round, they had to walk below us with their heads facing down and their legs upwards.

For a long time, I would tirelessly dig a hole into the ground and ask my mother each time whether it would take much longer, until I would have dug through the whole earth and could pull out an American by the legs.

Among adults, based on numerous individual experiences and based on ethnopsychology, we would readily interpret this game as an unconscious birth fantasy.

However, in the case of an “innocent child,” we do not dare do this.

For that reason and merely for the sake of completeness, I mention another favorite game: constructing balloons and other flying devices from paper.

By the age of five, I already knew that a mother carries a child in her womb to whom she then “gives birth.” In my imagination, the child would somehow be taken out, for example, by cutting her open, or, as someone told me, by unwinding the navel that closes the womb without causing pain and then taking the baby out.

Where babies came from was still terra incognita to me, and I asked about it.

I also asked who had created father and mother and who the mothers of mothers were and, finally, who had created God.

I was told that God has always existed.

Besides that, I knew that man was created from earth by God breathing life into him.

Mother had proven the latter to me by rubbing her hands against each other from which soil emerged.

I wonder how I reconciled the theories of birth and the earth.

Could it be that only the first person was formed from the earth?

If that is not the case, as far as I can remember, I believed that God created the child in

138  Understanding a Child’s Mind the mother merely by willpower and without any human action.

It was my burning desire to create a living human being, just like God.

For that reason, I became the omniscient and omnipotent goddess in my fantasies.

I tried to create humans from earth and clay, from all materials available to me, but I did not succeed in breathing life into them.

I kept myself busy drawing magnificent palaces and designing worlds of animals and plants for my empire.

One day our uncle, who was a chemist by profession, showed us an experiment that delighted me: He dipped a section of a little zinc rod into a lead salt solution, and it produced a multi-branched structure, like a real tree.

I suddenly realized that chemistry was the power that creates miracles.

Thus, I became an “alchemist.” I believe that already before this experiment, but possibly afterwards, I adopted a bad habit, much to the dismay of my parents: I would spill the leftovers of food and drinks onto the table, mix everything well, and make a big mess, because I wanted to see what it would turn into.

I was delighted when one color changed into another, or a new shape or consistency emerged.

Therefore, I cannot forget the mixture of joy and anxiety that I felt when a little piece of fabric transformed into paper through the mysterious interaction with a liquid.

Was it ultimately just my imagination?

I had many “secret” fluids in small flasks, “magic stones,” and the like, from which I expected the big “creation.” I constantly pestered my parents with questions about how all kinds of objects were “made,” and, although I could not “make” a human being, I eagerly made olives, soap and anything else that I could create.

Once I asked a little elderly lady whether I, too, could have a child like my mother: “No,” she said, “you are still too small to have a child; right now you could perhaps give birth to a kitten.” These playful words made an impression on me.

I was waiting for the kitten and pondered the question whether the kitten might turn out as intelligent as a human, if I raised it with the necessary care.

That is what I wanted to do.

Here we see the sexual etiology of anxiety: The kitten that triggered my anxiety was the child I was longing for.

Actually, I saw two kittens, so perhaps I was thinking (unconsciously, of course) of my brother, my dear playmate who, since he was younger, had to do everything I wanted him to do.

The immediate thought in this “vision” was: “This is Death” or “the Plague.” Therefore, I perceived the child as a dangerous and even fatal

Understanding a Child’s Mind  139 disease.

I frequently find among women this representation of pregnancy and birth as a form of disease (infectious disease, plague, especially bubonic plague), a malignant tumor or growth.

Therefore, in my opinion, this seems to be a common phenomenon.

Whether consciously or unconsciously, the woman perceives the new being as something that grows at the expense of the old.

It is interesting that we react to these ideas of destruction sometimes with pleasure, other times with anxiety, or at least with discomfort.

Based on what was mentioned above, the fear of infectious diseases was, in my case, the fear of a child, but not only that; it was also the fear of the abductor or seducer.

As far back as I can remember, as a child, I had no “idea” about the sexual significance of the father.

Like many other girls my age, I probably believed that father was there to earn money, and still I was looking for the abductor, for the man.

Once my brother and I climbed onto a chest of drawers, and with raised arms we prayed to heaven: “O, God, take us to you” (like “Abraham”).

Mother was shocked and took us down.

Apart from the fact that we could have fallen, she dreaded the thought that her children could be taken away from her (through death).

During anxiety attacks, an unknown force wanted to take me away from my parents.

Often, I believed I might fly away against my own will.

Animals and diseases that I pictured as living creatures, wanted to “afflict pain” on me and lead me into a dark and terrible death.

This early childhood fear of abduction or seduction was well depicted in Goethe’s poem “Erlkönig”: “I love you, I am charmed by your beautiful form, and if you are not willing – I will use force,” the seducer whispers to the young boy and, at the same time, reveals the poet’s fantasies about “pederasty.” Goethe must have experienced this “anxious wish” to substitute his father with a new lover, for otherwise, he could not have identified so well with the anxiety of the young boy.

My “Erlkönig” was God.

Let me add that, in my case, “God” represents a young uncle.

When he was in secondary school and about thirteen or fourteen years old, he loved to show off in our presence.

Sometimes he claimed to be God, took us into a dark room, and told us horror stories, while playing the violin.

I laughed about him, but my brother was afraid, despite my reassurances.

At the time, I was three to four years old. 1  Within two to three years, I forgot the “God stories” and the uncle in my consciousness.

However, the unconscious permanently dealt with him.

140  Understanding a Child’s Mind I can see this from the fact that I once dreamed of a giant god who was dressed as outrageously as my uncle back then.

Furthermore, I turned into a goddess and scared my brother, just as the uncle used to scare us (the unknown identification with the lover), and, finally, I had devised for myself in my imagination an “omniscient Moperl” (as a servant?).

In Russian, Moperl is also called “Mosjka.” Only during the analysis did I notice that we used to call the uncle “Mosjka.” This bothered him, and he gave us candy so that we would call him “Uncle Mossia.” The fantasy of being taken away by God (in the unconscious, by the uncle), always elicited a pleasurable feeling, because, evidently, already in my very early childhood, I, unconsciously, needed a substitute for parental love.

Only after my father’s threat did my lust turn into anxiety.

This case clearly demonstrates that scientific interest develops from sexual curiosity.

First and foremost, each individual is interested in his/ her own person, then in objects with which that person comes into contact.

The appearance of the new family member might have aroused in the child the questions of non-existence and the origins of people, and the questions of “from where” and “how.” How is one “made” – the little brother, father, mother, God?

Where is the beginning, and where is the end?

From the inaccessible, the questions shift to that which is accessible and which one can “make” oneself: How does one make all kinds of objects, like olives, soap, etc.?

The child is quite surprised that olives “grow,” and observes attentively the development of young plants from the seeds.

She also follows the development of young animals with great interest.

Particularly impressive to her is the transformation of the little zinc rod that is dipped into a lead salt solution and turns into a “real” tree, for in this way one can artificially create “life”!

Thus begins the passionate fascination with chemistry, though at first, as common among all societies, with the more primitive form of alchemy.

After all, there had to be a supernatural power that allowed the great miracle to happen before my eyes.

At the university, I was passionate about lectures on organic chemistry; it always seemed to me as if I had known all of this for a long time.

However, this was not the case if one only considers the conscious mind.

In my all-girl secondary school, the whole curriculum in chemistry consisted of barely two pages in a small book.

That was all I knew of this subject, because secondary school was more appropriately set up to stifle rather than foster any interest in the topic.

I rationalized

Understanding a Child’s Mind  141 my strange faulty memory to myself with the fact that the wisdom of our ancestors is passed on through our unconscious.

It does not matter whether this happens in the form of ideas, the shadow images which “have to be soaked in blood” in order to become alive in the conscious mind, or in the form of corresponding tensions of energy which make us search for analogue experiences.

The most important thing is that we can only experience and learn by analogue,2 if we also really experience it as such. 2.

Analysis of a boy In a social gathering, we were discussing how nervous anxiety can be removed when we discover its cause.

Of course, sexuality is not mentioned.

Soon afterwards, the highly intelligent thirteen-year-old Otto calls me aside and asks what the following means: When he was a child, he was always afraid someone would jump on him from behind with a knife or a revolver.

I do not want to address it and say half-jokingly that Otto probably had done something that was forbidden.

But he is relentless and tells me that, at the age of two (displacement), he frequently dreamt that an old woman would attack him from behind.

In dismay he would run to his mother who would protect him from the woman.

I ask him of whom this woman in his dreams reminds him.

He immediately recalls an old, ugly woman selling coal.

The female cook teased him that the woman selling coal wanted to kiss the boy.

On multiple occasions, he dreamt that the coal-selling woman was attacking him and that he wanted to scream but could not, and that he wanted to run, but his legs would not obey.

Even Otto himself does not know with certainty why he was afraid of the woman.

She could harm him, but most of all, she wants to pull him away from his mother to whom the boy runs for safety.

This is what came to Otto’s mind when thinking of the woman selling coal, without me doing anything: She is old, ugly, and he was teased that she wanted to kiss him.

These are all associations that belong to the erotic mindset.

In the unconscious of adults, we expect ideas that deal with how male and female bodies are built.

Up to this point, I have not explained to the boy the meaning of his anxiety, and I am quite surprised when, after the above-mentioned memory, he tells me, on his own initiative, about a dream in which an anatomical building plays a role.

It was evident that

142  Understanding a Child’s Mind in the case of the woman selling coal, his imagination also dealt with anatomy; otherwise, he would not remember this dream.

The dream is as follows: Otto is on Währinger Street in front of an anatomical building to which some (in reality three) steps lead up.

In front of the entrance is a grid.

He sees the horrifying woman and cannot leave because the grid is in his way.

To the psychoanalyst, the meaning of the dream is obvious.

An untrained person might want to visualize the strange linking of thoughts: “Someone” wants to attack me, stab me; it is the coal-selling woman; they say this old, ugly woman wants to kiss me.

The first dream: I flee from this ugly woman to my (beautiful) mother.

The second dream: I want to escape from this ugly woman into an anatomical building, but it is locked (to me).

If we examine these two dreams objectively, we see that the beginning is the same in both: I flee from this ugly woman.

In the first dream, the escape is successful, not so in the second.

The first dream is “more real.” The boy flees to the mother, something which, apparently, has happened before on several occasions.

In the second dream, the mother, as a place of refuge, is replaced with a building into which he, obviously, has never escaped.

In this instance, we are dealing with a mere fantasy, so we assume, based on what we have learned from dreams of adults, that the second dream is about the same topic (here, the escape to the mother), however, expressed in a symbolic way, like almost every intimate sexual desire.

As mentioned before, we expected the association with the “Körperbau” [body build] as a result of erotic fantasies in the conscious and unconscious.

Instead, Otto offers the anatomical building.

Consider, for example, that the word Frauenzimmer = woman [lit. “women’s room”] has been adopted in everyday language.

Among adults, we constantly find “building” as a symbol for woman.

In fact, Scherner, who was not even an analyst, used it.

This indicates that in dreams, we describe our own bodies in the shape of buildings.

A professor had surgery on his skull.

While his skull was chiseled open, he called, “Come in.” Apparently, the way in which he perceived his skull had changed, and he envisioned it as a room.

Also, in Otto’s case, the building is used as a symbol for the human body.

Especially the word “anatomy,” the science of the human body, shows us the expected notion of the body.

Instead of escaping to his mother, as in the first dream, in his second dream, Otto wants to escape into a body, but he is not permitted to do so.

The grid symbolically stops him from entering.

Of course, I do not explain anything to the boy.

The next day, he

Understanding a Child’s Mind  143 again tells me a dream that he, allegedly, had around the same time.

The content of the dream was as follows: Otto goes into a room (I attach the layout).

A governess sits there, her back facing the door.

He walks to the window and steals a piece of jewelry but, while doing so, loses one pearl.

A woman bursts in through the other door.

He wants to run away, but the door is closed.

The woman throws herself on him and, in Otto’s words, “knocked him for a loop.” At this point, he wakes up.

He was particularly scared by her wide-open mouth with big teeth. the woman door window governess door With these words, Otto walks to the piano. “A melody continually haunted me,” he says, and he plays the tune.

It is a chorus from Mozart’s The Magic Flute.

I ask him what he remembers from it.

It is a chorus dedicated to Iris and Osiris.

There are priests with long flutes, and one of the priests wanted to get married.

The meaning of the dream is, I did something that is forbidden.

Whether in reality or only in the imagination is yet to be determined and is of no importance to us: In the life of the psyche, there are only products of our psyche – Otto steals the forbidden jewelry, just as Adam and Eve stole the forbidden fruit.

The sexual interpretation of this theft is confirmed not only by analogous symbolism found among adults, but also by Otto’s subsequent associations: Otto receives the deserved punishment, and he is particularly horrified by her big mouth and her teeth.

It is evident that to him, the woman who gets him is the devil, because what follows are religious associations that are worth mentioning.

144  Understanding a Child’s Mind The part in The Magic Flute that Otto associates with the dream is as follows: Chorus of Priests (surrounding Sarastro in a half-circle): O Isis and Osiris!

What delight!

The dark night chases away the sun!

Soon the noble young man will feel new life; Soon he will be wholly dedicated to our Order.

His spirit is bold, his heart is pure, Soon he will be worthy of us.

Sarastro (gesturing to the right) Two priests (walk away to the front right and immediately return with Tamino who is covered by a veil)

22nd Scene [actually 20th] (The previous persons.

Tamino to the right of Sarastro.) Sarastro: Prince!

Your demeanor so far has been manly and calm.

Now you will still have to walk two dangerous paths.

If your heart still beats warmly for Pamina and you desire to rule as a wise prince one day, so may the gods accompany you.

Your hand!

(He gestures to the left.) One may bring Pamina!

This shows clearly that the dream evoked in him ideas of a young man like Tamino, who could resist many temptations in a calm and manly manner and now is worthy of a bride. “His god [quote has Geist = spirit] is brave, his god is pure, soon he will be worthy of us.” The dream shows the opposite of such self-confidence.

Therefore, we must assume that Otto behaved in a way opposite to Tamino: His heart is cowardly (because he constantly flees), for he does something that is dirty and, unlike Tamino, who is accepted by the gods, Otto is seized by the devil.

Of course, the devil seizes “sinners.” Otto is a sinner because he steals and, strikingly, he steals a feminine object, the jewelry.

This confirms our interpretation of “sin” in a sexual sense.

I do not want to pursue further the question as to why Otto equips the priests, and not Tamino, with flutes.

Neither do I want to speculate about what the flutes might represent.

When retelling the story, Otto mentions that the priests play trombones; then again, he says “horns.” This uncertainty is familiar in cases of strong emotional ideas. “There is a bridal

Understanding a Child’s Mind  145 couple,” he adds, “that is initiated by the gods.” However, he does not remember the name of the hero. “What would you call him?” – “Tomfool.

People who need gods are stupid; I do not believe in God,” he says.

On one occasion, he explains to me while being philosophical about “life:” “There is no God.

Man is a combination of a good man, the worm, and the bad man.” To divide humans into three parts is a remnant from the realm of religious ideas that he just rejected: The number three is the holy number.

As a result, the strange part of the human being, the worm, also has to be understood in the religious sense of the seducer.

As we know, The Hall of Worms or The Hall of Serpents means hell, as the serpent is nothing but a giant worm.

This classification comes from Otto’s unconscious, for when I showed interest in knowing how the word “worm” was to be understood, he was unable to give me an answer.

He first had to think for a while, and only in the evening, he explained to me that the worm basically meant life.

In his eyes, it had the same form as life, it moved, just as life does.

While saying this, the boy showed me this serpent-like form.

This is already the conscious redefinition of the fantasy that has emerged from the unconscious und clearly reveals to us that the worm, in accordance with the religious mindset, has something to do with enjoying the forbidden fruit of love.

Otto must have occupied himself quite a bit with this “worm,” since in his concept of man, he assigned to it a separate category and made it part of himself.

It is remarkable that he mentions the “worm” = “life,” as the middle part of man, as it corresponds to the anatomy of the body.

Otto is quite fond of his mother.

When he was a child, he was dyspeptic for a long time and she took care of him.

He now has two poems, in a little notebook, that are dedicated to his mother.

Whether he made them himself or, as his mother believes, copied them from somewhere, remains to be seen.

Nonetheless, it is interesting how he understands these poems.

One of them is called “Life”: “Life” Beginning is end End is song The beginning finishes The end wins.

Otto explains: It was a better state when we were not yet alive.

With the beginning of life through birth, this state of happiness ends.

I ask Otto where

146  Understanding a Child’s Mind one was when one was not yet alive. “In a human being,” he responds.

At another time, I ask him control questions and receive the same answer, and Otto adds that he thinks the happiest state was shortly before birth.

That corresponds to the sentiments of Romantic poets who want to enjoy the highest pleasure followed by death.

To Otto, birth is separation from his mother = death. 3  The end, nothingness, is the most beautiful state, according to Otto.

If the condition can no longer be as beautiful as it was when one still lived inside a human being, adds Otto later, then it is better not to live.

Compare this to the mother’s womb fantasy in Stekel’s “Language of the Dream,” and also in Otto Rank’s “Lohengrin Saga.” The latter researcher points to this “fantasy of the mother’s womb” in mythology and shows, especially in his work “Myth about the Birth of the Hero,” that humans perceived death as the return into the womb of the mother and as a rebirth.

Thus, Otto yearns for death, since he longs for the reunification (restoration) with his beloved mother, the time when he was not yet born.

The same wish was mentioned in his dream about the anatomical building.

Another poem that was discussed is “The Clock”: At the table, there is an old clock That the ancestress already possessed; It measured The end of man’s life Only one person understood the oracle of the clock.

It is the grandmother, it is she.

And I looked forward to that time When I would be told the age.

Suddenly one day I was ill; I looked forward to that correct oracle Then the old grandmother was no longer.

And I could not say to her What I so badly wanted to ask her.

Otto also told me about other women who took the place of the coalselling woman in his dreams.

They were always old women.

In this poem, he turns one of these women into his goddess of destiny, since she understands the oracle of the clock.

In Otto’s own words, the clock is the grandmother of two young coachmen, with whom he interacted although his mother had forbidden it.

He wanted to know from that grandmother

Understanding a Child’s Mind  147 how long he would live.

Freud says: Children and neurotics live outside time, because they live in their fantasy which is not connected to time.

Stekel writes in his article, “The Relationship of Neurotics to Time,”4 that neurotics are never able to cope with time, because they have not been able to cope with their childish wishes.

In this context, he mentions examples of children who constantly calculate numbers.

This turns out to be a calculation of the age difference between the child and the parents.

Consciously or unconsciously, every child wants to be as old as the respective parent is, in order to enjoy the same rights and pleasures.

Otto wanted to know the day of his death for which he constantly longed.

He fell ill and, apparently, during this whole time enjoyed his mother’s great love.

Death to him was nothing more than the culmination of this love.

However, he was not granted this culmination.

The anxious question as to when this blissful moment might come for him was not resolved, because the grandmother = clock grew silent. 3.

Analysis of a boy “Where does someone like you come from?” I jokingly asked the fourand-a-half-year-old Valli. “From Mama’s blood,” the little boy answered with a mischievous smile. “Where does she take the blood from?” – “From the finger; when you prick yourself, it comes out.” – “How do you know that?” – “From the fairy tale Snow White; she had cheeks as red as blood.” Evidently, the boy blends two fairy tales into one: that of Sleeping Beauty who pricks her finger and dies,5 and the one of Snow White with the bloodyred cheeks.

He reinterprets the death of Sleeping Beauty as a birth fantasy.

In order to avoid suggestive questions, I do not address the role of the father, but only ask where the father comes from. “Also from Mama’s blood,” the boy answers. “Mama first made me, then Papa.” Corresponding to Freud’s statements, the boy wants to be as old and as strong as his father; moreover, even older and stronger.

We know this from adults: As soon as a wish surfaces which ought not to be revealed, “resistances,” expressed by “distraction,” or by disregard for questions, and evasive answers, set in.

This also happens in Valli’s case. “But why do you call him father?” Valli gives a “nonsensical” answer: “Because he is an “agronomist” (profession of the father).

On another occasion, I raise the question about where the plants in the earth come from.

Any child from the country would say the answer

148  Understanding a Child’s Mind immediately.

Thus, it is all the more striking when Valli, whose father is an agronomist whom he frequently sees at work in the office and garden, claims he does not know.

When I push further, he says they come from the cherry (nota bene, which is red and round like a drop of blood). “So, what does one do with the cherry?” – “You have to plant it,” etc. (shows the location). – “So, what do you do, if you want to have flowers and grass here in the garden?” – “I don’t know.” – “I am sure you know; what is your father doing again?” – “You have to sow.” – “How do you sow?

What do you need for sowing?” – “I don’t know.” – Have you not seen your father do it many times?” (His father has many small jars with seeds.) – “I don’t know.” – “Of course, you know, you little rascal!” – “Seeds.” His mother was sitting nearby during this conversation.

This probably increased his “resistance” and caused him to feign ignorance: Earlier, Valli had told me, without any difficulty, where he came from.

When I asked him the same question in the presence of his mother, he smiled, hid his little head in my lap and said he did not know.

Now he probably guesses that the origin of plants and the origin of humans have something in common and that it is something that should not be discussed. 6  Valli’s father is away on a trip.

His mother is quite troubled by this. “Call me Father, then you won’t miss him so much,” the boy comforts her.

His mother thinks the little boy expressed himself awkwardly.

He wanted to say: “Call me by father’s name.” This is all the more likely, since we have already seen that the boy feels a rivalry to his father; he wants to be the older one for his mother.

Yet, during analysis, one still should be careful not to make arbitrary corrections to the words of the analyzed person.

Rather we need to follow up on the mistake (here a slip of the tongue).

We then see that it is justified in the unconscious images of the child where female lover and daughter are equivalent, as are male lover and son.

These connections are common in mythology and, therefore, also in the mind of a child.

After all, Eve was Adam’s daughter as well as his wife.

Egyptian myth is even more peculiar in that the Queen of Heaven, Nuth, is fertilized by her son Hathor (the sun) and then gives birth to him again.

By claiming that his mother had created her husband (as a son), Valli gives us reason to assume that he had the same idea.

On the same day, I asked the boy who had made his mother. “Father,” he said. – “Why?

Didn’t you say that your mother made your father?

So, did your mother make your father, or did your father make your mother?” –“Yes.”

Understanding a Child’s Mind  149 This apparent contradiction should not mislead us: A dream, just like primitive people, does not know an either-or, just like the child (Freud). 7  To the child, both theories are equally justified.

For that reason, both exist side by side for him, whether they are mutually exclusive or not.

As in the case of creation stories in folklore, we should not discount a child’s imagination as nonsense.

The conscious wish to have a little girl to himself is not foreign to Valli: For some time, he has wished for a dear little sister.

In the previous year, he asked his mother to buy him one.

Around that time, he once was invited for lunch.

There he “chose” one of his little girl cousins whom he caressed and kissed many times.

Once Valli closely nestled against his mother: “Mama, are my hands yours?” – “Yes.” – “And my little legs, too?” – “Yes, my sweetheart.” – “Am I altogether yours?” – “Of course.” – “And also Dad’s?” – “Certainly.” – “Am I Dad’s son?” – “Dad’s and my son.” – “No, I am not your son; if I were a girl, I would be yours.

I am Dad’s son, because I am a man and look like one, like Dad.” The mother “makes” daughters, the father – sons.

This, too, is a well-known mythological and childlike idea.

In this context, I also want to learn something about his ideas of death.

I ask Valli whether he had ever seen a dead person.

He had seen one during a funeral procession.

What happens to a person who dies?

After some resistances, Valli says, “blood.” A little later, when asked a control question, he says: “He is thrown into a pit.” – “What does he do there?” – “He swims in the water.” The end of life = beginning.

This, too, is an age-old mythological idea (cf. Rank, “Myth about the Birth of the Hero” and “Lohengrin Saga”8).

The next day, Valli tells us the following: “I dreamt of a tomfool.” – “What is that?” – “He pushes you into the water, into the pit.

He has a hoof.” – “Is he a human being?” – “No, he lives in the forest and is made from the breast.” – “From the breast?

Why so?” – “He comes out from the breast, when drinking tea, he comes out of the mouth; he also comes out of the teapot.” – “But you said he comes out of the breast?” – “Yes, out of the breast” – “And now you say out of the mouth?” – “Yes, out of the mouth, out of the teapot, he is made from skin.

He has little horns on his head and pushes people over.”9 – “Did you see one?” – “At Anna’s (maid); he puts on horns.

I was dreaming that there were horses in front of the house,” Valli continues. “Then the tomfool came.

He was thrown into prison by the governor because this tomfool pushes everyone into the pit.

He is a servant of the governor (guard).”

150  Understanding a Child’s Mind It is clear that Valli dreams of the devil who kills people (throws them into a pit – since this is how Valli pictures death).

At the same time, the devil is the child-to-be, which sometimes comes out of the breast, other times out of the mouth, at times out of the teapot.

The next day, Valli’s mother says to the feeble-minded farm hand that he should have children.

The latter answers that it is a sin to make sinful people. “Mama,” Valli interrupts her, “what is he supposed to do when a boy comes out of his mouth, and what should his blind wife10 do when a girl whom she will not even see comes out of her mouth?” Thus the dream from the previous day revealed to us further ideas the boy holds about birth.

Stekel points out that in a dream, soldiers (guards) often appear to represent death.

Valli, too, turns the “tomfool” into the “governor’s servant.” The house in which Valli lives is next to the governor’s house.

The servant, whom the boy sees regularly, is the guard.

This guard has a gun that shoots or stabs.

Here we see the analogy to the little horns of the “tomfool,” which leads to the blending of the two persons.

The “tomfool-guard,” who stabs one to death, creates new life, just as Snow White (Sleeping Beauty) created new life by the deadly prick into her finger.

His ideas of destruction do not create the slightest anxiety in the little boy.

Today he asks his mother how children come about, and she promises to explain it to him soon.

The mother who, upon my advice, observed the little boy whom she perceived as being entirely asexual, was surprised about the large amount of sexual ideas her only child harbored.

She admits that only now she understands the meaning of his monologues to which she had never before paid attention, because, like most mothers, she had regarded them as mindless fantasies.

Yesterday Valli told us that each woman had a husband.

Under no circumstances did he want to tell us for what.

Conclusion All three children are preoccupied with sexual questions.

Among the first two children, we find commonalities at the time when anxieties arise: the fear of being taken away or the flight to the parents (in the boy’s case, to the mother).

All children at first love their parents, but already in very early childhood, infants begin to become disloyal to their parents.

Valli is longing for a “little sister.” My mother tells me that my brother, already at the age of two, had a “love.” It was a little child whom he treated with the greatest tenderness, to whom he offered a chair, and once even gave

Understanding a Child’s Mind  151 his fruit compote, his favorite food.

I could list numerous other examples.

Each attentive mother knows this about her child, and one laughs about it, because the “innocent child” acts as if he knew the secret of the adults.

The reason for this is that the child’s unconscious knows it.

Real objects can be substituted by imaginary ones.

One wants to leave the house of the parents in order to become God,11 king, or queen.

One becomes enthusiastic about religion (or rather about God and angels), about the fairy-tale world where all the heroes are taken to foreign lands.

Then, a passion for travel adventures follows.

Baron Winterstein gave a talk at the Vienna Psychological Society about the connection between Wanderlust and sexuality: One leaves in order to find a new love or to get rid of the old one.

In this context, Freud talks about a custom in the primal family, according to which the grown sons are being expelled by the father and have to find an empire and a wife in a foreign land.

This custom is reflected in fairy tales; in all of them the princes travel (according to Freud).

The child who experiences the phylogenetic development of mankind and finds plenty of suitable food for his psyche in religion and fairy tales, pictures the new longed-for things (the new love) through forms of leaving, traveling, and flying away from the parents.

Correspondingly, we find even among very young children games with robbers, witches, and Robinsonades.

All these fantasies are, by all means, hedonistic.

However, why do so many children experience a time in which pleasure turns into anxiety?

Freud says: Each neurotic anxiety comes from a sexual desire that has been pushed into the unconscious.

In this process, pleasure turns into anxiety.

My own case can confirm this assumption: At the time when I became scared of the kitten, I no longer recognized its sexual meaning.

I forgot it, because I “displaced” the fantasy into the unconscious.

However, Freud himself says that not every desire that is repressed creates anxiety.

There has to be a causa movens.

In my case it was a threat.

However, my father did not tell me what would cause me to become afraid.

But why did I become afraid of sexuality in particular?

Of course, my case is not a unique one, and it is also a well-known fact that heightened pleasure, for example, in the context of a conquest, becomes pleasurable anticipation of sexual conquest.

Every intensified feeling is “sexualized,” each intensified love is “passion”; the latter expression is, not without good reason, taken from the sexual realm. 12  The difference between the sexual and asexual is merely a quantitative one.

By holding on to a rubric that has been created arbitrarily, one commits the error of denying children sexuality and of interpreting

152  Understanding a Child’s Mind their emotional life as entirely different from that of adults as we know it.

In sexuality itself lies something that can serve as a receptacle for such contradictory feelings like lust and anxiety.

New life comes about at the expense of the old.

A priori, it seems that we seek life and shun death, but close observation teaches us differently.

I want to draw attention to the fact that the same content can at one time impress us as a form of becoming, at another time as a form of destruction, just as my analyses have shown.

It is love that makes us overlook the dangers of self-destruction and lets us even seek it with delight.

My childlike love for God was still weak.

Therefore my father’s threat could activate the self-preservation instinct and the fear of perishing which, a moment earlier, I had seen as a form of becoming.

In the case of the boy, the more detailed etiology of his anxiety is unknown to me.

However, in his case the anxiety is also connected to ideas of destruction as part of sexuality and there, too, one can find a terror or a threat of a general nature which changed the fear of perishing into a fear of sexuality.

In the case of anxious people, namely among small children, these ideas of self-destruction also prevail with corresponding affects in the context of sexuality.

From these observations the educational methods seem self-explanatory.

Appendix: clock symbolism13 From the first years of life, even months of life, the tick-tock of the clock creates a great interest in people.

The clock lives by moving independently and making sounds.

Nothing is as amusing to a child as rewinding a clock and bringing it back to life.

Children especially enjoy toys that resemble living beings: Let me just mention how little girls long for dolls that can say “Mama” and “Papa” and can move limbs or open and close mouths, and the excitement (at times anxiety) of children about growling bears, miaowing cats, rolling mice, etc. Toys are constantly taken apart and then put back together.

Toys that a child cannot randomly modify will soon no longer be perceived as enjoyable, says Preyer, I believe, and we understand why: Toys should be suitable to represent as many of the child’s desires as possible, like a symbol in a dream which, in the same way, is all the more suitable the less clearly it is defined.

The child constantly wants to “create” something, even if it is only a small house out of building blocks, or a soldier cut out of paper.

Each mastering of nature, each new creation of one’s own, is something that even an adult desires.

Still, one never attains that

Understanding a Child’s Mind  153 which one desires, for the greatest miracle and the most precious creation would be that of a being, a human being who resembles us.

One patient who suffers from anxiety always believes she might suffer a heart attack, that her heart might get torn out, and that she might be buried alive.

Some of her ideas (sequences of associations which she produces upon my request quickly and without reflecting) are as follows: death, the reaper with the scythe, to cut off the thread of life.

One says: The clock of life has expired, the heart stops beating just as a clock stops striking.

When I suffered from pericarditis, there was a clock across from me.

It seemed to me as if sand or water were running down the clock.

Little grains of sand in the ocean.

Man is like a small grain of sand.

The clock is the patient herself, her life, her sick heart that currently preoccupies her.

She symbolically describes her pending death through the stopping of the clock.

The idea of becoming is impossible without that of destruction, and vice versa.

Accordingly, the patient’s death fantasy is, at the same time, a birth fantasy. “Man is like a grain of sand” (in the ocean), says the patient.

Vice versa, the little grains of sand that run down are “like” human beings.

Just as humans run out (of the mother), the little grains of sand run out of the clock.

The little grains of sand = humans, in the ocean = amniotic fluid.

As I just mentioned, the patient describes herself as a clock that is about to stop.

One should also mention that, in one instance, the patient perceived birth as a form of evisceration: The stomach is opened as if one were eviscerated when the child is taken out.

In this case, she pictures death and birth as the evisceration of a clock.
